Verlaine's Take: For EU Countries, Brexit Brings Opportunities and Headaches

Secret Recordings Play Role in SEC Probe of Insurer AmTrust

Bank of England 's Libor Role Under Scrutiny After Release of Recording

Wells Fargo Claws Back $75 Million More From John Stumpf and Former Retail Bank Head

Swift Releases New Security Mandates for Users

For EU Countries, Brexit Brings Opportunities and Headaches

BRUSSELS Brexit provides an opportunity for European Union countries to attract business as investment banks, insurers and asset managers seek to secure access to the continent. For the bloc's national regulators, the influx could bring added prestige as well as some headaches.

As the U.K. negotiates the details of its exit from the EU, both global financial firms with a London presence and big British firms are looking into how to keep a finger in the pie by opening offices across the channel. In the absence of a single EU-wide financial supervisory authority akin to the Securities and Exchange Commission in the U.S., the task of regulating these new entities will fall to national regulators and supervisors, supporting authorities such as the European Central Bank 's supervisory arm and the European Banking Authority .

Regulators will have to bolster operations and infrastructure to process new firms and licenses. They will also have to ensure financial firms don't play them off against each other as they seek the lightest-touch oversight possible, despite some harmonization of supervisory culture and processes in much of the eurozone.

National governments have been lobbying behind closed doors to attract key industry players, pointing to their niche expertise. Luxembourg and Ireland have experience in regulating investment funds, making them a natural destination for asset managers.

Lloyd's of London Ltd ., a specialist insurance market, said in late March that it would "https://www.wsj.com/articles/lloyds-of-london-to-set-up-eu-subsidiary-in-brussels-1490895996", opening the door for the city to become the continent's insurance hub. Lloyd's had also considered Germany, Ireland, Luxembourg and the Netherlands.

A Lloyd's spokeswoman said Brussels provided a strong regulatory framework in a central location. Belgium's national supervisor oversees Brussels-based Euroclear, which handles clearing and settlement for securities across Europe. Belgian authorities also have experience overseeing local operations of large banks such as BNP Paribas SA and insurance giant AXA, another point in their favor.

But EU officials are concerned about so-called regulatory arbitrage as financial institutions decide where to locate branches and main subsidiaries. Sabine Lautenschlger , who sits on the ECB 's six-member executive board and is second-in-command on its supervisory board, said a key question arising with Brexit is how banking groups might use the differences between national regimes and European regimes "to get the best deal."

Another question is whether national authorities will have the capacity to deal with the influx of new businesses relocating from the U.K.

In a "https://www.afme.eu/en/reports/publications/afme-implementing-brexit/" last week, the Association for Financial Markets in Europe , a trade group, outlined the challenges ahead for banks. One issue is whether supervisors in the EU can keep up with changes in banking business models and markets.

"Expertise in markets supervision is in relatively short supply," AFME said in the report. "There will be a major challenge for the Single Supervisory Mechanism [the ECB 's supervisory arm] and national authorities to ensure that sufficient resources and expertise are in the right place to provide timely delivery of license and model approvals."

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . said in March it was evaluating up to eight cities including Paris, Frankfurt, Luxembourg and Dublin where it could move operations and staff. Frankfurt or Paris are considered the destination of choice for investment banks given the German and French regulators' experience with wholesale banks, according to EU officials.

While Morgan Stanley favors Dublin, according to people familiar with the bank's plan, the Irish authorities have to take into account whether they will have the bandwidth to deal with a large global bank. Luxembourg's regulator, according to a person familiar with the matter, said it could turn down some firms seeking to open offices there so as to not constrain resources.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Secret Recordings Play Role in SEC Probe of Insurer AmTrust

By Mark Maremont, Leslie Scism and Michael Rapoport

The auditor at accounting firm BDO USA LLP tried to act casually while wandering around the firm's New York offices, striking up conversations with colleagues about the firm's audit of the large insurer AmTrust Financial Services Inc. AFSI -0.05%

Unknown to the colleagues, a tiny recording device disguised as an ordinary Starbucks gift card was capturing every word. The auditor was taping on behalf of the Federal Bureau of Investigation and cooperating as a whistleblower with the Securities and Exchange Commission .

The clandestine recordings in 2014, described to The Wall Street Journal by the whistleblower, were part of a continuing federal investigation being led by the SEC, according to people familiar with the matter. The focus of the probe includes accounting practices of AmTrust, a fast-growing, New York-based insurance company that in recent years has attracted skepticism about its results from investors betting against its stock.

The SEC's Fort Worth, Texas, office is leading the probe, and it isn't clear how far along the investigation is. The agency sometimes closes probes without taking action.

The auditor, who was directly assigned to AmTrust audits for at least three years but has since left the firm, has been working since 2013 with a larger group that includes Harry Markopolos, a forensic accountant who warned the SEC about the Bernard Madoff Ponzi scheme before it became public in late 2008.

The group hopes to profit by collecting a reward under the SEC's Whistleblower Program, if the agency ever successfully brings legal action in the matter.

An AmTrust spokeswoman declined to comment on whether the company knows of any SEC probe, saying the company didn't speak for the agency. It said questions about its accounting practices are "fantasies concocted and intentionally publicized by parties who clearly have a self-serving agenda and appear to be trying to profit from misinformation about AmTrust."

On Monday, AmTrust disclosed it has recently beefed up its financial-accounting team with the hiring of a chief accounting officer and other moves.

BDO declined to comment on AmTrust, saying it is "bound by professional standards which prohibit discussing confidential client matters."

The New York Department of Financial Services , one of AmTrust's lead state regulators, also is conducting a special, non-routine examination of AmTrust's New York unit, according to a person familiar with the matter. AmTrust said it doesn't comment on any state examinations.

Since its founding 19 years ago, AmTrust has grown, mostly through acquisitions, into the nation's third-largest workers' compensation insurer by market share. It protects millions of U.S. workers by bearing responsibility for their medical care and lost wages from job-related injuries. Its revenue in 2016 totaled $5.45 billion.

In early April, the company restated its financial results downward back to 2014 over a range of accounting issues. The restatements, which came after BDO was replaced as AmTrust's outside auditor by KPMG LLP , sliced more than 9% off AmTrust's previously reported net income for the past three years.

Even so, investors were encouraged when AmTrust finally filed its annual report, which had twice been delayed, and that the restatements weren't more significant. After slumping in recent weeks, the company's shares rebounded after the restatements were announced, though they later gave back much of those gains.

The Markopolos group claims that AmTrust has overstated its profits and financial health by understating what it may need to pay policyholders in the future. To mask this, they contend, the company has used complicated financial maneuvers, some involving related offshore companies.

In a 2013 submission to the SEC, which has been reviewed by the Journal, the group claimed it used internal documents gathered by the whistleblower to calculate that $277 million in losses had been shifted to an offshore affiliate from 2009 to 2012, bolstering AmTrust's operating income by that amount. This accounted for 38% of net income in 2012 alone, the group calculates.

In a presentation that the group says it gave to the FBI and federal prosecutors in 2014, Mr. Markopolos's team called one set of alleged accounting moves "The Washing Machine" for its purported cleansing effect on the bottom line, and another "The Loss Cemetery," for its alleged effectiveness in burying losses in offshore affiliated entities.

The recent accounting restatement, with KPMG 's approval, "does not change our fundamental view of AmTrust's shoddy accounting," says Mr. Markopolos, who says his group is not betting against AmTrust's stock.

AmTrust said it received clean audit opinions from BDO every year and has since received the same opinion from KPMG .

"KPMG 's audit included in its scope an audit of the allegations made by short sellers since 2013, including the baseless allegation that AmTrust shifted losses off of its books through reinsurance transactions."

KPMG declined to comment.

AmTrust has skirmished with critics before. In 2015, a New York federal judge dismissed a shareholder lawsuit against AmTrust based on some of its critics' allegations, ruling that the complaint failed to support its claims.

The judge cited correspondence between AmTrust and the SEC in which the insurer sought to work out "a difference of opinion" about some reinsurance accounting matters, and noted that AmTrust had changed its accounting approach in one area and "clarified and improved its disclosures." A federal appeals court upheld the dismissal in 2016.

The Markopolos group's submissions rely, in part, on emails, AmTrust bank statements and internal documents gathered by the BDO whistleblower. The whistleblower who had only a partial view into AmTrust's accounting practices claims to have witnessed seemingly unsupported adjustments to financial schedules by a senior AmTrust executive, and AmTrust's reliance on "plugs," or undocumented adjustments, in completing some financial schedules.

The Markopolos submissions also include detailed analyses of AmTrust's accounting produced by John McPherson , a forensic accountant and insurance consultant who is part of the group.

Many of the claims by the Markopolos group focus on alleged flaws in the audits of AmTrust by BDO, which ranks in the next tier down from the Big Four accounting firms. AmTrust had been among its largest U.S. clients until it was replaced by KPMG a year ago.

The whistleblower alleges that BDO often was rushed during its audits, partly because AmTrust was late or inconsistent in providing figures, or lacked documentation.

At least twice, BDO formally signed off on its AmTrust audit before completing some important checks, the internal whistleblower claims. The whistleblower claims that BDO staffers allegedly covered for their lapse by loading unfinished documents into an internal software system to show the right time stamp, then returned later to complete some of the work.

BDO said such allegations "are not consistent with our firm's values and the expectations we have for our professionals."

AmTrust said it relied on BDO's integrity, and "if there are allegations regarding BDO's internal procedures, those are the responsibility of BDO and AmTrust would not have visibility into them."

 Dave Michaels contributed to this article

Bank of England 's Libor Role Under Scrutiny After Release of Recording

By Jason Douglas

LONDON A recording of an 8-year-old phone call threatens to reopen a question that has dogged the Bank of England for years: How much did the central bank know about efforts to manipulate a key interest rate at the height of the financial crisis?

The U.K.'s main opposition Labour Party on Monday called for a fresh investigation into the BOE's possible role in the rigging of the London interbank offered rate, or Libor, in 2008, after the British Broadcasting Corp . published a recording online of what it said was a private telephone call between bankers at Barclays PLC in which they discussed lowering their firm's Libor submissions in response to "serious pressure" from U.K. government and central-bank officials.

A spokeswoman for Barclays declined to comment, adding that the bank doesn't know whether the recording is genuine or not. The Wall Street Journal wasn't able to confirm its authenticity.

The emergence of the October 2008 call represents the latest twist in a long-running saga that has resulted in billions of dollars of fines for big global banks in the U.K. and the U.S., landed some bankers in jail and hurt the reputation of regulators who oversaw the financial system.

At its heart is Libor, an interest rate at the time calculated from banks' own estimates of their cost of borrowing that was used as a benchmark for pricing everything from mortgages to corporate loans. Barclays paid $450 million in fines in 2012 to U.S. and U.K. regulators after admitting traders and managers had manipulated the Libor rate.

In the 2008 recording published online by the BBC's Panorama program, two bankers at Barclays are heard to discuss reporting borrowing rates to the Libor-setting panel that are lower than the bank's true cost of borrowing. One says that "we have some serious pressure from the U.K. government and the Bank of England about pushing our Libors lower."

The rate became a closely watched gauge of financial-market stress as the crisis that ultimately sank the world economy into recession unfolded. The lower the Libor rate, the healthier individual banks and the broader system were perceived to be.

The BOE denies any wrongdoing. In a statement Monday, the central bank said that Libor and similar benchmarks weren't officially regulated in the U.K. or elsewhere in 2008 and that it continues to assist fraud investigators who are still probing the scandal. It said it plans to make public material related to the manipulation of financial market benchmarks once these investigations are concluded.

Nevertheless, John McDonnell , economy spokesman for the Labour Party, said the call obtained by the BBC represents "an extremely serious revelation" that requires the U.K. Treasury to sanction "an immediate high-level investigation."

The U.K. Treasury said in a statement that the government "is absolutely clear that we must learn from the lessons of the past," citing reforms in 2012 that have made manipulating Libor a criminal offense.

"We will continue work with the U.K.'s independent regulators to ensure that the our financial sector operates to the highest possible standards," said a spokesman.

The details of the call published by the BBC echo closely evidence heard in an earlier probe into the Libor scandal by the British parliament's treasury committee, which scrutinizes economic policy.

In a 2012 investigation, lawmakers on the panel heard evidence that Barclays 's Libor submissions had been discussed by former Barclays chief executive Bob Diamond and former BOE deputy governor Paul Tucker in October 2008. Both Mr. Diamond and Mr. Tucker denied the BOE instructed Barclays to lower its Libor submissions.

But the panel heard that junior executives at Barclays  including Mark Dearlove, one of the bankers on the 2008 call obtained by the BBC got the impression that it had, which resulted in Barclays lowering its submissions to the Libor-setting panel, the committee said in a report.

Lawmakers said the episode represented "an extraordinary, but conceivably plausible, series of misunderstandings and miscommunications." They said that Barclays had demonstrated a "pattern of repeated dishonesty in Libor submissions" earlier in 2008.

They cleared the BOE of any wrongdoing, saying that although officials had been "naive" they weren't responsible for policing Libor. The now-defunct Financial Services Authority, which supervised banks at the time, came in for sharper criticism.

Subsequent reforms have put the BOE back in charge of supervising banks and the financial system more broadly. The U.K.'s Financial Conduct Authority now has oversight of Libor and other benchmarks.

Wells Fargo Claws Back $75 Million More From John Stumpf and Former Retail Bank Head

By Emily Glazer

A new report on the sales scandal at Wells Fargo & Co . portrayed its former chief executive as a tone-deaf leader who protected an irresponsible lieutenant and worked for board members who didn't keep the pair in check.

The report, a long-awaited investigation of a scandal that ensnared the bank with regulators and politicians this past September, also said Wells Fargo 's board will claw back an additional $75 million in pay from former Chief Executive John Stumpf and the lieutenant, former retail-bank chief Carrie Tolstedt .

The latest decisions on pay brought the total money clawed back from current and former Wells Fargo executives to $182.8 million, an amount that rivals the fine the bank paid last year and that its board described as the largest clawback ever for any financial-services firm.

The 113-page report, released Monday, is likely to be the board's final deep dive into the questionable conduct that dated back to 2002 and ultimately resulted in a $185 million penalty last year. It likely won't be the final discussion, however, of the high-pressure sales strategy that led to as many as 2.1 million accounts being created using fictitious or unauthorized customer information.

"The Board's report is a necessary examination of what went wrong in our culture, operations, and governance," Wells Fargo CEO Timothy Sloan said in a statement Monday.

"I wish we would've taken more action and done things more quickly," Mr. Sloan said later on a call with reporters.

The San Francisco-based bank continues to face federal and state investigations about its sales practices. And last week, proxy advisory firm Institutional Shareholder Services Inc . suggested Wells Fargo investors vote against 12 of the bank's 15 directors at the firm's annual meeting April 25.

In Monday's report, prepared by a board committee of independent directors and law firm Shearman & Sterling LLP , much of the blame fell on Mr. Stumpf and his protg, Ms. Tolstedt.

A lawyer for Ms. Tolstedt, Enu Mainigi of Williams & Connolly LLP , said: "We strongly disagree with the report and its attempt to lay blame with Ms. Tolstedt. A full and fair examination of the facts will produce a different conclusion." Mr. Stumpf's lawyer declined to comment. Mr. Stumpf last fall apologized and in congressional hearings took responsibility for the scandal.

The report also highlighted how the bank's push to boost revenue and profit trickled down to thousands of employees who felt pressured to meet unrealistic sales goals. One Wells Fargo branch manager, for example, had a teenage daughter with 24 accounts, an adult daughter with 18, a husband with 21, a brother with 14 and a father with four.

Ms. Tolstedt, an executive whose rise once paralleled Wells Fargo 's own success, was singled out 142 times in the report for setting the tone in the troubled retail-banking unit. At one point, she is described as being "scared to death" of hurting her unit's sales figures. There's "no evidence that Tolstedt showed serious concern about the effects of improper sales practices on Wells Fargo 's customers," the report added.

Compounding the issue, Ms. Tolstedt gave presentations that directors later felt "minimized and understated problems" at her unit. At a May 2015 meeting, the report said, she told some board members 230 people had been terminated over sales-practice issues, a number far short of the aggregate total. Board members didn't find out until the September 2016 regulatory settlement that 5,300 employees had been fired over five years related to sales practices.

In response to the investigation findings, the board decided last week to claw back Ms. Tolstedt's outstanding stock options valued at about $47.3 million, in addition to $19 million in earlier clawback activity. It also is taking away through scheduled retirement benefits an additional $28 million of incentive compensation paid to Mr. Stumpf in March 2016 on top of $41 million in earlier clawbacks.

The board's review caps a six-month investigation that not only has rocked Wells Fargo but the broader banking industry, with dozens of firms examining their own sales practices at the behest of regulators.

Years of aggressive sales tactics in pockets around the country led to Wells Fargo 's September settlement. Since then politicians, regulators and customers among others, have criticized the bank. In February, it reached a preliminary settlement for $110 million over a customer class action case. Other state and federal investigations are continuing and the bank has said it is cooperating.

Improper sales issues began to escalate within Wells Fargo as far back as 2002, the report noted, years earlier than the bank has agreed to check for consumer harm. The bank has said it is going back an additional two years beyond the 2011-to-2016 time frame regulators called for in last year's consent orders.

Mr. Sloan said Monday Wells Fargo will make things "right" with customers who reach out to the bank with problems related to the sales practices, regardless of the timing.

By 2004, a Wells Fargo 's internal investigations employee wrote to executives in that group about sales issues, according to the report. As early as 2012, some top Wells Fargo retail banking and risk executives knew the firm had a problem with its strategy of "cross selling" multiple products to the same customers. The issue gained more exposure with a Los Angeles Times story on the topic and a 2015 lawsuit by the Los Angeles City Attorney.

But the company was slow to stamp out the issues, the report said, in part because its decentralized structure gave Ms. Tolstedt too much power and officials from other units, such as risk management, human resources and legal, either couldn't connect the dots on problems or weren't powerful enough to do anything about it.

Meanwhile, the board's efforts to understand the scope of the issues were hampered by the "insular and defensive" way in which Ms. Tolstedt ran her division, as well as Mr. Stumpf's loyalty to her, the report said.

Mr. Stumpf "was by nature an optimistic executive who refused to believe that the sales model was seriously impaired," the report said. "He was too late and too slow to call for inspection of or critical challenge to the basic business model."

In late 2015, key board members including lead independent director Stephen Sanger pushed Mr. Stumpf to remove Ms. Tolstedt from her role. But Mr. Stumpf declined, saying that Ms. Tolstedt, while at times controlling, was "the best banker in America," the report said.

Ms. Tolstedt ultimately left her role in July 2016. The board "should have been more forceful in pushing Stumpf to change leadership," the committee concluded. Mr. Stumpf abruptly retired in October.

Mr. Stumpf's successor as CEO, Mr. Sloan, was among seven other executives, in addition to Mr. Stumpf and Ms. Tolstedt, who didn't receive 2016 cash bonuses, and who were subjected to certain equity clawbacks. Mr. Sloan hasn't had other pay clawed back, in part because he wasn't closely involved in overseeing the retail unit until recently.

Corrections & Amplifications

Wells Fargo & Co .'s board said it has clawed back an additional $75 million of pay from two former executives. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated the bank clawed back $72 million.

Swift Releases New Security Mandates for Users

By Katy Burne

Swift, the dominant carrier of cross-border payment instructions between banks, has finalized a new set of security standards its customers must follow by the end of this year or risk being reported to regulators.

The message carrier, known officially as the Society for Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunication , released the final rules last week, said spokeswoman Natasha de Teran. The move sets a formal time frame for customers to comply with the additional requirements now that they have been vetted by consulting with users. The company announced in September that it would be raising its security standards.

The stepped-up controls are a key plank in Swift's response to a spate of cyberattacks on banks in recent months that have drawn heightened scrutiny from the company's regulators, which include central banks. The attacks included an $81 million theft from the Bangladesh central bank 's account at the Federal Reserve Bank of New York last February and other heists in Ecuador, Vietnam and India over the past couple of years.

The perpetrators of the Bangladesh heist remain unidentified and only $15 million has been turned over to authorities, but U.S. prosecutors are building cases that would link North Korea to the attack. North Korea's mission to the United Nations didn't respond to requests seeking comment.

There are 27 new Swift controls, of which 16 are mandatory and the rest are advised. Users must demonstrate their compliance annually, and the results can be shared with regulators and correspondent banks, financial institutions that provide services on behalf of other banks. A randomized group each year also will have to show that their internal and external auditors agree. Customers' compliance status will be made available to their trading partners within the Swift network.

The new controls include detailed requirements about how users need to physically isolate local Swift-related equipment in a designated zone; protect access to tokens that contain Swift credentials; conduct annual security training for all staff; and have cyber-incident-response plans.

With these controls, users will be able to price in each other's risk to the costs they charge each other; require improvements of each other; choose to cease doing business with each other; or put in extra controls when doing business.

It isn't clear whether meeting all of Swift's new security controls would diminish the legal liability of a user in the event of a cyberbreach. In the case of the Bangladesh heist, Swift reiterated a corporate policy that customers are responsible for their own security, but the Asian central bank initially blamed the Fed and its correspondent-banking partners in New York for not spotting the fraud before executing the payments.

A committee of central banks now is looking to draft international guidelines covering the cybersecurity responsibilities of Swift and correspondent banks, aiming to provide a framework that doesn't currently exist across borders.

New York Fed President William Dudley told an audience of students in New York last month that central banks wanted a framework to define the roles of each institution in the process.

"The thing that we're trying to work on internationally is to try to make sure that everyone understands their responsibility in that [payments] chain," he said. "So it's really important that the person executing the payment, that their &facilities are secure." He added, "In the Bangladesh situation, their facilities were not secure."

China Regulator Warns Banks Away From Speculative Activity

By Shen Hong

SHANGHAI China is taking another step to contain the growing risk in its financial system, warning banks against engaging in speculative activity that has created unhealthy asset bubbles and prevented money from flowing into a weak real economy.

In a document titled Directive 46 circulated to banks Monday and reviewed by The Wall Street Journal, the China Banking Regulatory Commission asked them to conduct self-checks of certain actions it described as "irregularities," including making leveraged investments via wealth management products and the use of new financial instruments such as negotiable certificates of deposits. The regulator said its local branches would also launch spot checks of banks under their jurisdiction.

Without offering details, the regulator warned of "severe penalties" for banks found to have committed serious violations of the new guidelines. The CBRC didn't immediately respond to a request for comment.

Since last year, Beijing has grown increasingly wary of a fast-rising debt pile among companies and local governments, as well as periodic investment booms in markets ranging from bonds to sugar. Making the prevention of financial risk and reduction of leverage top priorities this year, authorities have stepped up their efforts in recent months, especially in the money market where the central bank has raised key short-term interest rates twice since late January.

In its latest directive, which runs seven pages, and a nine-page list of guidelines, the CBRC warned banks against using regulatory loopholes, idle funds and insider trading to profit from various forms of arbitrage.

In addition to voicing longstanding concerns about banks using financial products to remove bad loans from their balance sheets, the CRBC specifically defined the use of wealth management products to make leveraged bets in financial markets as an irregular activity.

Over the past decade, wealth management products have become prevalent in China's shadow banking sector, which includes nonbank lenders. These short-term products traditionally offer investment returns much higher than bank deposits. Because the products are typically invested in long-term assets such as bonds, the issuers are faced with periodic needs to secure funds to repay investors. This has contributed to increasingly frequent cash crunches in China's financial system, such as one late last month when short-term interbank funding costs rose to two-year highs.

In the directive, the CBRC also asked banks to conduct self-checks of whether they have asked nonbank institutions such as brokerages and private-equity firms to "add further leverage, duration and risk" in their investments. It also warned banks against investing their own wealth management products in those issued by others.

The latest clampdown also focuses on negotiable certificates of deposits, a popular new financial tool Chinese banks use to replenish capital but also add leverage. These bondlike instruments allow banks to borrow money typically for less than a year.

The CBRC instructed banks to disclose whether they have used large issuances of NCDs to finance various forms of speculative investment that intensify "latent liquidity risk."

The regulator said its campaign was aimed at "guiding the banking sector back to its roots" and ensuring that funds are "invested into the real economy." It warned against behavior it described as "working without bending down" and "sitting there collecting free money."

The CBRC asked banks to submit a report on their self-checks by June 12 and to complete them by the end of November.

WSJ Pro: Fight Breaks Out Over $15 Million Left Over From CFPB Settlement

By Yuka Hayash

WASHINGTON What should regulators do if money is left over from a settlement fund after affected consumers are made whole?

That is the conundrum a federal judge ordered financial regulators to address on Monday as he questioned a recent attempt by state attorneys general to redirect such funds to their own coffers.

The order from the U.S. District Court for the Southern District of New York is the latest development in an unusual case in which several state attorneys general have sought to modify the settlement terms of an enforcement action.

In June 2015, the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau and Sprint Corp. reached a settlement under which the company agreed to refund consumers $50 million to settle charges that it had allowed customers to be illegally billed by third-party merchants.

Sprint made refunds to consumers who filed claims but was left with $15.14 million that wasn't distributed. The settlement had called for the company to send any leftover funds to the CFPB. Unless the agency found other ways to use the fund for purposes "reasonably related to the allegations," it was to deposit the funds with the U.S. Treasury Department , according to the terms of the settlement.

However, the state officials stepped in, arguing the undistributed funds should be used to train and support state attorneys charged with consumer protection. State attorneys general, particularly Democrats, are flexing their muscles to step up their activities amid expectations that the Trump administration will curb the work of the CFPB and other financial regulators.

Sprint itself had an idea of what to do with the money, and later reached an agreement with the state officials to redirect $1.1 million of the leftover funds to a community organization that provides internet access to underprivileged high-school students. The agreement called for the remaining $14 million to go to the states, not the Treasury.

The CFPB itself didn't express its position on the matter at the time.

Calling the motion from the state attorneys general a "peculiar posture," District Judge William Pauley on Monday ordered the CFPB to state its view on the move. He also ordered the Justice Department, which will be representing the Treasury Department, to weigh in. Furthermore, the judge said the proposed arrangement might raise an issue with a law that provides that government officials "receiving money for the government from any source shall deposit that money with the Treasury."

A CFPB spokesman declined to comment Monday. Justice Department and Sprint officials couldn't immediately be reached.

The proposal to modify the settlement terms was filed by the Connecticut attorney general's office on behalf of its counterparts in Indiana, Kansas and Vermont. Specifically, they proposed redirecting the undistributed funds to the National Association of Attorneys General for the purpose of developing a training and research center for consumer protection.

A spokeswoman for the Connecticut attorney general's office declined to comment Monday.

The CFPB and the Justice Department have until May 10 to file their respective opinions.

Lending for Commercial Property Falls as Investors Pull Back; Regulators Concerned

By Peter Grant

Commercial real estate lending by banks, insurance companies and other financial institutions is declining as sales activity slows and regulators voice concern about the sector.

Lenders closed roughly $491 billion of mortgage loans in 2016, down 3% from 2015, according to new statistics from the Mortgage Bankers Association. Most of the decline occurred in the fourth quarter, when volume was 7% lower than the same quarter in 2015, according to Jamie Woodwell, the trade group's head of commercial property research.

Despite the decrease, the new volume number was the third highest since the association began doing the survey, behind 2015 and the record year of 2007.

The decline between 2015 and 2016 was due partly to a slowing of property sales, meaning fewer buyers needed financing. "There's a very tight correlation" between the two, Mr. Woodwell said.

Buyers, for their part, are getting jittery because values have been rising for eight years and are near record highs. In all, investors purchased $493.7 billion worth of U.S. commercial property in 2016, down 10% from 2015, according to data firm Real Capital Analytics .

The slowdown is accelerating this year. Investors have purchased just $50.3 billion worth of U.S. commercial property in the first two months of 2017, compared with $80.1 billion during the same period in 2016, according to data firm Real Capital Analytics .

Regulators have been expressing concern about overheated commercial real estate lending. At the end of the third quarter of 2016 there were 521 institutions insured by the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp . with potentially worrisome concentrations of risk, according to a FDIC report earlier this year. That's up from 474 institutions in the third quarter of 2015, the report said.

Yet banks and insurers are getting more aggressive over deals as investment in the sector declines. A flock of new lenders also are emerging on the scene, including investment funds formed by private-equity firms that are focused on real estate debt.

"It's tougher right now," said Craig Bender, who heads up ING Groep NV 's U.S. real estate lending business. "The banks are hungry. The life insurance companies are hungry."

ING Groep two years ago relaunched its real-estate business after shutting it down following the global financial crisis. Last year, it made a $570 million refinance loan for the Sony Building , an iconic mixed-use skyscraper at 550 Madison Ave. in New York City.

Many bank executives view the commercial property market as healthy, given rising rents and occupancies. Wells Fargo & Co ., the country's largest commercial property market lender, expects to lend about $30 billion this year, about the same as in 2016 and 2015, according to Mark Myers, the bank's head of commercial real estate. Those amounts are "high water marks" for the bank, he said.

Still, the bank recognizes that property values are at record levels after rising for eight years. "I'd say, if I could look back one year ago, we're probably more cautious [now]," Mr. Myers said.

Some lenders are competing by making riskier loans such as those that finance construction or occupy a "mezzanine" position between first mortgages and equity. In the first quarter of 2017, construction and land loans on bank balance sheets were up 12.8% to $306.1 billion compared with the same period a year earlier, according to loan tracker Trepp LLC .

Lenders and developers have gotten especially aggressive in building rental apartments. More units are under way today than in any period since the mid-1970s, experts said.

"There's no current looming event that regulators are looking to say we foresee a problem," said Matthew Anderson , managing director of Trepp. "It's just the sheer volume and pace of activity and the length of how long it's gone on."

Lenders also have been emboldened because loan performance is doing well. Just 0.59% of commercial mortgages held on balance sheets of banks and thrifts today are more than 90 days delinquent, the lowest rate in more than a decade, according to the Mortgage Bankers Association. By comparison the delinquency rate was 4.21% at the end of 2010.

The improving loan quality reflects steadily rising prices. A property value index compiled by Green Street Advisors more than doubled between 2009 when it hit its post-crash low point. But lately that index plateaued. In March, it declined by 0.5%.

Analysis: If Everyone Is So Confident, Why Aren't They Borrowing?

By Aaron Back

One of the great mysteries and biggest concerns in the economy right now is the slowing growth in bank lending. Economists are searching for answers but none are entirely satisfying.

Total loans and leases extended by commercial banks in the U.S. this year were up just 3.8% from a year earlier as of March 29, according to the latest Federal Reserve data. That compares with 6.4% growth in all of last year, and a 7.6% pace as of late October.

The slowdown is more surprising given the rise in business and consumer confidence since the election. And it is worrisome because the lack of business investment is considered an important reason why economic growth has remained weak.

Loans to businesses have slowed most sharply, with the latest data showing commercial and industrial loans up just 2.8% from a year earlier, compared with 8.9% growth in late October. Economists at Goldman Sachs estimate the slowdown in commercial and industrial lending alone equates to a $100 billion shortfall in loans.

Investors may start to get more clarity on what is causing the slowdown when banks start reporting first-quarter earnings on Thursday.

One explanation is that many companies have been tapping corporate bond markets to lock in low rates, and in some cases to pay down more expensive bank debt. In the first quarter of this year, corporate bond issuance rose by 18% from a year earlier, according to the Securities Industry and Financial Markets Association . But one reason for the increase is that the first quarter of 2016 was dismal because of market turmoil. The rise isn't enough to explain the entire shortfall in lending.

Goldman's economists also point to dynamics in the oil-and-gas industry. When oil prices were falling sharply in the first quarter of 2016, many energy companies couldn't tap capital markets for financing. Instead they drew on bank lines of credit. Now that oil prices have rebounded, many of these companies are paying down their bank lines, Goldman says.

As the Goldman economists admit, this hypothesis is hard to verify since the Federal Reserve lending data aren't broken down by industry. Using data from the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency on the total size of credit facilities, they assume commodities companies borrowed around one-third of loan commitments available to them by March last year, then paid down around 35% of that balance over the rest of the year. They figure this would account for roughly the entire $100 billion shortfall in C&I lending.

But Goldman's figures are very rough estimates, and others are skeptical. Paul Ashworth , an economist at research firm Capital Economics , says the oil-industry thesis doesn't explain why there was such a sudden deceleration starting in November. Moreover, he notes that lending has slowed across the board, not just in C&I but also in mortgages, consumer loans and commercial real estate. Strikingly, loans by U.S. branches of foreign banks have fallen by $85 billion since the end of October, and now account for $871 billion of loans outstanding.

Political uncertainty seems partly to blame. Consumers and businesses may express greater confidence since the election, but many might still hesitate to take out big-ticket loans to fund new projects until they have greater clarity on the outlook for tax, trade and health-care policy.

Such caution would only be rational. It also suggests the growth surge many investors are expecting may not materialize until the policy picture out of Washington becomes clearer.

WSJ Pro: Ukraine's Central Bank Governor Resigns

By James Marson

MOSCOW Ukrainian Central Bank Governor Valeria Gontareva submitted her resignation Monday, heralding the end of a nearly three-year tenure that helped Ukraine stave off a financial meltdown and clean up its notoriously murky banking system.

Ms. Gontareva will leave her post on May 10, according a statement posted on the National Bank of Ukraine 's website. She said she resigned as she felt her mission to turn the central bank into "a powerful modern institution" had been accomplished. Her resignation needs to be approved by Ukraine's parliament and President Petro Poroshenko , who will then select a new central bank chief.

Ms. Gontareva said that there would be no further changes to the central bank's board in the near future.

"Neither will there be changes in the central bank's policy," she told reporters, according to the statement. "Continuing the chosen path is the key to the success of our country and support of international partners."

Ms. Gontareva, 52 years old, took charge of the central bank in June 2014, with the country's coffers nearly empty after the corrupt previous President Viktor Yanukovych fled amid street protests, and separatists backed by Russia battling government forces in Ukraine's east. Ms. Gontareva, a former investment banker, introduced a flexible exchange rate for the country's currency, the hryvnia, and closed down around half of Ukraine's 180 banks, which for years had been run opaquely by rich tycoons.

Those successes helped stabilize the banking system and made Ms. Gontareva a rare bright spot for the Ukrainian government, which critics have accused of making slow progress on rapid overhauls to its economy and administration pledged after the 2014 revolution.

But it also provoked anger among some Ukrainians and populist politicians who blamed her for the sharp devaluation of the hryvnia and losses of deposits as the central bank closed down failing banks. Some protesters staged a mock funeral with a coffin outside the National Bank of Ukraine in March.

The International Monetary Fund , which Ukraine is relying on for financing, disbursed a further $1 billion last week as part of a $17.5 billion package, but warned that the government needed to accelerate structural and governance reforms to build a modern market economy.

SEC Targets Fraud on Stock Tip-Sheet Websites

By Chris Dieterich

That bullish stock tip you found on the Internet?

Turns out it might have been paid for by the company itself.

The Securities and Exchange Commission on Monday announced fraud charges against a raft of publicly traded companies, executives, writers and promotion firms, alleging that hundreds of articles published on tip-sheet websites including Seeking Alpha touted stocks without properly disclosing that authors were compensated to write them.

Companies that pay writers or publicists to hype stocks must disclose this relationship.

The SEC alleges that former executives at public companies including small-cap companies ImmunoCellular Therapeutics and Galena Biopharma , paid promotions firms to commission bullish articles.

A spokeswoman for ImmunoCellular, which settled charges with the SEC, said that the matter related to an investigation into a former CEO and that the company "cooperated fully with the SEC and is looking forward to putting this matter behind us."

A spokesperson for Galena did not immediately return a call seeking comment.

None of the websites were charged. A spokesperson for Seeking Alpha said that it takes stock promotions "very seriously." The company said that it "documents all authors' claims to not having been compensated by third parties, and we maintain accurate records of all Seeking Alpha authors' real-life identities. We don't have the ability to investigate claims by the SEC or other regulators. However, we do cooperate with their inquiries, and our policies act as a strong deterrent against potential promotions."

The SEC alleges in its enforcement action that more than 250 articles, mostly written under pseudonyms, falsely stated that authors had not been compensated by the companies they were writing about.

Some 17 parties have settled the charges for sums ranging from $2,200 to $3 million, the SEC said, while litigation continues against 10 others.

"These companies, promoters, and writers allegedly misled investors by disguising paid promotions as objective and independent analyses," said Stephanie Avakian, acting director of the SEC's Division of Enforcement.

The SEC's orders, as well as a pair of complaints filed in federal district court, allege that deceptive measures were often used to mask the true sources of the articles from investors.

Articles were ghost-written under pseudonyms including The Swiss Trader, Trading Maven, Mel Daris, Chemistfrog and Amy Baldwin. The latter nom de plume came with a profile stating that the author was "employed by a Fortune 20 company" and "assessing risk management and the financial health of various companies," when in fact no person with those credentials was employed by the publicity firms.

The SEC, in conjunction with the charges, released an alert to investors warning that "articles on an investment research website that appear to be an unbiased source of information or provide commentary on multiple stocks may be part of an undisclosed paid stock promotion."

WSJ Pro Transcript: Q&A With New York Fed President William Dudley

William C. Dudley, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, took audience questions after a speech Friday, April 7, 2017 in New York. He discussed the Fed's balance sheet, financial regulation, the economy and other topics. Here is a transcript of the exchange, lightly edited for length and clarity.

Q: (In progress) week the minutes came out, and I see you said very clearly you are going to run down the balance sheet. So can you talk a little bit about what's the thought process? Why is a big balance sheet a problem? Is a big balance sheet a problem because it's holding long rates down? And is the implicit message here that the (Federal Open Market Committee) would like to see long rates go up just a little bit?

WILLIAM C. DUDLEY : Well, first of all, we didn't say we would. We said we anticipate that we might be able to do it sometime  (laughter) if the economy follows in an appropriate fashion. So it was appropriately caveated. And we're still working on this. The FOMC has not made any formal decisions or announcements in terms of how this is all going to work.

I think, though, we have made, I think, clear in a number of ways how we are thinking about this. Number one, that we that the primary tool of monetary policy is short-term rates, not the balance sheet. And so that once we get to the point where we want to start to end the reinvestment process, we're probably going to do it in a way that's gradual and phased out. And, two, that once that process starts, the primary tool of monetary policy will be adjusting short-term interest rates, not the balance sheet; we don't see the balance sheet as an active tool of monetary policy.

So the question is, why might you want to, you know, allow the reinvestment to run off? Well, I think, you know, an obvious reason is that, you know, the Fed has a particularly large footprint now in the U.S. financial system, with $4  trillion of assets. That's one reason: maybe you don't want to have a footprint of that size. Number two, I think that you might want to have a Federal Reserve that actually has the capacity to potentially expand their balance sheet again in the future. If we ever have another, you know, bad, bad recession that pins monetary policy at the zero lower bound, you might want to have the ability to actually expand your balance sheet again to provide support to the economy. So I think there is you know, I think there's a number of reasons to think that this is sort of an appropriate part of the transition back to a to a more normal monetary policy regime.

Q: A follow-up question on the balance sheet question, actually that ties in more closely to financial regulation.

There's been, I believe from the (former Fed governor) Jeremy Stein publication suggesting that the balance sheet has a financial stability component, that a large balance sheet might mitigate the risk of maturity transformation; it might disincentivize excessive short-term financing. So to what extent does the Federal Reserve consider the balance sheet to be not only a monetary policy tool, but a macroprudential tool?

MR. DUDLEY: Yeah, I mean, Jeremy Stein 's done a lot of work on this issue of is there a shortage of sort of safe financial assets in the financial system, and could the Fed or others provide more safe assets and that would somehow reduce the risk for maturity transformation. I think and I think that's a you know, I think that's an issue that there's ongoing debate about: some people thinking that that's quite important, other people thinking that that's not as not as big a deal as maybe Jeremy what Jeremy sees.

Obviously, you know, the other way that you can obviate against the maturity transformation issue is you can have liquidity regulation. So we have the LCR. We have the NSFR, net stable funding ratio. So there's other ways of sort of mitigating some of that risk of maturity transformation.

I think, you know, in general, I mean, I think the you know, the decision that the that the Fed, I think, is going to make is I'm not sure that we're going to go back to a balance sheet as small as the one that we had prior to the crisis because that would imply that we'd be going back to a what's called a corridor system, with just a few amount of reserves in the system and the Federal Reserve having to intervene every day, adding and draining reserves to try and keep the federal-funds rate right at target.

I think there's a you know, speaking for myself, you know, I think I would I favor a system more like the current system, which is called a floor system, where you have sufficient amount of excess reserves in the system so that your interest rate control is really driven by the overnight RRP and the interest rate you pay on excess reserves. That way you don't have to do all this all this you know, a lot of gymnastics to keep the federal-funds rate close to your to your target. It also has the advantage, to your point, that there would be more safe assets in the system; but also probably actually also facilitate, you know, sort of just efficiency of payment flows because people would banks would have higher balances, and so you wouldn't have as, you know, much need for daylight overdrafts and other queuing of payments. So I think that my own view is I favor going back to a floor system.

Now, the question is, how big is that? We don't really know for sure exactly how many how much excess reserves you need to be able to just sit there and not have to intervene in the market to keep the federal-funds rate at your objective. But generally I think most people, you know, that have looked at it suggest that maybe you have excess reserves somewhere in the area of a  a trillion to a trillion dollars, something in that order of magnitude. That's a lot less than what we have currently.

So my view I mean, speaking for myself is that I would expect that if the economy cooperates that we will begin to normalize our balance sheet starting sometime late this year or next year, and it will be a very gradual process, and we'll get to a much smaller balance sheet. But not as small as where we were prior to the crisis, when it was really just currency.

Q: Hearing the early objectives, they're trying to stop expansion of Dodd-Frank, or whatever, to stop a real tragedy from occurring. When we get all through the regulations revived year by year, what responsibility is laid on bank management not to pursue bad loans, the things which caused this? Does this have to be done by regulation, or can it be done by some understanding bank managements have a responsibility?

MR. DUDLEY: Well, of course bank managements have a responsibility to run their banks well. I mean, absolutely. I mean, do you think it's really feasible for the Federal Reserve or any regulator to have to run, you know, an organization with hundreds of thousands of employees and be able to see into every nook and cranny?

That's why we put so much emphasis on things like, you know, good technology, good risk management, good governance. Those are things that we can assess, and then it's on the bank to make sure that they actually carry those things out.

But it's absolutely on the on the banks. And I would hope that the bank shareholders would care and the board of directors would care deeply about the safety and soundness of their institutions.

Q: Actually, two questions. Just if you could comment on the apparent gap between some of on the economy, if you could comment on the apparent gap between some of the soft data, which paints a very robust picture; and some of the hard data, which appears to paint a less-robust picture. The New York Fed is sort of in the robust camp. And so which one is going to converge to which one, which way? Is the 

MR. DUDLEY: You tell me. (Laughter.)

Q: And, you know, so that's the first question.

And then just on the on the balance sheet, just the sort of trade-offs between the front end and the long end. And are they are they fungible, or how do you think about the trade-offs between the front end and the long end in terms of you know, is deferring or delaying, as you've recently said, pausing perhaps, you know, a rate hike to give room to let the balance sheet off, a pause for how long relative to just the relationship and how you think about 

MR. DUDLEY: Well, let me take that one first because I think some people misconstrued what I said last week. I said a "little pause." I didn't a pause is pretty short already, and I think a little pause is even shorter than that. (Laughs.) I mean, the idea is basically when you make the decision that you're going to start to normalize your balance sheet, that should have some consequence for long-term yields, so that should tighten financial conditions. Similarly, when you make the decision to raise short-term interest rates, that should also tighten financial conditions. So presumably you at the time that you make the decision on the balance sheet, you might want to forgo the decision on short-term rates just to make sure that the balance-sheet decision doesn't turn out to be a bigger decision than you thought you were making. So I would emphasize the words "little pause."

The question of short versus hard data, you know, I think you just raised what's a, you know, interesting conundrum right now, where the soft data is, you know, pretty weak. I mean, if you look at people's projections of first-quarter (gross domestic product growth), I think most of the consensus forecasts I've seen out there suggest that it's sort of in the 1 , maybe even a little weaker 1 to 1  %. If you look at things, though, that include that soft data, like the New York Fed 's Nowcast, which we include everything that sort of weighs on our ability to forecast GDP it's quite a bit firmer than that. I don't know where it is today. We release it on Friday, so when I get home I'll take a look at that. I think last week, though, I think it was at 2.8 %, I believe. So that's there is a gap. You know, I just don't think we know yet.

Another aspect of this that makes it a little more difficult is it looks like we have a seasonal adjustment problem with first-quarter GDP. You know, every year for the last few years, first-quarter GDP has come in unusually weak relative to the other quarters. So, you know, even if we get something that looks like 1 % in the first quarter, it might be actually more like 2 % in reality. So I don't know how it's going to be resolved. I'm interested as interested in seeing the movie as you are.

The Fiduciary Rule Is Delayed. So Now What?

By Lisa Beilfuss

In delaying Monday's initial compliance deadline for a new retirement-savings rule, the Trump administration has given the financial-services industry a breather and some clues into how the regulation might change.

But investors and advisers will have to wait at least until year's end for clarity on whether the fiduciary rule which aims to ensure that retirement savers get conflict-free advice ultimately stays, goes or gets revised.

The delay in the fiduciary rule's applicability date to June 9, made official Friday, gives the Labor Department more time to execute an order from President Donald Trump to re-evaluate the rule's potential economic impact on firms and consumers. It also gives a bit of a reprieve to parts of the industry that have stood to be most affected and have struggled to come into compliance.

While the regulation's fate remains uncertain, some experts say the Labor Department's approach to the delay suggests the fiduciary rule will be revised rather than rescinded. Rob Cirrotti, head of retirement and investment solutions at Pershing LLC , a division of Bank of New York Mellon Corp . , said the delay notice signals that "certain elements are here to stay" and some others may be watered down or revised out.

Here are a few things to know about the postponement:

What exactly is delayed and for how long?

The postponement isn't as straightforward as many observers expected, Mr. Cirrotti said. The Labor Department essentially announced a two-part delay.

On June 9, presuming there is no further delay, the heart of the rule kicks in. "Retirement investors will be entitled to advice that adheres to basic fiduciary standards," a Labor Department spokesman said, meaning brokers and insurance agents must work in clients' best interest.

That's the spirit of the rule and it isn't going away, Mr. Cirrotti said. Before the fiduciary rule, such advice givers have had to meet a lower bar of suitability that allowed them to sell higher-cost products so long as they were suitable to the investor's risk profile.

Other elements of the regulation have been pushed out until Jan. 1, 2018. Those include some of the communications advisers would need to provide clients, such as conflict-of-interest disclosures required for certain transactions.

Also delayed through year's end: A stricter exemption that would apply to sellers of variable and fixed-index annuities, which typically generate commissions. The fiduciary rule as originally written requires sellers of those annuities to meet a higher bar if they want to be exempt from the regulation and sell them to retirement savers. The Labor Department said the new, more onerous exemption won't kick in until January.

Why kick certain elements out to January?

Experts say that 60 days isn't enough time to conduct a review that is consistent with Mr. Trump's directive, which ordered the Labor Department to review the rule with a focus on potential effects on consumer costs, litigation and industry disruption. At the same time, the Labor Department couldn't get a longer delay approved by the gatekeeper of such delays the Office of Management and Budget because of the resulting costs to investors and the industry.

The department said it wanted to "avoid the possibility of investor confusion and needless or excessive expense as firms build systems and compliance structures that may ultimately be unnecessary or mismatched with the Department's final decisions on the issues raised by the Presidential Memorandum."

What clues can be gleaned about the rule's fate?

Mr. Cirrotti said the elements kicked out to January, particularly around client communications and the new exemption for annuities, are where the landscape is most likely to change.

That could mean brokers and insurance agents won't be made to provide all of the disclosures around conflicts that the rule originally required, potentially making the compliance less onerous but also keeping investors in the dark about conflicts that can come about when a broker or agent earns commissions.

Most amendments to a pre-existing exemption that applied to sales of variable and fixed-indexed annuities, which typically generate commissions, have been delayed through year's end. The fiduciary rule as originally written required sellers of those annuities to meet the conditions of the new Best Interest Contract Exemption in order to sell those products to retirement savers. Before being amended, the pre-existing exemption allowed advisers and insurance agents to receive commissions and fees, under conditions that required, in part, that the fees and commissions are "reasonable," for the sale of products like annuity contracts and mutual funds shares.

The new exemption, called the Best Interest Contract Exemption, also allows commissions to still be earned. But to do so, sellers have to disclose their commission and commit to working in the client's best interest. The conditions of this exemption (except for the impartial conduct standards that include a commitment to working in the clients' best interests) won't kick in until January.

Will there be further delays?

It's possible.

In its delay notice, the Labor Department said "if, after receiving comments on the issues raised by the President's Memorandum, the Department concludes that significant changes are necessary or that it needs more time to complete its review, it retains the ability to further extend the Jan. 1, 2018 applicability dates or to grant additional interim relief."

Will commentary from consumers and industry affect the rule's outcome?

The Labor Department said it "continues to receive a very high volume of comment and petition letters on a daily basis." The Labor Department recently reopened the comment period, seeking feedback on two counts. It opened the window for 15 days specifically to generate comments on the delay, and it opened another window for 45 days during which consumers and the industry could voice opinions on the issues raised by Mr. Trump's memo.

The department reported 193,000 comments and petitions as of March 17, when the first comment period closed At that point, comments expressing opposition to a delay outnumbered comments in favor of a delay by 12 to 1.

The second comment period covering the broader issues raised by the president is open until April 17.

"Comments on the rulemaking process are always taken seriously," the Labor Department spokesman said. He said that during the six-plus years that the project has been active, "there have been several rounds of public comment which resulted in numerous changes to the rule and exemptions."

Proposed Mexican Fintech Law Would Lift Banks, Startups

A law drafted by the office of Mexico's Secretary of Finance and Public Credit that would put the country's growing financial technology industry under the authority of the country's banking and securities regulator would help lenders and fintech firms grow, Fitch says. A draft of the law released late last month shows it would apply to crowdfunding platforms, companies that deal with virtual currencies such as bitcoin and online payment services, and would require all fintech firms to register with the regulator in order to help root out money laundering and disclose their methodologies for assessing consumer risk. "Mexico has significant growth opportunities for fintech considering the country's large size, high rate of penetration of mobile phones and internet and substantial unbanked population," Fitch says, noting 150-180 fintech companies have sprung up in Mexico in recent years. (robbie.whelan@wsj.com; @RWhelanWSJ)

China's banking regulator warns banks against rising risks and has vowed to inspect their risk-control measures. Banks must strengthen their efforts to prevent risks related to liquidity, wealth management products, real estate and bond markets as well as local government debt, among others, the China Banking Regulatory Commission says in a guideline issued Monday. (liyan.qi@wsj.com)

Wells Fargo (WFC) Chairman Stephen Sanger gave more detail on former chairman and CEO John Stumpf 's most recent clawback as a result of the board's investigation. He said Stumpf doesn't necessarily need to write a check. Instead, it will be "captured" through Stumpf's supplemental retirement plan. Stumpf has to forfeit a total of $69M as a result of the bank's sales practices scandal. (emily.glazer@wsj.com; @EmilyGlazerWSJ)

Australian banks' exposure to the property market is yet another source of sovereign risk for the country, although the lenders' strong capitalization and generally high financial strength significantly reduces the risk of a banking crisis and any related costs to the government, Moody's says. It expects household debt will continue to rise from already elevated levels, as low interest rates and expectations of ongoing gains in home prices encourage purchases. Still, demand should be tempered somewhat by fresh regulation to cool investor demand and capping banks' interest-only mortgage lending, helping mitigate housing market risks, Moody's says. (robb.stewart@wsj.com; @RobbMStewart)

Regulators Likely to Benefit From Fintech Methods

Financial regulators are likely the next group to leverage analytical methods pioneered by fintech firms, according to a "https://www.carlyle.com/sites/default/files/market_commentary/Market published by private-equity firm Carlyle Group . Regulators have been slow to exploit advances that helped fintech startups challenge traditional banks and securities firms, according to Brian T. Schreiber and Jason M. Thomas . Between 2009 and 2015, banks paid more than $150 billion in penalties to authorities; during that time, implementation of the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act added more than 5,000 restrictions on the industry, raising its regulatory burden by a third, according to the paper. Regulators need to keep track of the impact of these rules, as well as others. The supervisory toolkit should include data feeds that provide regulators with an on-demand view of trends and risks, the paper's authors write. The argument recalls efforts by the Securities and Exchange Commission to build a real-time data warehouse of all quotes and trades in the stock market, which should be operational by 2018.

Banks' Confidence About Deregulation Might Be Premature

Banks are on a hiring spree, and a lot of the job growth is coming from states other than New York, including Illinois, Texas and Ohio. That indicates President Donald Trump 's promises to deregulate are being taken seriously by the financial-services sector, "https://www.bloomberg.com/view/articles/2017-04-07/banks-hire-like-they-believe-in-trump". It's true that banks might have other reasons to add jobs, including growing profitability, he says. Meanwhile, Mr. Trump hasn't done much to act on his words, and getting Congress to revamp the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act will be a tough task. "So to the extent that financial companies are getting excited about deregulation, their enthusiasm might be premature," Mr. Whitehouse concludes.

Fed Needs Fewer Academics, More Businesspeople

The Federal Reserve should "increase the intellectual diversity" of its board of governors by hiring fewer academics and more "candidates with real-world business experience," "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-fed-could-use-less-book-learning-and-more-street-smarts-1491864871". Mr. Ricketts, founder of financial-services company TD Ameritrade and President Donald Trump 's nominee to be deputy secretary of the Commerce Department, says, "Intellect is important, but it's not enough. Practical knowledge, experience and wisdom are needed to produce good policy, and good policy is essential for our free-market economy to prosper."

"http://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/04/10/an-unusual-pattern-is-taking-hold-in-the-junk-bond-market/" sold by U.S. companies is showing a never-seen-before pattern, one sign of how expensive that corner of the market has become.

The quickening evolution of trading   in charts

By Robin Wigglesworth, US markets editor 
609 words
11 April 2017
11:40
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The evolution of financial markets is quickening thanks to the rise of automated trading algorithms, which range from the relatively simple, such as exchange traded funds, to artificial-intelligence powered hedge funds. Here is a selection of charts showing some aspects of how investing and markets have changed in recent years.

The most apparent shift is in the world of investing, where traditional active managers have suffered big outflows in favour of cheaper, passive investment vehicles such as ETFs and index-tracking funds. Vanguard alone, with $4tn of assets under management, dwarfs the entire hedge fund industry. And as this chart from Citi shows, passive investment inflows are accelerating.

Here is another chart from Citi that shows the state of play. Passive funds now make up a big chunk of overall assets under management in Asian and US equities, and a smaller   but rising   proportion in areas such as US bonds.

In addition to the usual arguments for passive funds, such as their cheapness, Citi strategist Robert Buckland says that the  paradox of skill  means it is getting harder for fund managers to beat their benchmarks.

He points out that the number of chartered financial analysts relative to the number of listed companies has exploded over the past two decades.  That s a lot of well-trained buyside and sellside analysts for every stock. No wonder it s got so tough to beat the market,  he says.

Things look a little brighter in the world of quantitative investing. The growing view that the future of active investing will not be human fund managers judging a company by the firmness of the chief executive s handshake but computer scientists tinkering with trading algorithms has led to an explosion of job postings for financial engineers, while demand for more traditional analysts has stagnated, as this Bank of America Merrill Lynch chart shows.

The number of investment factors used by investment groups has swelled since the financial crisis, as financial scientists unearth more and more tradable signals from big data, such as credit card transactions, app downloads and even satellite imagery.

High-octane, algorithmic trading is also ascendant, with high-frequency trading volumes picking up from the post-crisis drop-off. Meanwhile, the volume of trading done by traditional active asset managers has flatlined, even as the share of trading done by passive investment vehicles has grown, as this Credit Suisse chart shows.

This is affecting the structure of the stock market, with the most actively traded stocks becoming even more liquid, while smaller stocks are becoming more expensive to trade. Flash crashes are becoming more common, with algorithms making, cancelling and adjusting quotes at a fast pace. Here is another Credit Suisse chart that shows how many quote changes are made per million of shares traded.

The rise of ETFs is also reshaping the trading day. Passive investment funds typically try to do most of their trading at the end of the day so that their holdings best match their benchmark, which has herded ever more trading activity in the last half-hour of the day, as this chart of the Russell 2000 small companies gauge shows. The pattern is what analysts call a  liquidity smile .

It is important to remember that despite the roaring inflows into ETFs and quant funds, we are still only at the early stages of this investment revolution, with the vast majority of money still managed traditionally by human fund managers. There will undoubtedly be some potholes along the way, but markets are increasingly going to be shaped by machines rather than man.
