Head of boutique says industry should start to listen to retail clients

When John Ions, a fifty-year-old graduate of North Staffordshire Polytechnic, was appointed chief executive of Liontrust seven years ago, he inherited a fund company in crisis.

In early 2010, Liontrust was abandoned by two star fund managers, causing its assets to crash from a high of 5bn to 1.1bn. Mr Ions had just been poached from Tactica, a rival investment boutique where he was chief executive, to be Liontrust's head of retail.

Shortly after his arrival at the London-based investment boutique, the dive in assets prompted Liontrust's chief executive at the time, founder Nigel Legge, to quit, and Mr Ions found himself catapulted into the hot seat.

Liontrust has more than recovered since. Although it remains small, the London-listed company has a cluster of funds popular among financial advisers and has gained a reputation for offering solid stock picking in the UK small-cap space.

Its Liontrust Special Situations fund holds its own in the UK equity sector, and is ranked as one of the top five best-performing funds over a three-year period, according to FE Analytics, the data provider.

Good performance has helped to secure money from investors; Liontrust last week reported a 36 per cent increase in assets under management for the year ending March 2017.

Speaking to the Financial Times from his office next to the Savoy Hotel in London's West End, Mr Ions, whose company today oversees 9bn of assets, is not afraid to criticise his more powerful competitors.

One of his biggest peeves in recent years was a ruckus at the Investment Association , the UK's asset management trade body, in 2015 over the consumer-led agenda of Daniel Godfrey , its chief executive at the time.

Some members felt Mr Godfrey made the IA behave more like a regulator than a cheerleader, prompting Schroders and M&G, two of the UK's largest fund houses, to temporarily quit the club and take their 550bn of combined assets with them.

At the time, Mr Ions branded the pair "disrespectful" to their fellow IA board members, who would have preferred to keep the dispute behind closed doors.

Two years later Mr Ions remains visibly irritated by the affair, and the fact that Peter Harrison , chief executive of Schroders, was recently appointed as chairman of the IA. His concern is that the asset management body looks resistant to change.

"[Mr Harrison's appointment] looks like: here's the old guard back."

Mr Ions is focusing on how to accelerate the company's growth, and has been hunting for acquisition targets.

A suitable buying opportunity presented itself late last year, when Alliance Trust, the investment company, decided to sell its investment arm, which had just over 2bn of assets.

Liontrust was the successful bidder, announcing last December that it had bought the investment business for 30m.

"It is a business I looked at a couple of years ago when Katherine Garrett-Cox was running it, so it was obviously something of interest to me," says Mr Ions.

This was around the time Elliott Advisors, the activist hedge fund that launched a dogged campaign to overhaul Alliance Trust, first "raised its head", he says, making a deal difficult.

He continued to pursue Alliance, however, due to the strength of its environmental, social and governance expertise, which Mr Ions believes will help Liontrust attract more institutional money, as well as capturing fussy millennial investors.

"If you look at any of the big institutions or pension funds in Europe, [ethical investing] is pretty much embedded in the first page," he says.

Alliance also brings a European distribution network to Liontrust, which has traditionally been a UK-focused house. Europe will become a "significant potential marketplace", says Mr Ions. He adds that the company will register any new funds in Dublin in future.

While optimistic about his company's future, he concedes the asset management industry faces growth challenges.

Active fund managers, in particular, are struggling to maintain the growth rate of previous years as investors flock to lower-margin passive funds. A tide of regulation from authorities in the US, Asia and Europe has not helped profit margins.

Mr Ions is critical of fund companies that are trying to buy their way out of these problems by making serial acquisitions in areas such as wealth management .

"If I look around at the asset managers now, it is harder and harder to see what [their] strategies are," he says. "There are groups that I think are slightly rabbit-in-the-headlights."

Adding to these woes, many asset managers are doing a terrible job of communicating with investors, he says. This is a significant problem in the UK, where retirees have recently been allowed to take their pension in a lump sum and spend it.

He says: "I've got mates in Newcastle that ring up and go, 'Johnny, what do I do with my lumper?' Obviously it is a hugely important decision for these people, and they don't really have any idea [about where to put their cash]," he says.

One of the consequences of this breakdown in trust has been the worsening reputation of the asset management industry. A damning review of the sector published last year by the UK watchdog exacerbated this problem, he believes.

He is in two minds about the regulator's criticism of fund managers.

On the one hand, Mr Ions is a firm believer in fund management's higher purpose. "[If the] asset management industry mobilises the capital that allows the economy to work, that is a good starting point," he says. "So how come we end up with a bad reputation at the end of it?"

But he also believes many fund managers are out of touch. They could, he says, listen more carefully to individual investors, rather than large institutions with more money.

"The significant change in the next five to 10 years is going to be [that we] now start listening much more to the end investor," he says.

Goldman's Wager on Credit Data Pays Off

By Liz Hoffman and AnnaMaria Andriotis 
1,002 words
10 April 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Goldman Sachs Group Inc. has backed skyscrapers and movie studios. But its big winner lately is a once-sleepy credit bureau.

Goldman bought TransUnion, the smallest of the three main credit-reporting firms, in 2012. By the time it went public three years later, TransUnion had become a data-mining machine, gathering billions of seemingly insignificant tidbits about ordinary Americans that it analyzed and sold to lenders, insurers and others.

Goldman has already pocketed nearly $600 million in profit and is expected to make about five times its initial $550 million investment.

The windfall shows the benefit of Goldman's decision to stick with private-equity investing after the financial crisis. Most big banks exited this activity as regulation stiffened. But Goldman, which has merchant banking deep in its DNA, stayed put and is even raising a new fund, its first since 2007, to buy companies.

Despite some duds -- Goldman backed a Texas utility that later went bankrupt in one of the biggest buyout flops of all time -- private-equity investing has been a moneymaker in recent years. Goldman has invested in a Japanese theme park, a U.S. network of home contractors and a Danish outsourcing company.

The Volcker rule, enacted as part of the Dodd-Frank financial overhaul in 2010, prevented banks from contributing more than 3% of the money raised by private-equity funds. Goldman adapted by doing deals directly from its balance sheet, rather than from a fund where its own money was mixed with investors. Doing deals this way complied with the letter, if not the spirit, of the Volcker rule, though it also limited Goldman to making mostly minority investments rather than the splashy takeovers it once pursued.

Goldman also raised new credit and real-estate funds, where there are more bargains to be found than in a red-hot stock market.

As the Trump administration weighs rolling back parts of Dodd-Frank, many expect the Volcker rule to be high on its list.

Goldman's new fund, which has already raised $4.5 billion and is expected to close later this year closer to its cap of $8 billion, includes just a sliver of Goldman's own money. It recasts the bank more like a plain-vanilla asset manager, investing other people's money and making its own profits largely on fees.

Goldman over the past months sold 20% of its stake as TransUnion shares reached records, climbing 24% so far this year, regulatory filings show. Taking into account stock sales and dividends, Goldman has so far pocketed nearly $600 million in profit and still owns a 25% stake in TransUnion valued at $1.7 billion.

The firm also has earned some $50 million in fees for underwriting TransUnion's IPO and raising debt for the company -- a unique perk for banks that have buyout arms.

TransUnion's contribution to the Wall Street firm extends beyond investment gains. Goldman's new consumer-lending platform, Marcus, was developed inside its private-equity arm by many of the same people who spearheaded the TransUnion investment, according to people familiar with the matter.

Their experience running TransUnion, which includes a seat on its board for a Goldman partner, helped build the bank's familiarity with consumer credit, some of the people said.

Goldman and private-equity firm Advent International bought TransUnion for about $3 billion five years ago, putting in $550 million each and borrowing the rest. The privately held company in previous decades had been owned by the Pritzker family of Chicago.

As Goldman and Advent dug into TransUnion's business, they found the fastest-growing revenue was coming from the company's dealings with online-lending startups, people familiar with the investment said.

These companies, such as LendingClub Corp. and Prosper Marketplace Inc., were using information from credit bureaus to find and vet potential borrowers. They were increasingly hungry for data that could pinpoint borrowers who traditional lenders might overlook or overcharge.

TransUnion's new owners doubled down on these clients. They recruited Jim Peck, a big-data enthusiast who had run LexisNexis Risk Solutions, as chief executive.

TransUnion began appearing at fintech conferences. It rebranded itself with a techy, purposeful vibe, wrapping its initials, a lowercase "tu," in an @ sign. "We're not just a credit bureau; we're a force for good," chirped a 2015 video.

One acquisition tracks public records to help with fraud enforcement related to online shopping, among other things. Another uses utility payments, cellphone billing records and other data points to identify creditworthy borrowers who lenders might have overlooked, either because they have little or no debt history or potential red flags on their traditional credit reports.

TransUnion's costs grew by 56% between 2011 and 2014, twice as rapidly as revenue over that period. That is unusual for a company owned by private-equity firms, which often cut their way to profits.

All three credit-reporting firms -- Equifax, Experian and TransUnion -- compile alternative data on consumers that go beyond the loan payments found in credit reports. And in some ways, TransUnion was late to the game.

Although it has been playing catch-up, TransUnion has found ways to stand apart from its rivals in terms of how it delivers its data to lenders. One example is Prama, a platform TransUnion launched last year that allows lenders to play with data. With a few clicks, lenders can see if their delinquency figures are better than peer groups. They can also find locations where delinquencies are on the rise.

By the time of its IPO in 2015, TransUnion had 30 million gigabytes of data, growing at 25% a year and ranging from voter registration in India to drivers' accident records in the U.S. The company's IPO documents boasted that it had anticipated the arrival of online lenders and "created solutions that catered to these emerging providers."

Markets & Finance: First Trust's ETFs Are Unsung Force

By Asjylyn Loder 
533 words
10 April 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B9
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Some of the biggest action in exchange-traded funds last month took place in two little-known First Trust ETFs.

The biggest trader? First Trust Advisors LP itself.

The trading was triggered by a rebalancing of the $2.5 billion First Trust Dorsey Wright Focus 5 ETF. The fund tracks a Dorsey, Wright & Associates index that analyzes recent market trends for signs of momentum, buying sectors and industries with the most substantial recent gains. It owns just five ETFs at a time, all picked from the First Trust lineup.

Last month, energy stocks dropped out of the index, while the banking sector gained favor, reflecting its postelection rally. The shift prompted the Dorsey Wright ETF to sell one First Trust ETF and buy another.

As a result, assets in a First Trust ETF that buys bank stocks swelled 34-fold in March to $910.6 million, marking the six-month-old fund as one of the most-successful recent launches in the ETF industry. During the month, First Trust Energy AlphaDEX ETF, which invests in energy companies, lost almost two-thirds of its assets.

Dorsey Wright, now a unit of Nasdaq Inc., has long been a favorite among investment advisers. Some follow its strategy without using the Dorsey Wright ETF, buying and selling the underlying First Trust ETFs themselves.

First Trust created the ETF in 2014 because customers wanted the popular Focus 5 strategy packaged into one product, said Ryan Issakainen, senior vice president and ETF strategist with First Trust. Bundling the ETFs saves investors the trouble of rebalancing themselves and offers some tax benefits. First Trust also offers a second Dorsey Wright ETF that makes many of the same trades.

But analysts have questioned the Dorsey Wright ETF for its high fees, market-roiling trades and subpar returns.

"There are significant benefits for First Trust for launching products that fit into this strategy, but some investors don't necessarily know what they're buying," said Todd Rosenbluth, director of ETF and mutual-fund research for CFRA, a consulting firm.

It has been one of First Trust's most successful ETFs. It is also one of its most expensive because First Trust charges investors both for the Dorsey Wright ETF and for the five First Trust ETFs it owns, pushing the total cost to $89 a year for every $10,000 invested. The average investor in an index-tracking stock ETF pays $20, according to Morningstar.

The ETF's trading also stokes interest in First Trust's other ETFs. Its recent purchase of the First Trust Nasdaq Bank ETF made the new fund more appealing to investors who won't buy small, thinly traded products.

While funds of funds are common in the mutual-fund industry, they are relatively rare in ETFs, accounting for 191 U.S. funds with combined assets of $35 billion out of a $2.8 trillion market, according to XTF, a market-analytics firm. Some of the ETFs, especially leveraged equity funds, buy ETFs from several issuers while others invest only in in-house products.

---

Chris Dieterich contributed to this article.

Finance & Markets: First Trust's ETFs Are Unsung Force

By Asjylyn Loder 
661 words
10 April 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B5
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Some of the biggest action in exchange-traded funds last month took place in two little-known First Trust ETFs.

The biggest trader? First Trust Advisors LP itself.

The trading was triggered by a rebalancing of the $2.5 billion First Trust Dorsey Wright Focus 5 ETF. The fund tracks a Dorsey, Wright & Associates index that analyzes recent market trends for signs of momentum, buying sectors and industries with the most substantial recent gains. It owns just five ETFs at a time, all picked from the First Trust lineup.

Last month, energy stocks dropped out of the index while the banking sector gained favor, reflecting its postelection rally. The shift prompted the Dorsey Wright ETF to sell one First Trust ETF and buy another.

As a result, assets in a First Trust ETF that buys bank stocks swelled 34-fold in March to $910.6 million, marking the six-month-old fund as one of the most-successful recent launches in the ETF industry. During the month, the First Trust Energy AlphaDEX ETF, which invests in energy companies, lost almost two-thirds of its assets.

Dorsey Wright, now a unit of Nasdaq Inc., has long been a favorite among investment advisers. Some follow its strategy without using the Dorsey Wright ETF, buying and selling the underlying First Trust ETFs themselves.

First Trust created the ETF in 2014 because customers wanted the popular Focus 5 strategy packaged into one product, said Ryan Issakainen, senior vice president and ETF strategist with First Trust. Bundling the ETFs saves investors the trouble of rebalancing themselves and offers some tax benefits. First Trust also offers a second Dorsey Wright ETF that makes many of the same trades.

But analysts have questioned the Dorsey Wright ETF for its high fees, market-roiling trades and subpar returns.

"There are significant benefits for First Trust for launching products that fit into this strategy, but some investors don't necessarily know what they're buying," said Todd Rosenbluth, director of ETF and mutual-fund research for CFRA, a consulting firm.

It has been one of First Trust's most successful ETFs. It is also one of its most expensive because First Trust charges investors both for the Dorsey Wright ETF and for the five First Trust ETFs it owns, pushing the total cost to $89 a year for every $10,000 invested. The average investor in an index-tracking stock ETF pays $20, according to Morningstar.

The ETF's trading also stokes interest in First Trust's other ETFs. Its recent purchase of the First Trust Nasdaq Bank ETF made the new fund more appealing to investors who won't buy small, thinly traded products.

While funds of funds are common in the mutual-fund industry, they are relatively rare in ETFs, accounting for 191 U.S. funds with combined assets of $35 billion out of a $2.8 trillion market, according to XTF, a market-analytics firm. Some of the ETFs, especially leveraged equity funds, buy ETFs from several issuers while others invest only in in-house products.

One common use of funds of funds is target-date retirement products. BlackRock Inc., for example, offers several iShares ETFs that invest in other iShares ETFs based on risk models that range from conservative to aggressive.

The strategy can leave ETFs with high concentrations in other funds. The iShares allocation ETFs combined own as much as 68% of some of the underlying ETFs, according to FactSet. The Dorsey Wright rebalance leaves the ETF as the biggest shareholder in the First Trust bank fund.

The Dorsey Wright rebalance is notable because funds of funds rarely make such a large investment in a new product, said Mr. Rosenbluth. It raises concerns about its potential price impact on the bank ETF once its biggest investor bails out, Mr. Rosenbluth said.

Finance & Markets: First Trust's ETFs Are Unsung Force

By Asjylyn Loder

Some of the biggest action in exchange-traded funds last month took place in two little-known First Trust ETFs.

It has been one of First Trust's most successful ETFs. The average investor in an index-tracking stock ETF pays $20, according to Morningstar.

The ETF's trading also stokes interest in First Trust's other ETFs. Its recent purchase of the First Trust Nasdaq Bank ETF made the new fund more appealing to investors who won't buy small, thinly traded products.

While funds of funds are common in the mutual-fund industry, they are relatively rare in ETFs, accounting for 191 U.S. funds with combined assets of $35 billion out of a $2.8 trillion market, according to XTF, a market analytics firm. Some of the ETFs, especially leveraged equity funds, buy ETFs from several issuers while others invest only in in-house products.

One common use of funds of funds is target-date retirement products. BlackRock Inc., for example, offers several iShares ETFs that invest in other iShares ETFs based on risk models that range from conservative to aggressive.

The strategy can leave ETFs with high concentrations in other funds. The iShares allocation ETFs combined own as much as 68% of some of the underlying ETFs, according to FactSet. The Dorsey Wright rebalance leaves the ETF as the biggest shareholder in the First Trust bank fund.

The Dorsey Wright rebalance is notable because funds of funds rarely make such a large investment in a new product, said Mr. Rosenbluth. It raises concerns about its potential price impact on the bank ETF once its biggest investor bails out, said Mr. Rosenbluth.

Markets
Meet an Unsung Force in Stock Trading: First Trust's ETF of ETFs; While funds of funds are common in the mutual-fund industry, they are relatively rare in ETFs

By Asjylyn Loder 
936 words
9 April 2017
13:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Some of the biggest action in exchange-traded funds last month took place in two little-known First Trust ETFs.

The biggest trader? First Trust Advisors LP itself.

The trading was triggered by a rebalancing of the $2.5 billion First Trust Dorsey Wright Focus 5 ETF. The fund tracks a Dorsey, Wright & Associates index that analyzes recent market trends for signs of momentum, buying sectors and industries with the most substantial recent gains. It owns just five ETFs at a time, all picked from the First Trust lineup.

Last month, energy stocks dropped out of the index while the banking sector gained favor, reflecting its postelection rally. The shift prompted the Dorsey Wright ETF to sell one First Trust ETF and buy another.

As a result, assets in a First Trust ETF that buys bank stocks swelled 34-fold in March to $910.6 million, marking the six-month-old fund as one of the most successful recent launches in the ETF industry. During the month, the First Trust Energy AlphaDEX ETF, which invests in energy companies, lost almost two-thirds of its assets.

Dorsey Wright, now a unit of Nasdaq Inc., has long been a favorite among investment advisers. Some follow its strategy without using the Dorsey Wright ETF, buying and selling the underlying First Trust ETFs themselves.

First Trust created the ETF in 2014 because customers wanted the popular Focus 5 strategy packaged into one product, said Ryan Issakainen, senior vice president and ETF strategist with First Trust. Bundling the ETFs saves investors the trouble of rebalancing themselves and offers some tax benefits. First Trust also offers a second Dorsey Wright ETF that makes many of the same trades.

But analysts have long questioned the Dorsey Wright ETF for its high fees, market-roiling trades and subpar returns.

"There are significant benefits for First Trust for launching products that fit into this strategy, but some investors don't necessarily know what they're buying," said Todd Rosenbluth, director of ETF and mutual-fund research for CFRA, a consulting firm.

It has been one of First Trust's most successful ETFs. It is also one of its most expensive because First Trust charges investors both for the Dorsey Wright ETF and for the five First Trust ETFs it owns, pushing the total cost to $89 a year for every $10,000 invested. The average investor in an index-tracking stock ETF pays $20, according to Morningstar.

The ETF's trading also stokes interest in First Trust's other ETFs. Its recent purchase of the First Trust Nasdaq Bank ETF made the new fund more appealing to investors who won't buy small, thinly traded products.

While funds of funds are common in the mutual-fund industry, they are relatively rare in ETFs, accounting for 191 U.S. funds with combined assets of $35 billion out of a $2.8 trillion market, according to XTF, a market analytics firm. Some of the ETFs, especially leveraged equity funds, buy ETFs from several issuers while others invest only in in-house products.

One common use of funds of funds is target-date retirement products. BlackRock Inc., for example, offers several iShares ETFs that invest in other iShares ETFs based on risk models that range from conservative to aggressive.

The strategy can leave ETFs with highly concentrated positions in other funds. The iShares allocation ETFs combined own as much as 68% of some of the underlying ETFs, according to FactSet. The Dorsey Wright rebalance leaves the ETF as the biggest shareholder in the First Trust bank fund.

Concentrated ownership hasn't been a problem because the allocation funds don't turn over that much, and the underlying stocks in the ETFs within the fund are easily traded, said Paul Lohrey, head of U.S. iShares product development and consulting.

The Dorsey Wright rebalance is notable because funds of funds rarely make such a large investment in a new product, said Mr. Rosenbluth. It raises concerns about its potential price impact on the bank ETF once its biggest investor bails out, said Mr. Rosenbluth.

"The strategy doesn't usually hold another fund that has just come to market like this," said Mr. Issakainen. "It's an unusual case, but we're still very comfortable with it."

Mr. Issakainen said the size of the ETF is less important than the tradability of the companies the fund invests in. While the Dorsey Wright ETF only owns five other ETFs, those funds invest in more than 200 stocks.

The Dorsey Wright ETF has shed $2.1 billion since the end of 2015 as momentum strategies fell out of favor. The goal is to capture the gains of a hot sector, but sell before it hits bottom. Such funds won't buy at the lowest price or sell at the highest, but try to come close enough to beat the wider market.

Momentum systems tend to do well when there is a clear and lasting market trend, but can suffer when sentiment changes quickly, as it has in the past year.

The Dorsey Wright ETF's recent rebalance is a case in point. The fund sold its energy ETF long after its peak, denting returns. Over the past year, it would have been more profitable to buy and hold an S&P 500 index fund.

Chris Dieterich contributed to this article.

COMPANIES
Luxury retail exodus hits New York property; Landlords have been slow to adapt leasing strategies after the departure of upmarket brands from 'vanity' store fronts

By Anna Nicolaou in New York and Judith Evans in London 
1,272 words
8 April 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Manhattan's Bleecker Street has seen better days. Retail rents on the main drag of Greenwich Village had soared for five years, boosted by a frothy investment market and booming tourism as New York recovered from the Lehman crisis. But in recent months upmarket retailers, facing steeper losses to online shopping and ever pricier rents, have shut their stores.

Marc Jacobs, Jimmy Choo and Ralph Lauren helped transform Bleecker Street from a bohemian centre to a high-end shopping paradise. Their departure, along with other luxury brands, has left two or three vacant shops on each block.

"The pioneers that made that street are pulling back," says Faith Consolo, chairwoman of the retail leasing and sales division at Douglas Elliman, a real estate group. "It really casts a shadow. I haven't seen a cycle like this before."

Similar trends are emerging along the priciest shopping corridors in the US, including Robertson Boulevard in Los Angeles and the design district in Miami, as retailers pull back from "vanity" real estate.

They have been deterred partly by runaway rental growth - as much as 50 per cent in five years, according to Cushman & Wakefield, a property consultancy.

But beyond the cyclical patterns of real estate, there is a structural shift, analysts say, as online sales draw retailers' focus away from expensive store sites. While a presence on a glitzy street was once deemed a necessary marketing expense, some luxury retailers are adapting to a new kind of shopper who prefers browsing the internet.

The Amazon factor is hurting retailers' margins, says Ihor Dusaniwsky, head of research at S3 Partners, a financial analytics firm, "so they are spending more on their own ecommerce platforms rather than bricks-and-mortar divisions".

Meanwhile, although consumer confidence surveys appear positive, sales growth overall has dropped . The signs of weakness are leading some landlords to offload properties.

On New York's Fifth Avenue, the world's most expensive shopping street, vacancy rates have jumped from 10 per cent a year ago to 16 per cent, according to C&W. Rents have fallen for the first time since the recession "and the trend is not over", it warns.

Vacancy rates across the SoHo district have climbed to 18 per cent, from 12 per cent a year ago, according to Jones Lang LaSalle, an investment manager that specialises in commercial property.

Retailers' newfound caution has had a "very significant and fast" negative impact on retail property, says Chris Conlon, chief executive of Acadia Realty, a real estate investment trust.

It is not just prestigious streets that have been hit. Malls are also hurting, as chains such as Sears and Macy's shut hundreds of stores. Analysts at Green Street Advisors say "low growth is the new normal", while rents are becoming decoupled from tenants' revenue growth as sales move online.

"[Rents] are at a price point now that exceeds what retail sales can perform," says Spencer Levy, global head of research for CBRE, a real estate group. He notes that a stronger US dollar also dents sales in New York, where deep-pocketed foreigners historically flock for deals.

Investors have caught the gloomy mood. Shares in US retail Reits have tumbled almost 25 per cent since a peak last August, according to Dow Jones, compared with an 11 per cent decline across all Reits. Capital values in the private market have yet to mirror that shift, but listed groups often act as a bellwether.

Meanwhile hedge funds are building significant short positions against the sector. Short interest against international retail Reits has more than doubled to $12.5bn since November, according to S3 data. Of this, $1.5bn of short positions are held against Simon Properties, the US's largest retail Reit.

"Looking back over several years, this is the first time I have seen [short interest] at over $10bn in aggregate," says Mr Dusaniwsky.

The nervousness is also affecting the non-listed sector. Thor Equities, a private equity real estate group, is seeking to sell three Fifth Avenue properties bought between 2007 and 2011 after failing to find tenants for much of the space put loan repayments at risk.

"If you want big tenants . . . there aren't that many that want that size footprint any more," says Jedd Nero, principal at the New York office of Avison Young, a real estate broker.

Thor chiefs "did not get the numbers they wanted" and are looking to lease out the spaces, says someone familiar with their thinking.

One of the properties, a 19-storey office and retail building at 590 Fifth Avenue, has had its asking price cut from $170m last year to $150m, according to Bill Shanahan, a broker at CBRE who is selling the property. Plans to convert it into a flagship retail space failed to tempt a tenant.

Analysts and brokers say landlords have been slow to adapt their leasing strategies despite an overhang of supply, leading to "very lengthy" deal negotiations, according to C&W.

Mr Conlon says: "If you bought in SoHo or Fifth Avenue in 2015 and you underwrote that real estate to capture that kind of growth, then it's kind of like musical chairs: the music stops and you're probably disappointed today."

As rents continue to slip, some landlords and tenants have agreed to meet in the middle, says Patrick Smith, vice-chairman of JLL's retail broker, who predicts that "by the end of this year you're going to see more transactions".

However, most retailers are staying on the sidelines, says Mr Nero: "No one wants to catch a falling knife."

There are pockets of excessive lending in the market. Green Street says Reits that own malls of the less desirable "B" and "C" grades are "carrying too much debt and are ill-equipped for a sizeable decline in asset values". However, more prudent lending since the 2008 crisis should limit the potential for distress.

Meanwhile the structural shift towards ecommerce looks likely to have some way to run. Green Street estimates that about 20 per cent of sales for "mall-like products" such as clothing have shifted online - and forecasts that the proportion will rise above 30 per cent in the next five years.

Other prime retail locations may also have to confront this shift. Rents in Hong Kong's Causeway Bay are weakening and on the Champs-lyses in Paris they have been flat over the past year, according to C&W - although downturns in tourism were bigger factors than online sales.

In New York it is difficult to gauge how long the correction will last because there have been few deals to draw upon, according to Mr Conlon. "We're in the middle of it," he says.

For spaces in SoHo that were renting for $1,000 a square foot at the market peak, rents have dropped as much as 20 per cent. "Retailers are saying: I'm not sure what I should pay, so I'm going to wait," says Mr Conlon.

He says Acadia is preparing to buy as markets become distressed.

Avison Young agrees. "You're seeing a seismic shift in retail, and you'll continue to see consolidation as tenants that can't figure it out go out of business," says Mr Nero.

"It's not the way it was in the past 100 years, where you put a product on the shelf and everyone comes in."

Tracy's Take: Glass-Steagall Where Are the Votes?

Gary Cohn Backs Breaking Up Big Banks

BOE's Carney Says Brexit Will Test Commitment to Globalization

Fed's Kashkari Rebuts Dimon on  Too Big to Fail'

Two Former Barclays Employees Acquitted in Libor Retrial

Glass-Steagall Where Are the Votes?

Last summer, supporters of the Depression-era Glass-Steagall law got a boost when both the Republican and Democratic party platforms endorsed a return of the policy. The biggest roadblock: Hillary Clinton, the Democratic nominee and favorite to win the presidency. Her support was "https://www.wsj.com/articles/clinton-staffers-wrestled-with-glass-steagall-position-wikileaks-emails-show-1476137650"; she didn't include it in her Wall Street proposals.

Mrs. Clinton lost, and now President Donald Trump's administration keeps saying it wants to work with lawmakers on a new version of the law, which forced the separation of traditional banking from investment banking before its repeal in 1999. First, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-treasury-nominee-mnuchin-backs-21st-century-glass-steagall-for-banks-1484857155". Then it was White House "https://www.wsj.com/articles/fdic-official-details-idea-for-a-new-glass-steagall-law-1489429800". And this week, Gary Cohn , the White House National Economic Council director, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/gary-cohn-backs-breaking-up-big-banks-1491491219".

One key source of support is missing: congressional Republicans. If they start to jump on board, banks should look out.

Mr. Cohn's statement was significant because as a former Goldman Sachs Group Inc . executive, he is perceived to be a firewall against populist policies. "With him supporting Glass-Steagall's restoration, there is no one in the [Trump] inner circle left to fight it," said Cowen & Co. analyst Jaret Seiberg.

For now, it doesn't appear that Mr. Cohn or anyone else on Team Trump is fighting for a return to Glass-Steagall. His comments Wednesday, in a private meeting with members of the Senate Banking Committee, came in response to a question from Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.). Most of the discussion focused on tax issues, according to people who were there.

The Trump administration hasn't articulated any Glass-Steagall-like proposal, and has given conflicting signals about what it would back. People who heard Mr. Cohn's remarks said he voiced support for a simple separation of the two businesses. Yet Mr. Mnuchin said in January that "separating out banks and investment banks" "https://www.wsj.com/articles/under-trump-u-s-banks-could-get-a-british-makeover-1485345603", suggesting he wanted some less draconian version of the policy.

Congress would need to sort out the details, and there is little evidence that Republicans in power on Capitol Hill want to spend their time doing so. House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas) not someone who shies away from supporting controversial policies is advocating a different idea for cracking down on big banks, focusing on higher capital requirements. Senate Banking Committee Chairman Mike Crapo (R., Idaho) seems more eager to tackle housing-finance policy than to pick a fight with the nation's largest banks.

Glass-Steagall boosters say Messrs. Hensarling and Crapo could win more support for their ideas by adding the bank-breakup policy to a legislative package. But do those votes exist?

Ms. Warren's 21st Century Glass-Steagall Act, reintroduced on Thursday, had 10 Senate co-sponsors last Congress, with Sen. John McCain (R., Ariz.) the lone Republican. A similar dynamic was seen in the House, where a comparable bill from Rep. Michael Capuano (D., Mass.) had two Republicans among its nine sponsors.

There used to be little incentive for Ms. Warren, Mr. Capuano or their co-sponsors to beat down lawmakers' doors gathering support for their idea. After all, the Obama administration wasn't interested in making it reality.

Team Trump's consistent encouragement has changed that dynamic. Now it is up to Glass-Steagall proponents to translate that change into votes.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Gary Cohn Backs Breaking Up Big Banks

By Ryan Tracy and Emily Glazer

WASHINGTON The former Goldman Sachs Group Inc . executive who is the top White House economic adviser told lawmakers he could support legislation breaking up the largest U.S. banks, according to people familiar with the matter, a development that bolsters congressional efforts to reinstate the Depression-era Glass-Steagall law.

The comments by Gary Cohn , director of the White House National Economic Council, came in a private meeting with lawmakers on the Senate Banking Committee Wednesday, these people said. His remarks were reported earlier by Bloomberg.

Mr. Cohn was asked by Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.) whether the administration planned to carry out a promise included in the Republican 2016 platform and made by the Trump campaign to restore the law separating traditional commercial banking from Wall Street investment banking. The law was repealed in 1999.

Mr. Cohn expressed an openness to working with Ms. Warren on the issue, and said he could support a simple policy completely separating the two businesses, these people said. There are various ideas for restoring some form of Glass-Steagall, running the gamut from splitting apart firms completely to separating their various operations under an umbrella holding company.

A White House spokeswoman said Mr. Cohn "was simply discussing the president's previously stated position," adding, "The president spoke to the need for a simplification of the banking system on the campaign trail, what he called a  21st century Glass-Steagall,' to make it easier for businesses to grow and create jobs in their communities."

Mr. Cohn's comments are consistent with other statements by Trump administration officials. But it isn't clear how much support there is for the idea among Republicans more broadly. Ms. Warren on Thursday reintroduced a bill called the 21st Century Glass-Steagall Act. Three other senators joined her, including one Republican, Sen. John McCain of Arizona. In the last Congress, Mr. McCain was the only Republican to sponsor the bill.

Treasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin has also said the administration is open to implementing some version of President Donald Trump's campaign promise. But in his Jan. 17 confirmation hearing, he expressed concern that "separating out banks and investment banks right now under Glass-Steagall would have very big implications to the liquidity in the capital markets and banks being able to perform necessary lending."

While the Trump team has endorsed the Glass-Steagall idea, it hasn't come forward with its own proposal. Meanwhile, Treasury officials are meeting with financial-industry officials to discuss ways to roll back rules adopted under the Obama administration. They are due to make recommendations to the White House in early June.

Some observers said they didn't view Mr. Cohn's comments as a threat.

"We continue to believe that a return of some form of Glass-Steagall remains more of a headline risk rather than a real policy risk and we think the odds are against the reinstatement of the law," Brian Gardner, an analyst with Keefe, Bruyette & Woods, said in a note to clients Thursday.

"I don't think we're concerned," said one banker at a large firm. The bank is "pleased with the direction" of Mr. Trump's administration and its executives aren't racing to change strategy or make a big new lobbying push. "It's the same day as it was yesterday," this person said. "It's not a time to go crazy."

Tim Pawlenty, president of the Financial Services Roundtable trade group, said Thursday: "Large financial institutions play a role in the American economy other institutions are not able to fill. We are working with Congress and the administration on a common-sense approach to financial regulation modernization and look forward to more progress."

Mr. Cohn's comments will give Ms. Warren and other supporters of the idea more momentum. He was until recently the No. 2 executive at Goldman Sachs , the Wall Street investment bank that took on more traditional banking businesses and the additional regulation that comes with them after converting to a bank holding company to receive government assistance during the 2008 financial crisis.

Mr. Cohn "was the most likely obstacle within the Trump White House" to restoring Glass-Steagall, said Jaret Seiberg, an analyst with Cowen & Co., in a note to clients. "With him supporting Glass-Steagall's restoration, there is no one in the inner circle left to fight it."

Mr. Seiberg said banks may be underestimating the threat: "At some point the market is going to have to accept that the Trump administration is serious about restoring the Glass-Steagall separation between commercial and investment banking."

Glass-Steagall was adopted in the 1930s as a way to keep securities businesses separate from taxpayer-insured banks. The separation between lending and investment banking slowly eroded in the latter part of the 20th century, as banks won regulatory exceptions to diversify their businesses.

Since Congress repealed the law in 1999, liberals have pushed for reinstating it, calling such a move a simple way to make the economy more stable by removing a taxpayer backstop from risky activities. Proponents of bringing back the law also say it would diminish the size and political influence of large Wall Street banks.

The 2010 Dodd-Frank regulatory-overhaul law took a half-step toward Glass-Steagall when it mandated the Volcker rule, which bars banks from certain activities unless they are trading on behalf of their customers. Many banks have since closed so-called proprietary trading desks.

The Glass-Steagall idea hasn't gained broad support yet, even among Democrats. Former Federal Reserve Gov. Daniel Tarullo , the Fed regulatory guru who stepped down Wednesday, was asked about Glass-Steagall earlier this week. He pointed out that in 2008, Bear Stearns and Lehman Brothers  two investment banks without traditional lending businesses caused significant financial stress.

"Just by separating things doesn't mean people stay out of trouble," he said, adding that there would be costs to forcing banks to separate and lose the potential business advantages of combining their operations. "If you are going to have those costs and still have financial stability problems &then maybe we are not getting much after all."

BOE's Carney Says Brexit Will Test Commitment to Globalization

By Jason Douglas

LONDON The U.K.'s exit from the European Union represents "a litmus test" for major economies' commitment to a robust, global financial system, the governor of the Bank of England said Friday, as the central bank ordered foreign lenders based in London to present comprehensive contingency plans that cover a messy Brexit divorce.

In a speech in London, Mark Carney said the global financial system stands at "a fork in the road," where one path leads to continued cooperation between regulators across borders and the free flow of capital world-wide, while another leads to greater protectionism, a fragmenting of the financial system and higher costs for borrowers.

"On one path, we can build a more effective, resilient system on the new pillars of responsible financial globalization. On the other, countries could turn inward and reduce reliance on each other's financial systems," Mr. Carney said during a speech at the London offices of Thomson Reuters Corp ., according to a text of his remarks published by the central bank.

"The net result would be less reliable and more expensive financing for households and businesses, and very likely lower growth and higher risks in all our economies."

Mr. Carney said coming Brexit talks between the U.K. and the EU will test leaders' resolve to maintain an open and global financial system. London is "Europe's investment banker," Mr. Carney said, and the two sides in the talks are "ideally placed" to carve out a deal that keeps capital flowing seamlessly around Europe. They already share common standards and regulators work closely together, he said.

A far-reaching deal between the U.K. and the EU on financial services would offer other countries a template to emulate in bilateral free-trade deals, he added. "Such an approach could be applied more broadly to the immense benefit of the global economy," Mr. Carney said.

His remarks underscore the high stakes for the U.K. in maintaining London's pre-eminent role in Europe's financial system after it formally leaves the EU, expected in early 2019. U.K. Prime Minister Theresa May has said she wants financial services included in a "new, comprehensive bold and ambitious" free-trade accord between London and Brussels. But European officials have signaled they want to reduce the EU's reliance on London's capital markets after Brexit, and some banks are already planning to shift some operations to other member states.

Mr. Carney said the BOE has written to London-based banks, including those headquartered in the U.S., to demand details of their contingency planning surrounding Brexit.

In a letter to bank executives, BOE deputy governor for prudential regulation Sam Woods said that planning has been "uneven" across bank boardrooms and hasn't sufficiently addressed the risk that Brexit talks collapse and the U.K. exits the EU with no deal.

Banks have until July 14 to update the BOE on their plans, the central bank said.

WSJ Pro: Fed's Kashkari Rebuts Dimon on  Too Big to Fail'

By Gabriel T. Rubin

The Federal Reserve 's Neel Kashkari pushed back against claims by J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. chief James Dimon that the problem of "too big to fail" banks had been solved, arguing that the issue is "alive and well" and that large banks still hold too little capital to absorb losses during a crisis.

Mr. Kashkari, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis, made the argument in a blog post Thursday. His rebuttal came two days after Mr. Dimon's annual shareholder letter, in which the chairman of the U.S.'s largest bank by market capitalization argued that "it was clear that the banks have too much capital."

Mr. Kashkari, who since assuming his position at the Minneapolis Fed last year has made solving "too big to fail" a signature focus, dismissed Mr. Dimon's contention that current capital standards are restraining lending and economic growth.

"The truth is that borrowing costs for homeowners and businesses are near record lows," he wrote.

Mr. Kashkari is an advocate of raising equity capital requirements substantially to avoid the possibility of a taxpayer-financed bailout for a large financial institution in the event of a severe financial crisis.

He did agree with Mr. Dimon, however, that financial regulations should be made less complex, a goal he believes could be achieved with a simpler, higher capital threshold.

Two Former Barclays Employees Acquitted in Libor Retrial

By Philip Georgiadis

Two former Barclays PLC employees have been acquitted of conspiracy to defraud in relation to the manipulation of Libor interest rates after a retrial in London.

The acquittals conclude the U.K. Serious Fraud Office 's final outstanding trial over Libor the London interbank offered rate although the agency said its investigation is continuing.

Ryan Michael Reich was acquitted Wednesday and Stylianos Contogoulas was acquitted Thursday morning, a spokeswoman for the SFO confirmed.

The monthlong retrial of the two traders came after a jury in July last year failed to reach a verdict on the two men, while convicting three other former Barclays employees in connection with the investigation.

Mr. Reich said in a statement that he had acted in accordance with the rules at the time. "I am saddened that it has taken so long to expose the case against me, a junior trader just doing my job over a decade ago, as being totally without foundation," Mr. Reich said.

Mr. Contogoulas's legal team didn't immediately respond to a request for comment. Barclays declined to comment.

British citizens Jonathan Mathew and Jay Merchant, as well as American Alex Pabon, were all convicted of conspiracy to defraud in last year's trial. Messrs. Merchant and Pabon were both Libor traders, while Mr. Mathew was a submitter.

Peter Johnson, a senior submitter and head U.S. dollar cash trader at Barclays, pleaded guilty to conspiracy to defraud in October 2014. Tom Hayes, a former bank trader at both UBS Group AG and Citigroup Inc ., was found guilty in August 2015 of rigging Libor and sentenced to 14 years in prison, though that was later reduced to 11 years.

The SFO has brought charges against 19 individuals in relation to Libor and the separate Euribor rate. Of those, eight have now been acquitted, four have been convicted and one pleaded guilty.

Six individuals from Deutsche Bank and Barclays have been charged and will stand trial over conspiracy to defraud in relation to Euribor.

The Wall Street Journal first drew attention nine years ago to industry concerns around how Libor was being set, sparking a global probe that has resulted in multiple convictions and billions of dollars in penalties paid by banks. Barclays paid $450 million to U.S. and U.K. authorities in 2012 and admitted that traders and managers had sought to rig the rate.

Data Clash Heats Up Between Banks and New York Stock Exchange

By Alexander Osipovich

Some of Wall Street's biggest firms are balking at an attempt by the New York Stock Exchange to assert greater control over market data a behind-the-scenes spat that highlights the growing importance of Big Data in today's financial markets.

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co ., Citigroup Inc ., Goldman Sachs Group Inc . and electronic traders Virtu Financial Inc. and KCG Holdings Inc . are among the firms concerned about the exchange's move, people familiar with the situation said. Virtu confirmed its opposition to NYSE's move, while none of the others would speak on the record.

The dispute centers on a new contract called the NYSE Master User Agreement, which the exchange operator is requiring firms to sign to keep trading on its markets.

At the heart of the dispute is legal language about who owns the data that brokers submit to the exchange when they buy and sell stocks. The contract implies that NYSE owns the data. Brokers and big trading firms say the data are rightfully theirs.

Exchanges aggregate such data and sell it in the form of high-speed feeds often to the same firms whose trading activity generated the data in the first place. Such data feeds have become a growing source of revenue for exchanges in recent years, prompting complaints from Wall Street firms that they are being overcharged.

"This dispute demonstrates the fundamental point market participants have been making about the exchange's unchecked control over market data and its pricing," Virtu Chief Executive Doug Cifu said in an email.

"You'd be hard pressed to find another industry where a government-licensed entity can take your intellectual property and sell it back to you at an arbitrarily high markup," Mr. Cifu said.

The master user agreement, a seven-page document available on NYSE's website, differs from similar contracts from the Big Board's competitors, lawyers say.

"It appears that NYSE could be going further than other exchanges," said David Franasiak, a lawyer who reviewed the agreement for The Wall Street Journal. Mr. Franasiak is a principal at Williams & Jensen PLLC, a law and lobbying firm that has worked for brokerage firms, but he isn't involved in the current dispute.

Dozens of other firms have already signed the agreement, a person close to NYSE said.

The clash began early this year when NYSE released an update to the contract, replacing a previous version that brokers had found acceptable and surprising them with the new language, according to one of the people familiar with the dispute.

Upset brokers reached out to one another and held conference calls, including as recently as March 30, people briefed on the discussions said.One person said NYSE was set to take part in the call last week but backed out. The person close to NYSE said the exchange has been engaged in ongoing dialogue with the firms.

Another call involved the Securities and Exchange Commission , which is monitoring the situation and remaining neutral, said a person familiar with the matter.

NYSE has linked the agreement to a technology overhaul called Pillar. The market operator has told firms they must sign the agreement to use new, faster networking hardware being introduced as part of the Pillar rollout.

The deadline to sign it was repeatedly pushed back, according to notices emailed to traders and posted on NYSE's website.

The new hardware was initially set to go live on NYSE's Arca marketplace on Feb. 13, but that was delayed to March 13 and then March 27. Most recently, a March 20 glitch on Arca prompted another delay. NYSE has yet to announce the new date.

Controversy over the cost of market data has been brewing for years. Brokers complain that exchanges like NYSE, which was a nonprofit until 2006, have become more aggressive about raising data fees since they turned into for-profit corporations.

NYSE's owner, Intercontinental Exchange Inc . or ICE , has beefed up its data business since completing its acquisition of the Big Board in 2013. Last year, 44% of ICE 's net revenues came from market data sales and related fees, up from 9% in 2011. Much of that increase is due to acquisitions of data companies, but some is due to price increases and new data products, according to the company's SEC filings.

Stock exchanges are "exploiting their monopoly over market data," the Securities Industry and Financial Markets Association , a lobbying group for banks, brokers and asset managers, said in a filing submitted to the SEC last year as part of a long-running legal battle with NYSE and Nasdaq Inc . over fee increases.

ICE has said in the past that its data fees are reasonable, and exchanges reject claims that they are abusing their market power.

In many of the user agreements for U.S. exchanges, firms give up some rights to market data in order to be allowed to trade on the exchange. There is typically a provision in which users grant the exchange a license to take their raw trading data and resell it in the form of aggregated data products, provided that the identities of the people or firms behind the trades aren't revealed.

NYSE competitors Nasdaq , Bats and IEX Group Inc . all have such clauses in their user agreements. They also contain boilerplate language stating that the user "retains all ownership and other rights" to the data they submit to the exchange.

But the NYSE master user agreement contains no such provision. Instead, it states that market data on the exchange is the property of NYSE. It also lacks a licensing provision in which users grant the exchange the right to use their data even though past NYSE user agreements have contained such language.

The contract "runs counter to the position that a lot of firms and Sifma have taken with respect to who owns market data," said Eric Noll, chief executive of Convergex, a New York-based brokerage. Mr. Noll said he was aware of the dispute over the master user agreement, although Convergex isn't among the firms that have been fighting it.

Squabbling over market data costs appears unlikely to cease even if the fight over NYSE's contract cools down. "It's a continuing, ongoing feud," Mr. Noll said.

 Dave Michaels contributed to this article.

Lehman Floats RMBS Settlement Worth at Least $1 Billion

By Jonathan Randles

Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc . is floating a settlement that would pay at least $1 billion to institutional investors holding debt tied to soured mortgage loans, as the failed investment bank looks to clear one of the last major hurdles in winding down its affairs more than eight years after filing for bankruptcy.

The proposed settlement, which must be approved by a judge, provides Lehman with a path to resolving a dispute that has dragged on for years over how much it owes for faulty residential mortgages it bundled into securities and sold to investors. A bankruptcy judge will consider approving the settlement in July, according to papers filed Thursday.

The proposed deal would establish the floor of $2.416 billion for the mortgage claims, which under Lehman's plan have recovered more than 39 cents on the dollar.

In a letter sent to trustees, Robert Madden , a Gibbs & Bruns LLP lawyer who represents the financial institutions supporting the deal, said there was no reason the other trustees "could or should refuse this guarantee of a certain recovery of more than a $1 billion in cash, with the opportunity to obtain more."

A bankruptcy judge will determine the final value at a so-called estimation hearing. A handful of financial institutions representing mortgage-backed securitization trusts have argued the value of the claims is much higher and wanted Lehman to set aside $12.14 billion cover them.

The proposed settlement relates to 244 residential mortgage-backed securities trusts issued by Lehman. The trustees allege Lehman misrepresented the quality of the underlying loans.

Trustees have until June 1 to accept the settlement offer, according to court documents. Lawyers representing the trustees didn't respond to a message Thursday seeking comment.

More than a dozen institutional investors holding a share of the residential mortgage debt are backing the deal, court papers say. Supporters include BlackRock Financial Management Inc ., Goldman Sachs Asset Management LP and Pacific Investment Management Co. LLC .

Mr. Madden said the proposed settlement provides a reasonable framework for resolving a dispute that he said has been uniquely challenging and time-consuming because of the size of Lehman's bankruptcy.

The settlement is good for investors because it only sets a floor on the claims and doesn't cap the amount trustees would be able to seek, Mr. Madden said. The underlying claims are based on tens of thousands of individual mortgage loans, he said.

"It really gives the trustee a shot to swing for the fences and, importantly, get this thing over with," Mr. Madden said.

Lawyers for Lehman are asking a bankruptcy judge in New York to set an October trial to determine the value of the claims. Investors would be in line to get a percentage of what the judge determines the final claims are worth based on Lehman's approved chapter 11 repayment plan.

Lehman, once the nation's fourth-largest investment bank, collapsed into the largest bankruptcy ever in September 2008 and its U.S. brokerage business was quickly sold off to Barclays PLC .

A team of bankruptcy professionals under the direction of turnaround firm Alvarez & Marsal managed Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc .'s assets until the holding company's exit from chapter 11 in 2012, when a reorganized company emerged, overseen by a new board.

The remains of the holding company is expected to exist in some form for several more years as it unwinds its remaining holdings.

 Patrick Fitzgerald contributed to this article.

Brazil Financial Markets Regulator Suspends IPO Over Irregularities

By Luciana Magalhaes

SO PAULO Brazil's market regulator on Thursday suspended the initial public offering of Azul Linhas Areas Brasileiras SA, alleging the airline broke rules regarding the release of information.

Azul, Brazil's third-largest airline by passengers, aimed to raise more than $500 million in the IPO in Brazil and in the U.S., which was set to be priced Thursday night.

But the process was suspended after the regulator, known as the CVM, said in a note that irregular advertising material was used during presentations to investors and confidential information related to demand and pricing of shares was released to the media.

The suspension will last for up to 30 days if the "irregularities" are corrected, the regulator said. Otherwise the sale won't be allowed to proceed. The CVM didn't immediately explain what measures would be needed to permit the sale to go forward.

Azul, created in 2008 by businessman David Neeleman, who also founded U.S. discount carrier JetBlue Airways Corp ., declined to comment on the suspension of its IPO.

The budget airline already had seen three previous efforts to sell shares to the public sink due to economic and political problems in Brazil since 2013.

It expected to sell as many as 72 million preferred shares, for up to 23 reais ($7.37) each in Brazil and as much $21.81 a unit in the U.S., this week.

The company had applied for the shares to trade on the B3 stock exchange in Brazil, which recently changed its name from BM&FBovespa, under the symbol "AZUL4" and on the New York Stock Exchange under the symbol "AZUL."

WSJ Pro: Iceland's Exit from Capital Controls Brings New Risks, Says Central Bank

By Nina Adam

Iceland's central bank said Thursday that the removal of capital controls on households and business in mid-March proceeded "smoothly," but warned the new environment could bring risks.

Icelandic banks' easier access to foreign credit markets could tempt them to grant loans in a foreign currency to residents who are unprotected against exchange rate swings, the central bank warned.

"If such loans are granted on a large scale, it could undermine the stability of the financial system," the bank said in its biannual financial stability report. It urged parliament to pass legislation that restricted such behavior.

The central bank said economics conditions in Iceland were "favorable," but house prices appeared to have risen faster than wages in the last few months.

Treasury Lifts Sanctions On Two Who Left Blacklisted Company

By Samuel Rubenfeld

The U.S. Treasury Department removed two people on Thursday from its sanctions list. The reason: They don't have any affiliation with a company under sanctions.

Treasury removed Donna MacBain, from the U.K., and Siobhan Hanrahan, from Ireland, from the sanctions list, it said in a notice posted to its website. They were targeted in September 2016 as part of action against Canadian payment processor PacNet Group, which Treasury said at the time is a third-party money launderer on behalf of fraud schemes targeting the elderly and the vulnerable.

PacNet Services Ltd., which serves as a holding company for various PacNet entities, said on its homepage that it categorically rejects the allegation.

"We will vigorously defend ourselves against these unproven allegations which we only learned about on Sept. 22, 2016, through media reports," the company said in the undated message, adding that it is "working to address" the U.S. blacklisting.

A Treasury spokesman said in an email on Thursday that both Ms. MacBain and Ms. Hanrahan "are no longer employed" by PacNet, and neither serves as a corporate officer for any PacNet entity. Moreover, the spokesman said, neither had any assets or property to freeze at the time of their designation.

The spokesman said they "no longer meet the criteria" for sanctions. Treasury removed sanctions in February from two other individuals who had been targeted over links to PacNet.

Separately on Thursday, the U.S. State Department named Abu Anas al-Ghandour as a terrorist, placing sanctions on him. The State Department said he is a military commander for Hamas who leads a brigade for the group in Gaza, and has been involved in many terrorist operations.

Analysis: Not a Dot-Com Bubble, Not 2007, but a Nasty Mix of Both

By James Mackintosh

When the market value of loss-making Tesla Inc . passed that of Ford Motor Co . this week, it was a perfect illustration of investors' love affair with disruptive technologies. Investors seem to have forgotten another valuation comparison they should think about more often: In October 2008, the market value of Mattel Inc ., maker of Matchbox toy cars, passed heavily indebted Ford.

Old hands in the markets like to say that one year in seven investors worry about balance sheets, rather than profit. It has been eight years since that last applied, but as the Federal Reserve starts to discuss running down its own balance sheet, it's a good time to think about corporate indebtedness again.

Companies have been loading up on debt based on two assumptions, shared by investors: that economic growth will be slow, and it will be steady. This is the perfect environment for leverage, as low growth keeps interest rates low relative to inflation, while the expectation of steady growth means few worry about a bad year interrupting repayments.

The danger is that either assumption proves wrong, and the focus shifts back from profit to balance sheet. Such a shift could be ugly, because there is so much more debt than usual being piled up by companies outside the finance sector: The ratio of debt to operating cash flow of the highest-quality U.S. companies is just slightly down from a record reached last year, Morgan Stanley calculates. Given leverage has in the past jumped in recessions, this is particularly unusual.

Rather than expanding overall profit, companies have been boosting the return to shareholders by replacing equity with debt a zero-sum transfer which cannot be repeated indefinitely.

"You've got high returns on equity in the U.S., but they're supported by radically higher levels of leverage so to some extent they're a mirage," says Andrew Lapthorne, head of quantitative equity research at Socit Gnrale .

Consider the bubbles of 2000 and 2007. In 2000, share prices were very (very!) high but debt was relatively expensive. The assumption was that new technology would lead to rapid earnings growth, but it would be volatile and uncertain, so needed to be financed with equity.

The rash of initial public offerings was a natural supply response to the demand for shares, and established companies took advantage too, financing takeovers far more than usual with new equity. The bust that followed was painful, but share prices bore the brunt of the adjustment and the recession was so shallow it barely shows up in the economic data.

Tesla is in some ways a repeat of the dot-com era. A sky-high valuation is justified by the hope that an untested product mass-market electric cars will generate big profits.

In 2007, debt was cheap compared with equity, so companies chose to issue debt to buy back shares, and to finance the takeover boom with loans. Worse, the finance bubble boosted earnings to unsustainable levels and left the banks vulnerable. When the bust came, the assumption that earnings growth was sustainable proved wrong, and the sudden focus on the balance sheet forced companies to replace debt with equity.

Ford was doubly caught out by the 2008 crash, with earnings plunging and debt too high (although unlike rivals General Motors and Chrysler, it didn't go bankrupt). Ford has learned a lesson, with more cash than debt on its automotive balance sheet today, although its finance arm, a separate source of trouble amid the banking collapse, is big again.

Today's market is different from both 2000 and 2007. Equities are expensive, but still look relatively cheaper compared with debt on widely used models which compare bond yields to the "earnings yield," forecast earnings as a percentage of share price. CFOs like to issue whatever investors will pay the most for, and at the moment that is debt, so companies have been selling debt to buy back shares.

If and when there is a nasty surprise from the economy or the Fed, highly valued shares in companies with a lot of debt and a mistaken assumption of solid earnings will be the most vulnerable.

The market isn't yet repeating the 2000 equity bubble or the 2007 debt bubble, but it has some of the worst features of both. If investors turn out to be mistaken in thinking the economic cycle won't turn down for years yet, it's going to hurt.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

 Glass-Steagall-Like' May Not Be Glass-Steagall

It's possible that a "Glass-Steagall-like change" could increase systemic risk, create unregulated too-big-to-fail firms and put taxpayers on the hook for future bailouts, warns Dennis Kelleher, CEO of Better Markets, a nonprofit organization advocating for financial-industry reform. The White House "could recreate the dangerously un- or under-regulated shadow banking system that existed before the [2008] crisis," which "could incentivize unacceptable risk-taking and moral hazard," Kelleher says. He made his comments in response to reports top White House economic adviser Gary Cohn says he could support legislation for breaking up the largest US banks. Most troubling is the "potential benefit that would be uniquely enjoyed" by Goldman Sachs (GS ), Cohn's former firm, Kelleher says. Glass-Steagall, a law which separated traditional commercial banking from Wall Street investment banking, was repealed in 1999. (Daisy.Maxey@wsj.com; @DaisyMaxey)

House Passes Bill to Promote Online Investing

House overwhelmingly passes legislation that provides some regulatory relief for private investors to join venture capital funds in order to promote online capital raising for small businesses. The legislation amends a 77-year-old investment law so more investors can join a fund without having to register with the SEC. It lifts the threshold required for a fund to register with the SEC from 100 investors to 250 investors. "Excessive regulations give the biggest companies a competitive advantage but undermine entrepreneurs, small business owners, and the investors that back them with capital," House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R, Texas) said in a press release. The bill introduced by Rep. Patrick McHenry (R, NC) is among several proposals meant to spur financial technology, called the "innovation initiative," that McHenry is likely to reintroduce to Congress soon. (rachel.witkowski@wsj.com; @RachelWitkowski)

A Fresh Wrinkle in the Case Of Jeffrey Lacker

Documents obtained by the activists associated with the Fed Up campaign are raising questions about the timeline as to when the Richmond Fed found out about its former president's role in a leak investigation. The group obtained letters from all of the regional banks from late 2015 dealing with the renomination process regional bank presidents were undergoing at the time. Only the Richmond Fed sent a supplemental letter to the Board of Governors, in a letter dated Jan 7, 2016. The content of the letter is redacted but it stands out because no other bank submitted such a document. Fed Up says this follow up letter suggests "the circumstances regarding Lacker's reappointment were unique, or that new information had come to light." (michael.derby@wsj.com)

CarMax : Credit Tightening Amid Subprime Delinquencies

Used-car seller CarMax (KMX) is seeing a decline in sales financed by third-party finance providers, to whom KMX pays a fee. A "credit tightening" by those providers, lower credit application volume "from customers at the lower end of the credit spectrum" and delayed federal income tax refunds "all contributed to the decline in Tier 3 mix," KMX says. This comes as suprime auto lending has gone sour recently, with the level of such auto loans slipping into delinquency climbing to the highest level since 2010. KMX also reports a $15.4M increase in its provision for loan losses. KMX, which reported better-than-expected profit and revenue, falls 1.5% to $55.85. (joshua.jamerson@wsj.com; @joshjame)

Wells Fargo Overhauls Its Credit-Card-Processing Business

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/wells-fargos-aggressive-sales-tactics-hit-small-firms-1491384600?mod=djemFinancialRegulationPro&tpl=fr" involves helping businesses handle debit- and credit-card transactions for customers, might seem like a mundane business. But small-business owners and industry participants say high-pressure sales tactics are common. They add that complex pricing structures and costly early-termination penalties that can lock customers into contracts are also prevalent.

Merchant processors collected about $18.15 billion in debit- and credit-card fees from merchants in 2016, according to the Nilson Report, a newsletter tracking the payments industry. Banks such as Wells Fargo , Bank of America Corp . and J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . are among the biggest players.

Dodd-Frank's Effect on Middle-Class Credit

The Dodd-Frank Act caused a "substantial redistribution" in credit from middle-class to wealthy households by increasing the cost of originating mortgages, find Francesco D'Acunto and Alberto G. Rossi in a "http://conference.nber.org/confer/2017/CFs17/D'Acunto_Rossi.pdf". "Lenders reduced credit to middle-class households by 15%, and increased credit to wealthy households by 21%" between 2010 and 2014, they find. Credit to low-income households was unaffected, which the authors attribute to protections under the Fair Lending Act. The redistribution of credit was particularly high in counties that were more exposed to large lenders.

Republicans Target Retirement Savings

Recent moves by Republican lawmakers and the Trump administration will make it harder for Americans to save for a secure retirement, "https://www.nytimes.com/2017/04/06/opinion/rising-risks-for-retirement-savers.html?emc=edit_th_20170406&nl=todaysheadlines&nlid=46659547&_r=1". The House and Senate recently voted to reverse an Obama administration regulation that would allow cities and counties to organize retirement savings plans for private-sector workers without employer plans, and the Trump administration is delaying the effective date of the Labor Department's fiduciary standard rule. "What congressional Republicans and Mr. Trump are doing is looking for ways to line Wall Street's pockets with the retirement savings of working people," the board writes.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/richmond-fed-board-says-it-was-unaware-of-extent-of-lacker-probe-1491520757" of the Federal Reserve Bank of Richmond said Thursday a lawyer for then-bank President Jeffrey Lacker left out some details when briefing them in late 2015 on his involvement in a federal investigation. The board said in a statement it didn't learn these details until Mr. Lacker's resignation letter Tuesday.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/deutsche-bank-close-to-successful-8-5-billion-share-sale-1491480060?tesla=y" closing in on a successful $8.5 billion share sale Thursday, the final day for investors to opt in to a capital-raising long seen as necessary to put the German lender on firm financial footing, according to people familiar with the matter.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/startup-trov-finds-new-funding-for-insurance-on-demand-1491484119" aiming to bring on-demand services to property-casualty insurance, says it raised $45 million to expand to markets such as the U.S. and Japan.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/citadel-securities-names-jamil-nazarali-senior-adviser-to-ceo-peng-zhao-1491518464" of execution services, Jamil Nazarali , is switching to a new role, the latest reshuffle at the electronic market-making firm that handles more than a third of U.S. retail equities orders.

"http://blogs.wsj.com/cio/2017/04/06/blackrock-using-symphony-communications-platform-to-chat-with-other-firms/", the world's largest money manager, has begun using a messaging platform from Symphony Communication Services LLC to communicate with other firms, signaling continued interest in the chat system after more than a year of internal use.
