Transcript: Q&A With Fed's Patrick Harker in Philadelphia; Philadelphia Fed president talks about fintech, financial regulation and community banking

4,246 words
4 April 2017
18:15
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia President Patrick Harker took audience questions on Monday, April 3, 2017 in Philadelphia, Pa., following "https://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-harker-still-sees-three-rate-increases-in-2017-as-appropriate-1491246399". He discussed fintech, financial regulation, community banking and other topics. Here is a transcript of the exchange, lightly edited for length and clarity.

Q: The assumption that you've been making is that fintech is coming from a lot of startup companies, right? That may be true or not, but I wanted to ask you to clarify that point. What about if a Google or a, you know, social media company like Facebook wanting to enter (inaudible)? Could that be how would that work in conjunction with the rest of the banking system?

PATRICK HARKER: Yeah, I mean, there are a lot of different players. I don't think it's necessarily just startups. The banks themselves are making some investment in this space. I mean, you do have other players Apple, Google, and others that are moving into this space. But, again, it's a big space, right? It's a very big space, and there's lots of functions across the spectrum.

If you think of financial services, I mean, there's directly you know, Venmo. How many use Venmo, right? So you've got that, directly consumer-facing. But then you have merchant banking and correspondent banking, and you've got the back end of all the services provided to corporations. So it's where in that space you'll find most of the innovation. Right now it tends to happen more on the payments-system side as opposed to these back-office kind of rails that are running.

There we are the Federal Reserve has initiated a conversation over the last several years with the industry to develop some thinking about what is the new payments system in America. And so we've started two major work streams. And this is really being headed by one of my colleagues, the president of the Kansas City Fed, along with a fellow who left the Chicago Fed as first vice president and is now running this full-time. And it's a conversation that involves large firms, as you mentioned; mid-sized firms; and then small firms.

And it involves banks at all levels, too, because one of the challenges is we think about banks you probably think about banks as Chase, Citi. But if I'm sitting out in the bank out in Amish Country that's in our district, the Bank of Bird-in-Hand, they're serving the Amish community  very small, but critically important to that community. So we can't think of it as just the large banks, but it's really all the banking sector because we need to make sure those guys are protected as well, right, in this evolution.

That conversation we're having involves two major work streams. One is on faster payments, so almost real-time payments, because in many of the systems that exist today, even though it's a cool frontend on your phone that you're paying off of, it's still running on very old rails, right, very old pipes through the Federal Reserve System as we try to clear those payments over time, right? So those are happening on the backend. Not all, but many of those.

And then, of course, the other work stream is not just faster, but also more secure. I mean, one of the big challenges every bank faces and we've surveyed the banks every year I've been at the Fed and we go out and talk to the banking community. The first thing on their list, the most the biggest risk they face is not interest rate risk, it's not credit risk, it's not the downside of the credit cycle. It's cybersecurity. They are petrified around cybersecurity. And so it's an issue around security of these systems that is paramount.

Now, what we've asked the industry to do is to put proposals forth about where they want to play in this new system. But we've laid out and, of course, we don't necessarily want to be in the game, necessarily, right? We do provide some of these services today. ACH, we are one of the providers. Not the only provider, but we're one of the providers that gets your paycheck into your checking account. But then, on the other side, on the wholesale side, there's Fedwire and Fedwire Securities, one of many services we provide of moving money between institutions. Again, we don't necessarily have to play in this if there's a private-sector solution that's better, but what we insist on are a set of criteria.

One of those criteria is ubiquity: everybody has to have access. What we're worried about is that there may be cream-skimming, and people taking the most profitable segment and then leaving that small bank that's serving the community shut out.

So those two work streams have come together. We're now analyzing them. We have a process for analyzing these proposals. And we'll what comes out and where people want to play.

It's not obvious to me and I can only speak for myself that people necessarily want to build the fundamental rails that we've built over many decades, right? It just doesn't seem that that it could happen. I'm not ruling it out, but I sort of doubt that.

So there's a lot of players in that industry. And we're trying to create a safe space and a and a level playing field where they can all play because, again, we want this innovation. And we actually would like to create and some other countries have created what are called sandboxes, where some existing financial institutions can actually be not relieved of, but some of the regulation relaxed so that they can innovate a little bit in this space, so that they have a level playing field as well. Because one of the issues with an existing financial institution is as opposed to, say, a startup fintech they have a regulatory structure they have to fit under versus the Google or the startup who doesn't. So we're trying to create a level playing field there, too. And some countries have done that effectively, and we're learning from that.

Q: Perhaps you would comment on how do banks manage risk. How do they estimate risk? The kinds of things I would assume are, I mean, the estimated risk of inflation and so forth. Perhaps you would comment on that.

MR. HARKER: So if there's if there's a core competency of a well-managed financial institution, it's risk management, right? Yeah. What everybody's bad at and not just banks, but this is generally true, right? seeing the proverbial black swan, right, the thing that just nobody thought was going to happen. And that's part of what happened in the crisis, right? We had thinking  one could argue groupthinking, almost, because it wasn't just I mean, we as a society believed this, that house prices couldn't go down. It was sort of a fundamental belief. I mean, look at all the shows, the flip-the-house shows that were on, right? I mean, it was just you couldn't prices could never go down. Of course, that was wrong, right? I mean, it was sort of fundamentally wrong, and it led to the crisis we had to live through.

But, absent that which some would argue was a black swan, but you know, there's arguments on both sides of that in academic literature the banks are actually quite good at managing the risks they know, right? And so interest rate risk, credit risk, those things are pretty well-known.

Now, there's new advances in technology there. There have been a lot of advance in a lot of the banks in machine learning being brought into a lot of those models to try to enhance, because there's a lot of opportunity to reduce cost and increase profit if you get the right model in place, right?

And you're starting to you're starting to see patterns that emerge. And so institutions spend an enormous amount of time on that. And then that's on the that side of the business. And, of course, there's a whole industry when it comes to the trading side of the business, right? So investment models. I mean, that's I won't talk about that. But on the banking side, the risks they know I think they manage quite well.

But part of our job as the regulator, or one of the regulators so the way  just sort of step back. The way that regulatory structure in the United States works, we have a very complicated regulatory structure. So we have keep it simple two types of banks. There are more than that, but let's call it two for now. There are national bank think Citibank and then there are state member banks, local banks the Bank of Elmira, the Bank of Bird-in-Hand, right? They have a state regulator, and so they have to adhere to state regulatory. And then they also have a choice of which federal regulator they want. That can be either us or the FDIC, the people who insure the deposits. They have a choice.

The national-level banks do not. Their regulator is the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency. If you've never heard of that, look at a dollar bill. That's the other person that signs, you know they are the Comptroller of the Currency. They regulate national banks, but we regulate the holding companies of those banks, all banks. We regulate all the holding companies. So it's a very elaborate structure of regulation.

But one of the issues after the crisis and what was brought in with the Dodd-Frank Act was that large institutions, above a certain size, would have to go through what's called stress testing or CCAR, and that we would impose we would pose a scenario to them or a set of scenarios to them and we have to check if they had adequate capital to absorb the losses in a in a situation that was negative, all right? And that process is underway. There's a lot of conversation about modifying that process. It's not perfect, but it has caused when I talk to bankers privately, a lot of them will say, even though they don't like the process because they've had to put in a lot of effort, they've learned some things, right? Not everybody said this, but some have said they've learned some things about their own risk-management processes.

And that's a healthy thing. That's a healthy thing for our economy, right? If banks can learn to be better and better risk managers, they benefit and we benefit as an economy.

Q: One of the things I was struck by when you compare the national banks to the state banks is you have investment that's necessary to keep what do you think is going to happen to the state bank system, you know, 30 years from now? How are they going to have anywhere near the ability to compete with the Wells Fargos?

MR. HARKER: Yeah, so that's a really, really good question. So I think did everybody hear the question? You have these large banks and small banks.

And so these are community banks. We'll call them that, right, as opposed to the large institutions that you would know. Not only the Wells and Chases as well, but a TD Bank, which is a super-regional bank. That's the old phrase for them. But there's categories of banks going up and down in terms of size.

It is a challenge. Right now, for a variety of reasons, there has been consolidation of those banks, and you see that in this market. There's just banks are acquiring other banks to get scale at the community level.

There's a concern there. This is the dynamic you see all the time in these markets. When a large national bank comes into a market, while there's a lot of advantages to them coming in with the scale they have, the disadvantage is they don't know their customers well. And so what they tend to first lose are a lot of the small-business loans because they don't understand a lot of the small businesses. And then they lose some other local knowledge. So you'll actually start to see some de novo banks that is, startup banks although we've not had those because of the crisis. We're starting to see a little uptick in some of the de novos, the new banks that are coming in. So larger banks acquire, they lose touch with some of the idiosyncrasies of those local markets. So that's the plus for the small banks. That's their core you know, that's their comparative advantage.

A good example. Again, this is Bank of Bird-in-Hand. So our regulators actually had to spend some time learning their business because here is an Amish farmer who has a barn and we ask, OK, you're going to get a loan; let's see your insurance on the barn. And the bank says, well, they don't have any. And we say, what, they don't have any? No, no, no, no, no, no, right? You know, Risk Management 101, you know. They're Amish. When the barn burns down, the community comes and rebuilds the barn, right? Well, a big national bank would have no clue about that, right? Just like that concept just but those individuals living in that community completely understand the local culture of that community and they serve that community.

Another good example, in the insurance world. My daughter and her husband sold a bought a place in South Philly, a gentrifying area of South Philly. They called a bunch of national insurers for homeowners, and the person asked, do you have a flat roof? Yes. So you live in a townhouse? No, I live in a rowhouse. There is no such thing as a rowhouse. I have a townhouse on my screen. (Laughter.) I don't know what a rowhouse is, right? I'm sitting in, I don't know, Omaha or something. I have no idea what a rowhouse is. So they finally gave up, after days of trying. They went down the street to the local insurance guy. He said, yeah, we get this all the time, you know? So there's local knowledge that matters.

The challenge for the small institutions, they've been challenged in this low-rate environment. Their margins have been really compressed. That's starting to ease up a little bit as rates start to come up, so there's some relief there.

But the other two areas that they're deeply concerned about, one is in the area of cybersecurity because they don't have the ability to protect themselves like the large guys. Now, most of these banks don't actually build their own back-office stuff, right? They go to a third-party vendor, and they, frankly, just have to trust that that third-party vendor knows what they're doing. And we try to help them through our regulatory process to do some due diligence. But this is number one on their list.

In fact, for the last several years we've been convening large banks in our district together with the U.S. attorney, Secret Service the Secret Service has some jurisdiction in this space and us and the FBI to give them advice on how to protect themselves. And some of the advice, it's high-tech, but here's a good example.

This is one I sat in on a bunch of these meetings, and our chief information security officer asked a bunch of information security officers: How many email addresses do you have at your bank? And everybody said, one. So you used the same email address to go to the Web as you do to manage your network? Yeah. Ooh, that's a bad thing. (Laughter.) So, one, you have two email addresses, one that never goes outside the bank. It was one of these a-ha moments. Like, well that's cheap, right? So there's some simple cyber hygiene that we're trying to provide the institutions.

But the last piece is probably the most problematic, and I don't know what to do about it because I believe these institutions are really important. In the old days I hate using that phrase but the large financial institutions had bank training programs. They hired freshly minted undergraduates, trained them on how to be a banker. They'd work at the big bank for a while, then the smaller banks would hire them, promote them with a higher level of authority. Those programs are gone. So a lot of these community banks that are aging out. You look at the age profile of their management team and they don't have people behind them to take over.

So I've heard from a lot of these folks. The dynamic is it's time to get out, right? We'll just sell, because I can't find people I don't have a succession plan for myself. That you don't think about, but that's very real for a lot of these institutions. And so I don't know how to solve that one.

The cyber one, we're helping a little bit. But those institutions I mean, again, we think we're living in cities, we're urban. You go out to the small, rural communities throughout America, these small banks are their lifeblood, right? And without them, they're in real trouble. So we want to try to maintain that community banking structure.

Q: What I also am wondering is, is there a move of the Fed with respect to its responsibility to grow the economy, if community banks were necessary and if fintech is about to crush them? And one of the things the American Bankers Association has a for-profit arm that sells services to its member banks so that they can compete. What I'm wondering is, is that a role that the Fed might have to step in?

MR. HARKER: Yeah.

Q: You know, beyond you're already in this business with (Automated Clearing House) and check clearance. I wonder if you need a broader 

MR. HARKER: Yeah, I'm not sure, because what's emerging we have one here in this district  are bankers' banks. So in between us we're the ultimate banker's bank, right, because we're the lender of last resort and the bank, there are emerging coalitions of banks that form another company that provides all the scale services, right? So they provide all the (information technology), the IT support. The one bank I'm thinking about even provides low-cost telecommunications and Web services to the bank, and provides a whole and also credit. So I think there is an intermediary in there that's starting to emerge that I actually am pretty supportive of because they're allowing these small institutions to do their thing, knowing their customer, but then not having to worry about all this other stuff. That's what the banker's bank does.

And so that outsourcing of a lot of that activity is actually, I think, quite beneficial. I've written on this. There's a paper that I wrote with my colleague Grard Cachon several years ago on independent of whether you would even have the scale issue, which you do, it may be in these small banks' self-interest in terms of price competition to do this. And there's a long theoretical argument why that's the case, but that's playing out in the market right now, that these coalitions actually seem to make a lot of sense to me. And so I would encourage these banker's banks to continue to grow.

Q: Take you back to the risks. You were talking about the idea of outsourcing part of your the things you do. Like somebody does checks to make sure the IRA regulations are being followed or whatever. The more you move things out, don't you increase risk? Now you are depending upon the  somebody (inaudible)  the external agencies who are providing essential regulatory services for you.

MR. HARKER: Well, I don't think I'm necessarily talking about them providing the regulatory services. They may provide training and so forth. But you as a bank are still going to need a compliance staff. We're going to insist on that, right, as a regulator. And the state regulator will, too.

So it's now, it's more of the scale stuff, the IT stuff, right, that I think you can get dramatic cost savings with, particularly as we move to cloud computing. And that's a slow process for the industry because of security and security concerns, but it is it is happening. And so I think you're going to see more and more of this with cloud computing.

Q: Let me take you back on trust for a moment. There is at some high level either regulation the regulatory mechanism is providing increasing trust in the enterprise and (inaudible). But in some ways (inaudible)  especially during the recession, things broke because people didn't expect it. (Inaudible) things depended on things depended on things that nobody understood, including the regulators.

MR. HARKER: That was yep, and nor did many people in the bank. (Chuckles.)

Q: (Inaudible)   right? So the regulators are charged now with assessing the risks, but there might be areas where risk is extremely hard to assess. Would that then put a ceiling on the kind of trust we can expect to get from the institutions?

MR. HARKER: Well, look, I think healthy skepticism's always a good thing, right? So we're never going to be perfect at this. I mean, I think you have to go into this just facing reality: you're never going to be perfect at this. And if there are going to be issues, the goal I mean, you're going to miss things. I mean, I think it's just human nature, right? You're just going to miss them. At least try to minimize the damage, right?

And so part of the issue is trying to figure out all those tightly coupled I mean, think about what we had. It was this tightly, tightly coupled system, right, where there were all kinds of derivative products that people were investing in and not knowing and it just cascaded along. And when you pulled out one string, this whole thing came apart.

So this is the engineer in me, right: Don't design such tightly coupled system (laughs) right, to the extent you can. Or least put buffers in, right, so that when that one string gets pulled, only a piece of it fails, right? I mean, this is the kind of engineering design thinking that's part and parcel of what we do. And I think that's how we have to think of regulators, too.

There won't be a perfect solution for everything, but I think it's and it's, by the way, the same way that people are thinking about cyberattacks. You're not going to stop them. Now, you try as hard as you can, you're not going to stop all of them. But at least minimize the damage when they happen, right? So, again, I think it comes back to, in these tightly, tightly coupled systems, don't make it that way, right? (Laughs.) So design it so that the network isn't that tightly coupled anymore. I think that's part of the design thinking.

There's actually been some recent papers on that in economics. There's actually a Ph.D. that's graduating I think he already graduated from Penn here, Penn State in economics, who's working on exactly that question right now. And he's been he's been at the bank a little bit and giving some seminars. So I think that's the way we need to start thinking in terms of regulation. We want a level playing field, but we want to minimize risk. We're never going to get rid of it completely. In fact, it would be dangerous to get rid of it completely because the economy grows with prudent risk-taking, right? So you don't you can't get rid of all risk because that's just unhealthy for the economy, but you can get rid of these let's call them catastrophic risks.

Rubin's Take: Populists, Globalists Clash on Financial Integration

Dodd-Frank Overhaul Vote Unlikely Before Summer, Lawmaker Says

Fed's Kashkari on Too Big to Fail, Dodd-Frank and More

Frankfurt Landlords Welcome Brexit Business

Populists, Globalists Clash on Financial Integration

Resurgent nationalism in the U.S. and Europe could have lasting consequences for global financial regulatory standards, and some argue the changes could hurt economic growth and financial stability.

The twin geopolitical earthquakes of Brexit and the election of Donald Trump as U.S. president pose acute challenges to regulatory bodies such as the European Commission or the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision and their ability to impose or recommend (with any significant amount of moral suasion) uniform standards for the global financial system.

With Brexit in particular, the financial integration of the European Union is at stake, given the likely loss of "passporting" privileges for British banks, which have long constituted the hub of financial services in Europe. Passporting allows London-based lenders and insurers to operate anywhere in the EU. Large multinational banks with operations in London are "https://www.wsj.com/articles/j-p-morgan-scours-europe-for-best-post-brexit-home-1490779804"for new homes within EU member countries.

"You've just turned your back on 40 years of financial integration," said Institute of International Finance President Tim Adams in an interview with The Wall Street Journal, referring to Brexit. A subsequent "tit-for-tat effort to wall off capital" would introduce major inefficiencies into global capital markets and encourage financial institutions to seek favors from national regulators, rather than operating under a set of uniform international standards, he said.

Mr. Adams highlighted the impact that devolution in financial integration could have on economic activity, using the example of an international supply chain. In an integrated system, a single bank could handle the financing for all the transactions involved in a particular deal, regardless of where they occurred. But in a less-integrated system, different banks might have to finance individual parts of the deal creating inefficiencies and higher costs for the parties involved as well as consumers.

Brexit, which has been championed by Mr. Trump as an example of nationalist interests winning out over unaccountable globalists, has also emboldened Republican skeptics of international regulatory bodies.

Shortly after Mr. Trump's inauguration, Rep. Patrick McHenry (R., N.C.), the vice chairman of the House Financial Services Committee, sent a letter to Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen calling negotiations with international standards setters like the Basel Committee "unacceptable."

In a committee hearing shortly after that, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/gop-lawmakers-tell-yellen-to-pause-new-fed-rules-1487201336"on suggestions the Fed should stop engaging in conversations with international regulators and noted that rules are only binding if U.S. regulators choose to implement them.

Nonetheless, some international regulators recognize the need to be more transparent and accountable if they are to maintain the trust of national policy makers, particularly those skeptical of their mission.

"https://www.bis.org/speeches/sp170228.pdf"about financial integration in Geneva in February, Bank for International Settlements General Manager Jaime Caruana argued for making the "positive case for financial globalization," which he said has been responsible for boosting living standards.

Financial globalization hasn't yet reached its peak potential, Mr. Caruana said, cautioning that potential "can be realized only if there is far-reaching cooperation in its management."

Both the U.K. and the Trump administration seem to be moving away from the model of international cooperation on regulatory standards and financial integration. It remains to be seen whether the "globalists'" can regain their footing in the debate and restore the influence of international financial organizations.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Dodd-Frank Overhaul Vote Unlikely Before Summer, Lawmaker Says

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON The House won't vote until this summer at the earliest on changes to the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul law, a senior Republican said Monday, demonstrating how the path for regulatory relief remains in flux as lawmakers grapple with health-care policy, a tax overhaul and other issues.

Rep. Patrick McHenry (R., N.C.) said financial regulatory policy could make it to the House floor "when it is warm out &perhaps June, July would be my hope." The vice chairman of the House Financial Services Committee and Republicans' chief deputy whip in the House was speaking in an interview with WSJ Pro Financial Regulation.

"The whole year is shifted because we have taken longer on health care," Mr. McHenry added, saying he and other Republicans need to get used to passing "imperfect" legislation that can actually become law. When a Democrat was in the White House, GOP ideas were unlikely to become law, but now Republicans "are shooting with real bullets" when it comes to writing legislation, he said.

When Republicans do move toward voting on a financial regulatory bill, there is "no question" the House can "pass a major change to financial services law," Mr. McHenry said. "What the Senate can do from there is an open question," he added, nodding to the fact major Dodd-Frank changes might face opposition from Senate Democrats.

Mr. McHenry said there are other ways for Republicans to adopt major financial regulatory changes. Republicans are considering using changes in financial services law to "pay for" a tax overhaul, he said. He mentioned one specific provision: repealing the government's authority under Dodd-Frank to provide temporary loans to a failing financial firm.

The Congressional Budget Office estimates repealing the provision, known as orderly liquidation authority, could save about $15 billion over 10 years. "Even in D.C., that is real money," Mr. McHenry said. "It is a large enough dollar amount where the threshold would make sense for the fight."

Other potential changes that could find their way into a tax overhaul or budget bill are more in flux, Mr. McHenry said. That includes changes to the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau's structure, which he described as a "moving target" because Republicans have different ideas for how they want to change the agency.

Mr. McHenry also called on U.S. regulators to disclose more information about the positions they take in international regulatory discussions, including on the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision.

He criticized the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency for what he views as moving too fast in its recent efforts to adopt a federal banking charter for financial-technology firms, and said that the OCC's efforts have resulted in a charter that is probably too narrow for those firms to try to obtain.

"I would like to give them more authority," he said, referring to potential legislation giving federal regulators wider latitude to offer charters for fintech firms. "I don't think you're going to have a huge movement into the space around what the OCC is attempting to do."

WSJ Pro Transcript: Fed's Kashkari on Too Big to Fail, Dodd-Frank and More

Neel Kashkari, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis, delivered remarks and took questions at an event on March 31, 2017, at the Minnesota CLE Conference Center, in Minneapolis. He discussed the structure of the Fed, the issue of "too-big-to-fail" financial institutions, the 2010 Dodd-Frank law and other issues. Here is a transcript of the exchange edited for clarity and length.

NEEL KASHKARI: I do want to make it a conversation. So, please, I always ask audiences to show me respect by asking me your toughest question. And I mean that sincerely, because if you're thinking of a tough question there's a chance somebody else is thinking of a tough question, and I'd rather get it out in the open and have a chance to address it than have you leave having not asked and answered your question. I'm only going to speak for a few minutes up front, and then I'll turn to Karen and we'll have a discussion, and then turn to all of you.

Let me just start with level setting. You all most of you probably know this but some people many not on why I'm here. I didn't know a lot about the Federal Reserve before I went to Washington when I was at the Treasury Department.

One of the things that's unique about our country is that our central bank is distributed around the country. If you look back in American history, as a country we hated the idea of having a central bank at all. Alexander Hamilton created the first one. It lasted about 20 years. We didn't like the idea of all this power concentrated in this group in Washington. It just felt un-American.

And so we had these fits and starts and then we had a lot of financial crises in the 1800s, and finally Congress said, we need to do something about this, and they just said, we need a central bank.

And so but the negotiation was the compromise was we're going to have a central bank but it's not going to be all concentrated in one place in Washington, D.C. under the control of the executive branch. So the compromise was they created the Board of Governors in Washington and then they created 12 regional Federal Reserve banks around the country and those regional bank presidents, myself included, are appointed by local representatives.

So there's a board of directors here. It's chaired by a woman named MayKao Hang, who runs the Wilder Foundation in St. Paul. Our deputy chair is Ken Powell, the CEO of General Mills and there's nine total and they're the ones who did a search and ended up appointing me. And the point of this is that you get a distributed central bank. You have a diverse set of perspectives, not just representing Washington, not just representing New York.

So the reason that's important for you to understand is it's important for me to hear from you so I can get a better sense of what's going on in our region. I travel a lot around our region, which is Minnesota, North and South Dakota, Montana, part of Michigan, part of Wisconsin, to understand what's happening in the local economy, what's happening in the credit market there, and that helps me become smarter. When I go back to Washington, D.C., and make our policy recommendations, I'm here to represent you and to represent your clients. And so that's why I'm here and that's why it is very important that I hear directly from you what's on your mind and what you are seeing and experiencing.

Now, I've been at the bank for a little over a year. I joined January of last year. One of the first issues I wanted to take on was the issue of too-big-to- fail. So I was as Karen said, I was one of the first responders to the financial crisis. I saw firsthand how hard it was to arrest the crisis once it was in full bloom and I feel very strongly we never want to have that happen again as a country.

The problem is if you look at human history, human societies are prone to exuberance. They're prone to overreach. They're prone to crisis. So these things happen every 50 years, every 80 years, every hundred years and they tend to be different when they happen in the future. But there are echoes. There are some similarities as they happen.

And so I, right out of the gate, started with our researchers at the Minneapolis Fed, which has been a longtime leader on the issue of too big to fail, and said, what can we do about this what analysis can we bring to the table. And so last year we brought the best experts in the world to Minneapolis, hear from them. Some of them said, break up the banks. Some of them said, increase capital for the banks. Some of them said, don't do anything Dodd-Frank is just fine it's addressed the problem.

But we said, let's hear all sides let's do our own analysis and put forward our own plan, and in November we released a draft of that plan and we've been receiving comments and are preparing to release a final plan. The bottom line is, and I've been saying this to anybody who will listen, is the biggest banks are absolutely still too big to fail. Don't let anybody tell you otherwise.

If one of the big banks ran into trouble today, they would be in the Oval Office on their knees begging for a bailout and the administration would have little choice but to help. Why? Because you all saw what happened when Lehman went into bankruptcy, the chaos it created in the money market markets, the chaos it created in the broader banking sector, in the nonbanking sector. It almost brought our economy to its knees. Only with massive government intervention both in the money markets themselves and through the (Troubled Asset Relief Program) and the Federal Reserve were we able to stabilize the economy.

And so I don't think that's a good situation for us to be in as a country and so the plan after all the analysis that we did, the plan that we put forward effectively doubles the capital requirements of the biggest banks and it forces them to make a tough choice: you can either stay large that's fine and you're going to have a lot more capital to protect all of us, or you can choose to restructure yourselves, and if your business model can support that higher capital, that's great. You can choose to make that decision. You decide how to respond to that.

But the bottom line is the taxpayer should not be on the hook for the big risks that the biggest banks are taking. And keep in mind, when you and I go to get a home loan the banks ask us to put 20% down, typically. Why do they do that? That 20% is to protect the banks in case you and I screw up and get into financial trouble.

Well, it's pretty simple. If we make the biggest banks put 20% down on their investments, then we get protected from their bad investments and their bad bets. Seems pretty fair to me. So our basic plan and there's a lot of detail there's 50 pages of detail on our website you can look at our basic plan is substantially higher capital requirements on the biggest banks those $250 billion in assets and above and then with that we can relax regulations on the small banks that are not systemically risky, because I know you hear from a lot of small banks I do how they're getting smothered by the same regulatory net that's trying to address too big to fail.

Well, the truth is little community banks are not systemically risky for the economy, they do have a very important role to play in providing credit to our communities and they should not be captured in the same regulatory net. So that was a big a big initiative for us and I'm proud of the work that we've done and, again, happy to take your feedback.

I'll say this. We put this out. We sought comments from experts. To be blunt, nobody's been able to poke holes in our analysis. Our analysis is spot on and I'm very proud of that and it's based on analysis that the (International Monetary Fund) has done looking at the history of financial crises around the world and figuring out how do we best learn from that, how do we best have a regulatory system that can protect us from that and how do we do it in a way that society is better off. So that's number one.

Number two, in a very different way, one of the things that surprised me about our region you know, this is a really remarkable local economy. It's very diverse. We have financial services. We have manufacturing. We have ag. We have mining. We have health care, et cetera. It's a diverse economy that's doing quite well overall and we have overall low unemployment.

But we have huge gaps in our society here all around the country but also here, and that surprised me the extent of the racial and economic disparity in our region, and I wanted to understand why that is. And so I started talking to economists at the Minneapolis Fed and around the country and I was amazed at how little we understand what the source is of those gaps.

I'll give you just a data point. In America nationwide, black unemployment is almost always twice white unemployment. If it's a booming economy and white unemployment is four, there's a really good bet that black unemployment is going to be eight. If it's a recession and white unemployment is seven, pretty good bet black unemployment is going to be 14. And even if you adjust for education, so it's college graduates versus college graduates, same disparity, or non-high-school graduate versus non-high-school graduate, same disparities.

So I started asking the basic questions. Why is that? What are the structural factors? Now, we know as the nation's central bank we can't tailor monetary policy to individual communities or segments or groups. It's a blunt tool for the whole country. But my view is if we can do the research to understand these problems and put forward ideas that other policy makers, whether it's the executive branch or Congress, could then take and implement and do something with, then that's an appropriate role for us to play.

So in January of this year, a few months ago, we announced what we're calling the Opportunity and Inclusive Growth Institute, which is a research institute focused on understanding economic and racial disparities. And it's not just about race. t's also about rural communities and the gaps between rural America and the cities that are generally doing better, understanding what can be done, whether it's education or housing, et cetera.

And so we're just getting started. In May, we're having our first conference. We've recruited the best academics in the country who are working on these issues to advise us as our board of advisers. We have visiting scholars who are going to be coming from around the country to Minneapolis to work with us to understand these issues and see what role the Federal Reserve can play in analyzing that and putting forward solutions.

This is a long-term research initiative. It's going to take years. I mean, honestly, these problems are decades or more in the making. We're not going to come up with answers overnight. But we're going to do our part and we're going to get going, and so that's another big initiative.

And what the thread that is woven underneath all of this is I want us to take on the biggest economic challenges that we face as a nation, period. I believe the Federal Reserve banks, and there is 12 of them, should be research centers taking on these big long-term issues. If we're not going to do it, who's going to do it?

I mean, you're watching Washington like I am. Washington, by its nature, being a political city, is much more has to be focused on short-term issues, short-term challenges. Somebody needs to be doing the long-term economic research and thinking on these big challenges and I want us to play that role in partnership with others. And so that's what we're going to do.

And so with that, happy to I could sit down next to you, Karen 

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Well, you've made several comments about the structure of the Fed and its decentralized structure, and there have been numerous proposals saying that it doesn't work anymore. What do you say to those? Any merit?

MR. KASHKARI: You know, it's nobody would if you started from a clean sheet of paper today nobody would design it exactly the way it is today. I'll give you an example. So the Minneapolis Fed, we're here in Minnesota. We have a fairly small population. The San Francisco Fed has 60 million people in their Western region of the country.

Now, a hundred years ago these diagrams, the way the districts were designed, made sense. Missouri has two Federal Reserve banks. They're in St. Louis and Kansas City. That was a compromise to buy off some very powerful senator who was negotiating the bill. So if you started with a clean sheet of paper you wouldn't design it this way.

Having said that, it works, and, you know, we've found a way over decades to get the system to work and so I think we need to be more transparent in how we're making decisions. I think we need to be engaged with the public more, more transparent, getting more feedback so that people have confidence in the way we're approaching our decisions.

So I'm doing everything I can to try to innovate in how we reach out to people, whether it's doing a lot of Q&A like this. By the way, the reason we're live streaming this is I want as many people who want to know what I'm talking about, what I'm doing, I want them to be able to do that.

So I live stream all of my events. I'm on Twitter. I do Twitter Q&A with people to engage with people from the public. I'm looking for every way I can to try to demystify the Fed and I think if people saw what we're really doing people would have a lot of confidence in the current structure. So I don't think we need a big change in the structure. I do think we need to communicate more freely than we have been historically.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: You've seen many aspects of government. You were at Treasury. You ran for governor in the state of California. Anything surprise you about the Fed? Anything you didn't expect?

MR. KASHKARI: You know, I knew the Fed from when I was at Treasury because we worked very closely together, had very smart people, very high quality, dedicated to public service, and that's absolutely true. If you got to know our colleagues here in Minnesota and around the country at the Minneapolis Fed and elsewhere, you'd be very proud of them and proud that our nation attracts people who have this commitment to public service.

So that didn't surprise me. I knew that. One thing that did surprise me is I talked about some of the disparities and the racial gaps in our region. We have those in the Fed, too. We don't have enough diversity in the Fed itself and it's something that we are working hard to try to improve.

So if you look at the (Federal Open Market Committee), currently I'm the only nonwhite person on the FOMC. The new Atlanta Fed the Atlanta Fed just selected a new president, [an] economist named Raphael Bostic. He'll be the first African-American Federal Reserve Bank president in the history of the country. It's amazing. It took 103 years for one of the 12 presidents to become African-American. And so we need more diversity at the Fed at the staff level and at the senior levels. It's something the whole system is committed to. I was surprised to the extent of that before I got in and saw it for myself.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: You talked about the plan and too big to fail, and you referenced two pieces of it, I think, and I believe it's a four-step plan. The first step was the capitalization of the very biggest banks. How are you defining the big banks?

MR. KASHKARI: So $250 billion assets and above.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: So why that benchmark?

MR. KASHKARI: You know, that's admittedly, it's somewhat arbitrary. Whenever you make policy you have to pick some boundaries, and it just based on our analysis and looking at different literature reviews, it seemed like that was a reasonable way to capture the biggest banks. And the truth is the idea of systemic risk there is judgment involved. I wish it was a simple formula that we could perfectly define, and it is contextual.

So I'll give you an example. You know, Bear Stearns was saved in a weekend in March 2008 when the financial system was in a very perilous state. If Bear Stearns had run into trouble three years earlier and the economy was very strong and all the other banks were fine, it probably would have been okay to just let Bear Stearns go into bankruptcy. So the definition of what is systemic depends on what else is happening in the rest of the economy. And so we just looked at the biggest institutions in America and said, we have to draw a line somewhere. This seemed like a conservative place to draw the line that erred on the side of protecting taxpayers rather than protecting shareholders.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Second part of your plan gets at the systemic risk, and you've designated the secretary of the Treasury to have a pretty significant role. Why did you put that huge obligation with the secretary of the Treasury?

MR. KASHKARI: So what in what we say in the plan is for these biggest banks it's about a dozen of them first thing we do is you take up their capital levels to, roughly, double where they are today and then the Treasury secretary must certify affirmatively that these banks are no longer too big to fail.

Now, imagine you're the Treasury secretary. You're not going to want to make that certification because if you certify that this bank is no longer too big to fail and it runs into trouble and it needs a bailout, you have some big-time egg on your face.

So we wanted to put the Treasury secretary in a bind to make the Treasury secretary really reluctant to make that certification, and he or she will only make that certification if they are really confident that this bank is no longer too big to fail, and if they if they refuse to make that certification, the capital levels start cranking up even higher.

And this will put again, the whole idea is we want somebody on the hook to make this call. And if they don't make the call because they're not sure taxpayers are protected, the banks are going to see even higher capital levels, which is going to create huge incentives for the banks to restructure themselves.

So here's the thing. The banks love to tell you and me and brag about their great economies of scale that we all benefit from as a society. Well, if the banks actually have these great economies of scale that we all benefit from, they can afford higher capital. If they cannot afford higher capital, then these economies of scale are a myth and all they're doing is their business model depends on taxpayer subsidies, in a sense. So we're putting that to the test by ratcheting up capital requirements automatically absent this certification.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: But if you ratchet up capital won't people put their money elsewhere?

MR. KASHKARI: Absolutely.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: What's going to stop investors from not putting money in other industries and will we have management talent also leave the federal or the financial services industry?

MR. KASHKARI: Well, I mean, what would you expect to have happen? So we jack up the capital requirements on the biggest 12 banks in America. What's going to happen? One is those banks may choose to restructure themselves, which I would not be opposed to.

Two, they may the activity and the talent may move to community banks or regional banks. We may not have a we won't have as concentrated a banking sector as we have today. We'll have a more competitive banking market, which I also think is good for the economy. I also think it's good for financial stability to having more diversity and more competition, and smaller banks playing a bigger role. So I think that's a good thing.

The bigger risk that we're worried about is activity moving from the biggest banks let's say, J.P. Morgan or Goldman Sachs to hedge funds, and if it all moves from J.P. Morgan to Citadel or Bridgewater these really big hedge funds have we really accomplished anything or have we just moved the risk from one place to another. And that's why in step three of the plan we apply a tax on the debt of the biggest shadow banks.

So if you're a little shadow bank, a little hedge fund, you don't pay any tax. Or if you're a big hedge fund and you don't have any debt, you don't pay any tax. But if you're a big hedge fund, 50 billion (dollars) and above that's debt financing your investments then that could be a systemic risk in the future and we want to level the playing field so that the cost of funding of the highly capitalized banks and these big shadow banks is level.

So there's no incentive to migrate the activity to just some other unregulated part of the market and this, we think, is a real innovation. Nobody else had put out put forward a way to deal with the shadow banks and we do, and so we do think that that's important.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Now, you've talked about big banks and community banks. What about the middle?

MR. KASHKARI: In the middle, we are leaving them alone, basically saying we're not calling for any change to the current regulations that they're undergoing. We're not increasing their capital requirements.

I will say this. If we have confidence that the biggest banks are all highly capitalized, then the system as a whole is much more resilient than it is today. And then we could look at all sorts of regulations and saying, do you need these other regulations on whether it's community banks or mid-sized banks, do you need to be have them as onerous as perhaps they are today?

You know, and having the biggest banks with substantially higher capital requirements, it's like vaccinating them, right? You vaccinate the herd, and then any one of them can't bring down the whole herd because the whole herd has been vaccinated. And that's why it's so important that the biggest banks all have higher capital.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: One thing I liked about in reading your plan is you addressed supervision and regulation. In my experience, people tend to talk about regulation and forget about the supervision part, which to me is important and very distinct from regulation, particularly in this environment where on-site exams, informal rule-making, policy statements I would argue probably affect the banking industry maybe more than some of the statutes and rules. So what do you envision in terms of that supervision of the community banks versus the midsize versus the big? Will the exam process stay the same? Would you allow this informal rule-making to continue? Do we need to redo that part of the equation?

MR. KASHKARI: Yeah. I think on both sides, supervision and regulation for community banks, we need to take a fresh look and get it right. And again, I just talked to a lot of community banks. But some of the things that they're asking about, just to be candid, are not things that maybe the Fed or the OCC directly affects. It may be things that it's the CFPB. And so I think we need to take a comprehensive view on what we're putting community banks through.

I'll give you I wrote an op-ed in The Wall Street Journal a few months ago about my own experience getting a home loan in Minnesota, which was brutal. I mean, I couldn't believe how painful the process was in terms of the documentation requirements. I thought that they had figured out who I was and they were punking me (laughter) by just continuing to ask me more and more and more things.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Maybe they were.

MR. KASHKARI: Maybe they were.

And so it was just and I actually felt bad for because of the banker that I was dealing with, the mortgage lender, would call me and apologize that underwriting was asking for this additional documents. He's, like, I know this is ridiculous, but there is no negotiating with the underwriting department. It was I mean, I felt bad for them because it felt like a climate of fear, like, just they're terrified of making a mistake. I finally got the loan approved and I bought my house, but it was still a very, very painful process.

So I and I do think that we have it we do need to take a step back and look at what we're doing, doesn't make sense, rationalize these things, especially for community banks, which are not systemically risky for the economy and because they are uniquely positioned to provide credit, especially in small communities in America. It's very hard for the Bank of Americas and Citibanks and J.P. Morgans to know what's happening in rural communities across our region. That's the role that community banks have to play. And so I do think we have to take a fresh look.

But I also know, when I and I meet with members of the House and Senate a lot when I'm in Washington. While there seems to be bipartisan consensus on wanting to relax regulations on community banks, they're nervous that that might be a Trojan horse for relaxing regulations on the biggest banks. And that's why these two issues have become linked. And so it's so important that we really address too big to fail for the biggest banks so that we can then decouple regulation and supervision of the community banks.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: You talked about the process of going through your home loan, so that touches on, you know, consumer regulation and protection. How do we balance that consumer protection with safety and soundness? And is it your sense it's out of balance right now?

MR. KASHKARI: Well, I think if you look at loans are being made, business loans are being made. If you look at surveys of small businesses, they're generally able to get access to credit. It's just a very painful process, as I experienced. I got my loan. I was prequalified for it. I got an attractive rate. It was just an extremely painful process. So you have to really want it if you want to go through the process.

People with bad credit it's much harder to get credit today. And I'm not sure so that's unfortunate, but I'm not sure that that's necessarily a bad like, we don't want banks making loans that they will be nervous about, right? We don't want to force them to make loans to people who maybe there's not a high probability of them paying it back. And so that those getting people with helping people have better credit is a separate policy problem than whether or not banks are making loans.

So I don't know. Did I answer your question?

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Mmm hmm which leads to another interesting aspect of what's going on. Increasingly, banks are being looked to as the solution to some of those policy issues, be it redlining, jobs, terrorism. Should banks be used in that fashion?

MR. KASHKARI: Well, I think banks we need to make sure banks are treating their customers fairly. We need to make sure that you know, the Wells Fargo account scandal is pretty shocking to anybody who's looked at it. So making sure that banks are treating their customers appropriately and fairly is important, whether it's a big bank or a little bank. Making sure banks are not turning a blind eye to potential money laundering or financing of fraudulent activities or terrorist activities is also it's important that they abide by the rules and take that very seriously. And I know the regulators are focused on that appropriately so.

But we can't expect banks or banks are not there to solve all of society's problems. As I said, if we have Americans who are struggling with bad credit, that's not the banks' fault, right? Those are issues about education, financial education, financial literacy, the job market more broadly other things that affect poverty, affect families' lives, et cetera. We can't expect the banks to solve all of that.

But we can expect the banks to do their part, which is treating all their customers fairly, being honest with them and following the rules and following the law.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: One of the comments often made about your proposal is it puts the American banks at a disadvantage with their global competitors. Do you want to address that topic?

MR. KASHKARI: Sure. You know, it's true. The fact is, if you go to Europe today, they don't really care about "too big to fail." Europe basically says, we have these national champion banks, and it's their pride and joy. And if every 20 years or 30 years, have to bail them out, that's OK; we have national champions, and we're happy to have that. That's a silly policy position, I think. I don't think it's one that we as Americans should have.

And if we can show the world that we can have large banks that are well-capitalized, that are not exposing our taxpayers to unnecessary risks, then maybe we can lead the world and show the rest of the world that there is a way that they can have similar in their countries. But I don't think we should wait just because Europe wants to bail out their banks every 20 or 30 years. I don't think that that means we should follow their path.

And by the way, I'd also say, if any of these European banks that are undercapitalized want to do business in America, we should subject them to the exact same rules that we subject our banks to. And that's how we can start to level the playing field.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Your paper spends a lot of time on the cost-benefit analysis. And my understanding is there would actually be a drag on the economy, putting in these high-capital levels. For the longer-term good, my experience is we live day to day, quarter to quarter. I mean, if you look at what shareholders expect of publicly traded banks, for example: What have you done for me today? How are you ever going to convince citizens, Congress, to take and put something in place that's going to deal with something 50 years from now, maybe, that hurts us now?

MR. KASHKARI: Well, we do it and so the analysis is this: When you raise capital requirements, there are some economists who say there is no harm to the economy, it doesn't affect credit availability. I don't believe that. I believe there is some cost to it. It does raise the cost of borrowing a little bit for those banks' borrowers.

Now, remember, we're raising capital requirements on the dozen or so biggest banks in America, none of the rest. So what'll end up happening is some of the activity is going to move smaller institutions again, which is good from a financial stability perspective.

But let's admit there are some costs. Safety isn't free. Right, this is just like physical safety. So after 9/11 we as a country imposed many new requirements to improve our physical security. We hired more police officers, hired Homeland Security officers, more metal detectors at airports. Physical safety isn't free. There is a cost-benefit analysis. We avoid potential terrorist attacks, but we pay for it in some inconvenience and some more taxes that we spend.

So we as a country have to decide how much financial safety we want. Financial safety isn't free. So we've done the analysis and said, if we raise the cost of credit a little bit for the customers of these biggest banks, but we avoid these terrible financial crises that we have you know, whether it's the Great Depression or now that we had in the in the financial crisis in '08 there is a benefit. And so do the costs exceed the benefits? No. The benefits exceed the costs. So, over time, society is better off.

One more analogy is nuclear power. A big bank is like a nuclear reactor, right? If it melts down, if it makes a mistake and fails, it's devastating for society. So what happens if there is a nuclear power meltdown? Government will spend whatever it takes to stabilize that reactor because of the costs of that meltdown are catastrophic for society. So we can either ban nuclear power, or what have we done? We regulate it so tightly that it virtually can't fail you can never say never, but it virtually can't fail.

That's the same way I think we should treat the biggest banks in America, which is, you can choose to stay large, but we're going to regulate you so that society is protected. And those regulations of nuclear power does increase the cost of power a little bit for all of us, but that's a choice that we've made. And so I feel like what we're doing is we're presenting you and the American people some options, some costs and some benefits, and then the public can decide how much safety you want in our financial system.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Now, to accomplish your plan, do you need legislation? Can this be done only through a new regulation? And how do we get there?

MR. KASHKARI: Yeah, we do need we would need legislation, especially for things like the tax on shadow banks, et cetera. And I do think at the end of the day this decision about safety versus cost is ultimately a political decision and I don't mean partisan politics; I mean the American people expressing a view through their elected representatives. I do think that Congress is the right body to make this type of a decision. And, you know, we just got to keep educating people.

And the biggest concern that I have is we as a society tend to forget the lessons that we just recently learned. We just recently went through this terrible crisis, and now the winds are blowing, maybe we should deregulate, as though, oh, it can't happen again. And so it's not that you and I are not going to make the mistake. It's the next generation and the generation after that after we're all retired or beyond that are going to make that mistake.

And so the question is, can we put in place a regulatory system today that can stand the test of time so that when we've forgotten and now this time is different, it can't happen again, the regulations are there to protect us from ourselves?

MS. GRANDSTRAND: A number of proposals, and in particular one has focused on Glass-Steagall. What are your views on Glass-Steagall? Do we put it back in place, tweak it?

MR. KASHKARI: Yeah, you know, Glass-Steagall in my view wasn't I'm not opposed to a reintroduction of Glass-Steagall, but I don't think it would address the too big to fail issue by itself. If you look at the last crisis, just as an example, there were pure-play investment banks that got into trouble, like Lehman or Bear Stearns, and there were pure-play commercial banks that got into trouble. It wasn't simply the merging of the two of those activities. And so I'm not opposed to it, but again, I don't think it would solve the problem. All of the analysis that I've seen, or most of it, suggest that substantially higher capital requirements are the best way to address this. And even (FDIC Vice Chairman) Tom Hoenig, parts of his proposal also called for much higher capital requirements focusing on the leverage ratio.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: With respect to the supervision of banks, we were talking earlier today about how the way supervision is conducted seems to have evolved. You know, back in 1985 some of us were (chuckles) thinking back to those years there were no such things as enforcement actions. And now you open the newspaper and, you know, I think the top five headlines in American Banker are which banks have gotten the penalty of the day. What do you think about that system? Is it is it good for society to set rules through enforcement actions? How has this affected that relationship between the industry and the bank regulators?

MR. KASHKARI: Well, I mean, I can't imagine it's pleasant to receive an enforcement action. But enforcement actions in my experience come after a lot of dialogue saying, you need to address this, you need to address this, you need to address this. And they don't address it. And then guess what? You're going to get an enforcement action. And so there needs to be some form of follow-up.

It's funny; I meet with banks, and they say, oh, it's so arbitrary why we got this enforcement action, you know, we've been complying with everything. Then I go I meet with the examiners, and I meet the supervision, and I meet with folks in Washington, and they say, that's ridiculous. These are the five things we asked them to do. We asked them a year ago. They said, yes, we got it. A year later, they haven't done any of them. And we went back, and we said, we really meant it, you need to do these five things. Yeah, we got it, we're on it, we're doing it. And they didn't do it. Guess what? You're going to get an enforcement action. So there needs to be some teeth. You need to follow the rules.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Switching topics you made headlines recently for your dissenting vote. You want to talk a little bit about the FOMC, why you dissented, and what happens (inaudible) doors?

MR. KASHKARI: Sure. So I in the last in March, the FOMC meeting, the FOMC (inaudible) to raise interest rates by 25 basis points. And I was the lone voter to dissent, saying, no, we should hold rates steady. And I have in January I published an article looking at exactly the data that I look at on inflation, on the job market to reach my decision on where the appropriate level of interest rates is. If you're really interested in this topic, there is a lot of detail in that website that I would point you to.

So Congress has given the Fed what we call a dual mandate, which is stable prices which means what we define as 2% inflation and maximum employment, as many Americans working as who want to work as possible. So we look at data on both of those to see, are we achieving our dual mandate? And if you look over the last five or six years, we keep getting it wrong. For five or six years in a row, we keep expecting inflation's going to rebound, right around the corner, and we keep thinking, oh, the job market is as strong as it's going to get.

Well, it turns out inflation has been coming in under our target consistently for the past several years and the job market keeps exceeding our expectations. We keep thinking that we're at maximum employment, and then all these Americans re-enter the labor force which is a really good thing and it turns out we're not yet at maximum employment. So we would have declared victory too soon.

So I voted no because in both of those measurements we're not there yet. You know, just this morning our measure the most important measure we look at for inflation, called PCE or personal-consumption expenditures, came out, and core inflation which strips out energy and food, which are really volatile core inflation ticked up ever so slightly, maybe from 1.7 to 1.8, but it's more rounding than anything. So we're still coming up a little short on our inflation target, and we're not coming up quickly at all, and the job market keeps surprising us.

You know, one of the data points that we look at is called labor-force participation, how many Americans are in the labor force. And it fell dramatically after the financial crisis. Now, a lot of people said, well, that's because of demographics. Our society is aging. More people are retiring. But even if you strip out retirees and you just look at working-age Americans, there's still some room to come back to what we used to consider normal in terms of how many working-age adults are actually working.

So, because we're coming up short on both of our measurements, I said there's no reason to hurry up and raise rates. Let's let inflation keep climbing back to target, and let's see how the job market if it can keep strengthening and bringing workers back in, that's a good thing.

And so I don't think there's a huge disagreement between me and my colleagues. We're looking at the same data. I just see the balance of risk a little differently than they do.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: Could you comment on the Fed's balance sheet?

MR. KASHKARI: Sure. This is a topic of great interest these days. We have about a $4 1/2 trillion balance sheet as a result of the quantitative easing programs that the Fed launched after the financial crisis. And the reason this is let me just back up for a second. The reason the Fed launched (quantitative easing) is because interest rates fell so what are interest rates? Interest rates are where savings and investment are meant to balance. They clear the market. Interest rates fell down to zero because of the Great Recession and the financial crisis and the slow recovery. But if you look at the economic fundamentals, the economy as a whole was actually calling for deeply negative interest rates to clear the market of savings and investment. But the Fed couldn't do that. They were stuck at zero. So either they were out of ammunition, or they said we need another tool to provide monetary stimulus to the economy because we can't go deeply negative. So they said, well, we're going to buy up long-term bonds to try to drive down long-term interest rates, which will have a similar effect as reducing interest rates. So that's why the Fed ballooned its balance sheet.

Now that the economy is getting stronger and we're starting to raise interest rates, there is interest among FOMC members to start to shrink that balance sheet in a gradual and predictable way. And so what I've said is I would like us to announce our detailed plan for exactly how we're going to do that so the markets can see it, can understand it, we can see the market's reaction to it, and give the markets as much advance warning as possible. Then we start that process, and then we can go on with interest rate increases when the data call for it.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: I gather from your comment you would support greater transparency in terms of Fed decision-making?

MR. KASHKARI: Yeah, I think so, and different transparency.

So one thing that's different about me and my colleagues is I never make I don't think I do ever make predictions about when the next rate hike is going to be or how many we're going to have this year or next year. The reason is we're usually wrong, and people get really upset when we say, oh, it's going to be three hikes this year it's going to be three hikes, and then it ends up being one. Or it's going to be four hikes, and then it ends up being one. And so I just think it adds a lot of noise and confusion when you have all these Fed heads offering these predictions about how many hikes there are going to be.

So I think it's much better to say I don't know how many hikes there are going to be; here's the data I'm going to look at in reaching my decisions. I'll be very transparent in terms of what data I look at. You can look at the same data I do. You can look at it in the same real time that I do, and you can use your own judgment. And so I try to err on the side of giving you more information about what data I think is most important rather than making predictions, which I think just leads to confusion. And so I would encourage us to have a different type of communication, rather than more of the same communication.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: I think it's time now to open it up to the audience and the really hard questions.

MR. KASHKARI: Good.

MS. GRANDSTRAND: So do we have our first question?

Q: Your proposal to double the capital obviously has a number of industry people who think it's not a good idea, but I haven't seen a lot of support from your fellow bank presidents. Why? And without that support, does it really go anywhere?

MR. KASHKARI: Well, some of them I'll let them speak for themselves. Some of them have expressed some support on the idea of more capital for less you know, if you really increase the capital requirements, you could relax some of the other rules. I think that there's more support for that than maybe you realize. You know, we'll see. I feel like my job is to identify risks, call them out, being as transparent as I can, and offer potential solutions, and then it's up to regulators and ultimately up to Congress to decide what to do with that. And so we're going to keep pushing. You know, I meet with as I mentioned, I meet with senators and representatives of both sides of the aisle, and there's a lot of agreement that we need to address the too-big-to-fail issue. And there's a lot of agreement that we need to relax regulations on community banks, and so this is one of those issues where I actually think something could get done in Washington. But I don't feel like just because it's hard means I shouldn't take it on.

Q: Karen asked you a question about transparency. So from the standpoint of an ignorant observer who reads the newspaper, it seems like there's been this evolving trend toward greater transparency over the years at the Fed. So things that used to be a black box, policy decisions and the like, are now talked about, as you mentioned, with fairly divergent approaches. Is that the new normal? Is that intentional? Has it just happened over time?

MR. KASHKARI: No, it is intentional. You know, so the Fed's posture really in the Greenspan era was we're like "The Wizard of Oz," right? We're so mysterious; don't even ask because you couldn't understand, and we wouldn't tell you anyway. And that didn't serve us well. Because when the financial crisis hit, all of a sudden we needed the American people to trust us, and we had not earned that trust because we had not built that trust over time. And so we're still paying the price for that lack of transparency. So all of us are trying in our own ways to be more transparent, to earn the trust of the public that we need in order to do our jobs.

And so yeah, I think that that is it's intentional. You know, for example, in the 80s the Fed never even put out a statement when they made a monetary policy decision. They just figured the markets would figure it out depending on what's happening in interest rates. Now, we put out statements after every meeting. Now, four times a year the chair does a press conference. So starting with the chair first Chair (Ben) Bernanke and now Chair (Janet) Yellen they've really dialed up transparency and communications and all Fed principals are trying.

Q: What did you do up to this time to cut possibility of stealing money of small organizations by some individuals? For instance, secretary-treasurer of some organizations can take all money of their organization and move to another account, and nobody has access to them. An organization comes to a sort of dying situation because it cannot pay their financial obligations. So did you do something not you your organization did something to preclude, to stop such situation?

MR. KASHKARI: So let's say you have an institution of some kind and you have a financial professional who could steal from that institution by routing the money somewhere where nobody else knows where it's gone. Is that right?

Q: Yeah, nobody knows because you will not tell.

MR. KASHKARI: Well, so I think now you're getting into things like audits and financial controls and the ability you know, does that institution have appropriate financial control? So I think the Fed's role is, as we're supervising banks, we're also talking to the auditors, we're also looking at the financial controls to make sure that the appropriate financial controls are in place. But you're right, it is very hard to prevent one sole bad actor from doing something bad in their own institution, and it's, as you've indicated, in that institution's interest to put the appropriate controls in place. So I think that our job would be talking to the auditors, looking at the financial controls ourselves, making sure they're following rules and regulations, but at the end of the day it is also going to be up to management to make sure they put in place appropriate controls in their own organization. I think it's very hard for us to know that.

Q: No, I don't believe that is very hard, I'm sorry, because I contradict you.

MR. KASHKARI: That's OK.

Q: Impose on all banks, all tellers understand, to accept money to be deposited but to not be withdrawal from account except of signature of secretary-treasurer or somebody from board, or two people from board? And then chances to be stolen will be much less, understand? But at this time, for instance, secretary-treasurer can go and take money at least in some organization I see and took them and put them in another account at another bank, and the organization can do nothing.

MR. KASHKARI: Right, but you're speaking I'm not disagreeing with you.

Q: It's a simple formality.

MR. KASHKARI: You're speaking about the core financial controls and the rules within that organization of who's authorized to transfer money?

Q: Yes.

MR. KASHKARI: I understand. And so I'm with you. I'm not disagreeing with you. So I guess I'm in agreement with you.

Q: Yeah, should be one, two person from the board or president, plus secretary-treasurer. Secretary-treasurer should do simply bureaucratic work, not to take decision what to be done with money. It should such decisions should be taken by executive committee, or by president plus somebody from executive committee. Yeah, so I don't know, at this time do you do you implement it in banks' obligations for financial withdrawal, financial payments-- &

MR. KASHKARI: But again, it's going to depend on the bank.

Q: to have two, three signatures from the organizations?

MR. KASHKARI: Large institutions will have a higher threshold than smaller institutions.

Q: (Inaudible.)

MR. KASHARI: And so we do. We do talk to banks and look at what financial controls they have in place and try to assess it. So if you have specific instances where you think that that's not being done appropriately, let's chat afterwards and let's I want to get to the bottom of the risk that you're talking about.
