Q: Let me ask you a question about the political scene. I heard Barney Frank on radio the other day quite passionately saying that Dodd-Frank had gone too far in creating handicaps for small community banks and that it ought to be changed. And he was asked, do some of your former colleagues agree with you, and his response was, yes, he said, I think the majority of them do, but nothing really has happened in that regard. Do you have a thought about that?

MR. KASHKARI: Yeah. I think it hasn't happened I agree with him that there is a lot of consensus on that point, but I think it's being wrapped up in the big bank issue, is my read of the political situation; that people who do want to relax on small banks are nervous that it's actually going to be relaxed on the biggest banks, and that's why these two issues have gotten conflated. And that's why in our plan that we've put forward, we've said here's what we're going to do to the biggest banks, and once people have real confidence that the biggest banks are safe and sound, that's going to make it easier to then do what I believe both sides of the aisle want to do and relax regulations on community banks. That's why I think they're personally, I think that's why there hasn't been much movement.

Q: Could you comment on the impact of rising rates on federal deficit and what that's going to do to the challenges that Congress has got in trying to solve the budget problems?

MR. KASHKARI: Sure. I mean, I think you spelled it out right there. As rates go up, there are going to be impacts on not just the private economy but the federal deficit as well. To be candid with you, that's not what factors into our interest-rate decisions. Our interest-rate decisions are based purely on what's happening in the U.S. economy what's happening in terms of inflation and the dual mandate, what's happening in terms of the job market, and what's the optimal interest rate for achieving our dual mandate over time. And you know, we think that other policy makers are paying attention to what we're doing, and hopefully it will factor into their thinking.

And let's talk about fiscal more broadly. Depending on what Congress decides to do in terms of taxes, in terms of health care, in terms of trade, that also factors into our own analysis of what the outlook is for the U.S. economy. So these things are definitely interrelated, but the mandate that we've been given is look at the U.S. economy, look at your dual mandate, set interest rates based on that, not based on making it easier or more difficult for Treasury to fund their spending. That's up to them.

Q: The Minneapolis Fed has been regarded for decades for the research that they produce and somewhat cutting edge, particularly related to public stadium funding and early education done by your predecessors. What lessons do you think could be learned given the Twin Cities region is kind of a hallmark in taking an opposite approach to that public stadium funding? What lessons can we learn in getting your initiative marketed and out in front of people in a different way?

MR. KASHKARI: Repeat what you just said about the hallmark?

Q: Well, between, you know, the stadiums in St. Paul and Minneapolis over the last couple of years, the growing trend around the country of (National Football League) stadiums now saying everybody who's got one everybody who wanted one has one, and public funding was some factor involved in most of those.

MR. KASHKARI: So, I mean, that's not an example of success on our part, right? Well there are lessons, though, nonetheless. We may not have won the so, first of all, you know, this idea of the NFL has a great racket, and they restrict the number of teams so that there's overdemand for teams. And then they get the cities to bid against each other, and they extract those rents for the NFL's benefit. I mean, it's a great racket if you're an NFL owner, but the taxpayers pay and so that's a problem. And the only way to solve that, I think, would require federal legislation to prohibit this because each state is operating in its own what it thinks is its own self-interest and but the NFL owners are the ones who really benefit from it. So we failed in delivering that message.

However, the Minneapolis Fed and some of my predecessors a guy named Art Rolnick, who is our research director and a researcher who is currently at our bank, Rob Greenwald have been working on the issue of early childhood education for years, and it's an important lesson to the gentleman's question about, you know, how likely is this to happen in terms of too big to fail. So what I've taken away from them is they pounded this drum on early childhood education and the research behind it, showing that you know why we got into early childhood education? Because the economists at the bank said public money for stadiums is stupid, and someone said OK, smarty pants, well, what should we be investing in if that's stupid? And they said OK, we'll go think about it, and the highest (return on investment) they could find was public taxpayer dollars investing in early childhood education in terms of payback for the taxpayers. That's how they came on that issue.

So the lesson, though, is, they'd been pounding this drum over and over and over again and eventually people paid attention, and eventually the stuff is actually getting implemented. And so while we may not have been successful in convincing people not to spend public money on stadiums, they've been pretty darn successful in getting people to realize early childhood education is a good investment for the taxpayers. And so the lesson for us, as it relates to too big to fail is, don't give up easily, you know. People say, well, it seems like nothing changes; well, nothing changes until everything changes.

You know, I look at I take a political lesson from the country's views on gay marriage. So in California I came here from California. In 2008, not that long ago, the state of California, which views itself as the most progressive state in the world, the state of California voted to ban gay marriage in 2008. But the activists stayed on it, stayed on it, stayed on it, stayed on it, stayed on it now, basically the laws of the land have changed not very long later, and so it seems like nothing changes until people wake up and then everything changes. So I feel like on the issue of too big to fail, we have an obligation to keep speaking up, and then, people may not agree with us, but if we keep speaking up maybe they will agree with us.

WSJ Pro: SEC Chairman Nominee Was Vetted by Executives Close to Trump

By Dave Michaels

WASHINGTON President Donald Trump's pick to lead the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission met with several prominent business executives close to the president's campaign before he was named to lead the top Wall Street overseer.

Jay Clayton disclosed the meetings with billionaire venture capitalist Peter Thiel and GOP fundraiser Rebekah Mercer in a letter to Sen. Sherrod Brown, the top Democrat on the Senate Banking Committee, which plans to vote Tuesday on Mr. Clayton's nomination to be SEC chairman. In response to follow-up questions sent after Mr. Clayton's March 23 confirmation hearing, the nominee wrote that he also met with Darren Blanton, a Dallas-based investor and founder of Colt Ventures. The firm's activities include private-equity investing and proprietary trading, according to its website.

"While I have not specifically looked into it, I believe it is fair to presume that one or more of these individuals may be affiliated with one or more public companies or other companies that are regulated by the SEC," Mr. Clayton wrote to Mr. Brown (D., Ohio).

Mr. Clayton, a partner at law firm Sullivan & Cromwell LLP, could be confirmed this month by the full Senate after he clears the banking committee Tuesday. It's unclear how many Democratic votes he will get. Democratic senators including Mr. Brown have questioned his personal and professional ties to firms that are overseen by the SEC. Mr. Clayton has represented many Wall Street banks during his career as a lawyer, while his wife works as a wealth adviser for Goldman Sachs Group Inc. She has said she would step down from the job if her husband were confirmed.

Mr. Thiel, an investor and entrepreneur, has helped recruit people for jobs in the Trump administration and served on the executive committee leading Mr. Trump's transition to the White House.

Ms. Mercer was a member of the committee and oversaw a super PAC that supported Mr. Trump during his campaign against Hillary Clinton. Her father, Robert Mercer, is the co-chief executive officer of Renaissance Technologies LLC, a quantitative hedge fund that is regulated by the SEC.

In the letter, Mr. Clayton wrote that he met with Mr. Trump, White House Chief of Staff Reince Priebus, White House counselor and chief strategist Steve Bannon, and Martin Silverstein, a corporate lawyer who was ambassador to Uruguay under former President George W. Bush. Mr. Clayton also wrote that he met with activist investor Carl Icahn after his nomination was announced, saying Mr. Icahn sought the meeting after media reports surfaced naming Mr. Clayton as a potential chairman.

Testifying before the banking committee last month, Mr. Clayton called for providing regulatory relief to encourage more initial public offerings. U.S. investors, he said, are among those missing out the most if fast-growing private firms decide not to go public to avoid the regulatory scrutiny that goes along with listing their shares on an exchange.

In his written responses to Mr. Brown's questions, Mr. Clayton further described how he might seek to reduce regulatory requirements. Public firms face a litany of internal controls and compliance requirements intended to bolster the integrity of financial statements "that comparable, well-run private companies may determine are not in the best interest of shareholders," Mr. Clayton wrote. "I believe we should be examining this situation with an eye toward identifying less burdensome means to achieving effective regulation of newly public companies."

Banks Turn to Virtual World to Modernize Physical Commodities Trading

By Stephanie Yang

Banks and traders are experimenting with the technology behind bitcoin in an effort to solve longstanding problems in the trading of physical commodities.

Blockchain, the technology used to record ownership of the cryptocurrency bitcoin, has been making inroads recently in the financial world. Central banks and other institutions are exploring it for payments and data sharing. Major banks including J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Citigroup Inc. have tested the technology in recording financial transactions.

Now commodities players are trying out blockchain to help buy and sell goods and raw materials.

French bank Natixis and the commodity trading firm Trafigura Pte Ltd. unveiled a platform in late March with International Business Machines Corp. to carry out U.S. crude oil transactions electronically using blockchain technology.

A group of participants in the cotton market is also evaluating the use of blockchain in trading and plans to release its results in May. The consortium, which has partnered with IBM to use its blockchain platform, includes major agricultural commodities traders Cargill Inc. and Louis Dreyfus Co.

The technology records each transaction in a ledger, of which every computer has an identical copy. This makes counterfeiting virtually impossible, since every transaction can be checked against the other records, and obviates the need for costly and time-consuming back-office record-keeping by third parties like finance companies.

Firms are turning to blockchain because the exchange of physical goods is plagued by pitfalls that have existed for decades in these notoriously opaque markets.

Trades routinely get bogged down by paper documents that take time to verify and can be lost or forged during ownership changes. This can be costly for consumers and producers, especially when payments or deliveries are delayed or interrupted. It can also cause frustration for those unable to track exactly where their materials come from.

By establishing a decentralized system, traders think they can cut down on time, expenses and opportunities for fraud. Blockchain can create digital codes for, say, each bale of cotton or gold bar, and records all points of sale on an encrypted list that is verified by multiple parties.

"I'm more and more convinced that this technology is going to be disruptive for the industry," said Patrick Arnaud, managing director of trade and commodity finance at ING Groep NV.

ING, Socit Gnrale and commodities trade house Mercuria in February executed the first delivery of crude oil to run entirely on a blockchain platform. The trade tracked the transport of crude oil from Africa to China.

ING found that the use of blockchain, instead of paper certification, reduced its involvement in each transaction to about 25 minutes from an average of three hours. Blockchain also cut costs by about 30%, the bank said.

The banks are in discussions now to conduct a similar trade in liquefied natural gas, according to Mr. Arnaud.

Despite the eagerness of some traders to adopt the technology, blockchain faces a number of hurdles to widespread use in commodities trading.

Regulatory requirements for exchanging goods are different from country to country and may need to change to become compatible for cross-border blockchain trades.

"It's going to brush up against an existing compliance regime as well as regulation or legislation in its own right," said Emma Weston, co-founder of AgriDigital, a commodity management platform that has used blockchain to trade grain.

Another challenge is persuading an industry that has been resistant to change to embrace a new system especially one that has had some miscues. While the underlying protocol for bitcoin has never been hacked, apps and services built on top of it have been plagued with privacy and hacking scandals.

Mallory Alexander International Logistics has run two shipping trials with blockchain this year, but its president remains skeptical about new trading technologies.

Over the past 15 years or more, attempts to modernize data in shipping "always seem to hit a snag," said Neely Mallory. "There are a lot of countries that U.S. agriculture is shipped to that are not ready to embrace it."

Another concern for commodity traders is how much proprietary information would be shared with others if blockchain is used. Those experimenting with the technology say there is still discussion on what data would be limited and for which parties.

"No one would want their information on who's trading what" to be seen on the blockchain, said Mark Pryor, chief executive of commodities trading software provider the Seam, which is leading the cotton consortium.

In metals, the U.K.'s Royal Mint and futures exchange CME Group Inc. plan to launch digital certificates for physical gold this year. The exchange IEX Group Inc. is also experimenting with a gold exchange run on blockchain.

Mr. Pryor said using blockchain could change the mind-set of some hardened commodities traders.

"People are not used to working in an environment where they trust the data, where they trust the systems," he said.

Frankfurt Landlords Welcome Brexit Business

By William Wilkes and Art Patnaude

FRANKFURT The office market in Germany's financial capital is emerging as one of the winners from Brexit less than a year after the U.K.'s historic vote to quit the European Union.

British Prime Minister Theresa May last week began the process by formally notifying the EU of the U.K.'s intention to withdraw. That began a two-year divorce process.

But major financial institutions with offices in London already have begun to plan for difficulties with doing business across the English Channel. For instance, banks in London currently can sell products to clients in mainland Europe. Brexit is likely to change that.

Competition among European cities and countries to lure business from post-Brexit Britain has been heating up, with Paris, Dublin, Milan and even Wroclaw, Poland, making their case heard.

But Frankfurt appears to be in pole position. Around 15 major banks from the U.S., Japan, China and Britain are in talks with German officials about relocating to Frankfurt, according to people familiar with the matter.

Banks could shift up to 900 staff each from London to Frankfurt to ensure they can continue operations when the U.K. quits the EU, as soon as 2019.

Russia's second-largest bank, VTB, in February said it would establish a new headquarters in Frankfurt, which it said will likely gain in importance after Brexit. Deutsche Bank AG and Goldman Sachs Group Inc. said they could relocate staff to Frankfurt.

What's more, other tenants in Frankfurt's market have been getting more aggressive in leasing space partly with an eye toward incoming competition from across the English Channel. Last year, businesses leased 525,000 square meters (5.6 million square feet) in the city, the most since 2007 and 34% more than in 2015, according to JLL.

Prime office rents at the end of the fourth quarter were  37 per square meter a month, up 4.2% one year earlier, JLL said. Frankfurt has a limited supply of very large offices, so newcomers will likely drive prices higher.

By contrast, the London market has been softening. Office tenants there leased 10.1 million square feet in 2016, down from more than 11.7 million in 2015, according to JLL.

London landlords have been increasing their offers of free rent and other incentives to lure tenants. Some also have begun to cut rents, a trend that would be exacerbated if firms start leaving en masse.

"Risk is heightened," said Alex Jeffrey, chief executive at U.K. firm M&G Real Estate.

But big financial services firms still want a London presence. German lender Deutsche Bank even as it talks about moving jobs home to Frankfurt is also negotiating to lease office space in a new London development from Land Securities PLC, one of the U.K.'s biggest landlords.

London also still has advantages over Frankfurt. While leasing trends are less favorable, London's vacancy rate remains lower: 4.2% compared with 9.1% for Frankfurt, JLL says.

Meanwhile, it is easier to build in Frankfurt than in London partly, because there is more available land and other redevelopment opportunities, analysts say. More Frankfurt projects are moving off the drawing board, raising the likelihood of more competition.

New Frankfurt projects that are under way include Tishman Speyer's Omiturm, a 45-story tower that will include 472,000 square feet of office space and 88,000 square feet of rental space. It is scheduled to be completed by the beginning of 2019.

No office leases have been announced yet, but negotiations are intensifying with several financial services firms, according to Florian Reiff, a Tishman Speyer senior managing director. "You can see now the market is firming up," he said. "The Brexit decision is becoming more concrete," he said.

PGIM Real Estate, a unit of Prudential Financial Inc., also sees Brexit-related demand picking up for its Junghof Plaza project in Frankfurt. A venture led by PGIM acquired the 14-year old office building late last year and is converting it into a mixed use project with office, retail and a hotel.

"Investment banks, especially from the U.S., are in the market" even though they might not move for a few years, said Sebastian Ferrante, PGIM's head of Germany. "Every move by a financial institution with all the logistics that involves is nothing to be done over a six-month period."

 Peter Grant contributed to this article.

Inspector General Says Fed Could Improve Monitoring of Big Banks

By Katy Burne

The Federal Reserve could be more consistent in how it carries out the ongoing monitoring of big banks, the central bank's Office of Inspector General concluded in a report, also pointing to resource constraints facing examiners.

In their report released Monday, auditors at Inspector General's office said they had assessed the effectiveness of the Fed's continuous monitoring for large and complex banks, and suggested a series of improvements to its training practices and record keeping.

The Fed conducts this monitoring from a unit called the Large Institution Supervision Coordinating Committee, or LISCC. The group, composed of officers from both the Fed's board and its individual reserve banks, was formed in 2010 and now is the hub for Fed bank supervisory decision-making.

Ongoing monitoring is less formal than a targeted examination of a bank, and it involves supervision of an institution's day-to-day operations to keep on top of emerging and known risks. During 2015, examiners for big banks spent 44% of their time on routine continuous monitoring and 12% on enhanced continuous monitoring, the report found.

But the Inspector General said its assessment showed the LISCC's structure could be more effective, and noted inconsistencies around how bank examiners document their work and retrieve reports from databases around the Fed system. "Although the Board and the Reserve Banks have multiple documents that address the expectations for certain aspects of continuous monitoring, the Board hasn't issued guidance that harmonizes these expectations across its supervisory portfolios and the Reserve Banks," the OIG wrote in a summary of its report.

Some examiners indicated that their resources felt stretched, according to the OIG.

During interviews, auditors heard from examiners that they don't always have sufficient time to review the information gathered, and that the teams may not have analyzed all the information before it is uploaded.

Other examiners said they were reluctant to cancel document requests and meetings for fear of missing vital information, but the OIG said given the healthier state of banks in the wake of the financial crisis, it makes sense to rationalize these requests.

This could also help teams that complained about not being able to find specific documents and monitoring results across the many information storage facilities and repositories around the Fed.

"Team members indicated that it is difficult to find information that they are looking for," the OIG said in its report.

The OIG suggested more enhanced training and supervisory guidance that would help to "foster more consistent execution" across the Fed system and maximize its effectiveness.

The work was a follow up to the Inspector General's earlier field studies showing how opportunities existed to help encourage Fed bank supervisors to share divergent views about examinations more openly.

In an earlier 2014 evaluation of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York's continuous bank monitoring, the Inspector General recommended  the bank lay out more detailed expectations for documenting the work of supervisors, and how those materials could be accessed. Monday's report broadened the scope to include a review of the Cleveland Fed, the New York Fed, the Richmond Fed and the San Francisco Fed, which concluded last November.

A senior officer at the Fed board wrote in a March 21 letter to the Inspector General that he agreed with the recommendations and would follow up.

Michael Gibson, director of supervision and regulation at the Fed Board of Governors, said in the letter he recognized "that there are opportunities to clarify our expectations for continuous monitoring through additional guidance and training" for both the LISCC teams and supervisors of large foreign banks.

Mr. Gibson said the Fed had introduced a new monitoring and analysis program for the LISCC, whose organizers are responsible for ensuring consistency across supervisory monitoring. A steering group is also working on integrating the  program, which Mr. Gibson said should be approved by May 31, including guidance about developing enhanced training.

Digital Clue Links North Korea to Theft at New York Fed, Security Firm Says

By Robert McMillan

A newly discovered digital clue links the hacking group blamed for a multimillion-dollar cyberattack on Bangladesh's central bank to a computer in North Korea, according to the Russian cybersecurity company Kaspersky Lab ZAO.

Kaspersky announced Monday at its security conference on the Caribbean island of St. Maarten that its researchers had obtained digital records showing a European server used by the group to launch its attacks exchanged data in January with a computer that had an internet address belonging to North Korea's state-owned internet service provider.

Security experts have long suspected the hacking group, known as Lazarus, was backed by North Korea, but that assessment hinged on a 2014 Federal Bureau of Investigation claim that one Lazarus hack the compromise of Sony Pictures Entertainment in 2014 was conducted by North Korea.

Although The Wall Street Journal reported last month that the FBI is pursuing a criminal case linking North Korea to Lazarus's activity, the agency hasn't provided any public evidence to substantiate the alleged North Korean link.

Multiple calls to the North Korean mission to the United Nations went unanswered.

The newly discovered evidence came from a rare technical error by the Lazarus hackers, according to Kapersky.

The hackers failed to remove computer-log files on a server used by the group, and these digital records show the server briefly connecting to another computer in North Korea, said Vitaly Kamluk, a researcher with Kaspersky.

North Korea has "very little presence on the internet, and the chances that this is just a random connection are extremely small," Mr. Kamluk said. "It does place North Korea into this equation."

As with most developments in the shadowy world of computer intrusions, this latest piece of evidence isn't conclusive. It is technically possible, for example, for someone to hack the North Korean computer and connect it to the Lazarus server to throw off investigators, Mr. Kamluk said.

Although the attacks against Sony and Bangladesh Bank occurred in 2014 and 2016, respectively, the Lazarus group has been active since at least 2009 and recently has targeted banks in Europe and the U.S., Mr. Kamluk said. Lazarus is a name given to a group of the hackers by security researchers. No member of the group has been publicly identified.

Often, as with the Bangladesh central-bank hack, the Lazarus hackers quietly break into computer networks and lurk undetected for months before attacking, the security experts said. The Lazarus group is suspected of stealing $81 million from Bangladesh Bank's account at the Federal Reserve Bank of New York last year after initiating fraudulent orders asking for nearly $1 billion.

The use of digital currency may have been one of the reasons that Lazarus's server logs weren't removed, Mr. Kamluk said. The European server containing log files was configured to perform a series of calculations that generated Monero, a digital currency similar to Bitcoin. But this computationally intensive work seems to have overwhelmed the machine, Mr. Kamluk said.

"They overconsumed the resources of the server and the server just crashed," he said.

Bank of America Using FATF Reports to Gauge Risk

By Samuel Rubenfeld

Hollywood, Fla. Bank of America Corp. is using a recent assessment of the money laundering risk in the U.S. economy to help determine risk among its customers, according to a bank official.

William Fox, global head of financial crimes compliance at Bank of America, said during a panel discussion at an anti-money laundering conference that he had briefed senior management about the latest evaluation of the U.S. from the Financial Action Task Force, an international standards-setting body.

"If you haven't briefed senior management [about the report], you should think about it," he told the audience at the conference being hosted by the Association of Certified Anti-Money Laundering Specialists in Hollywood, Fla.

Mr. Fox, who previously was director of the Financial Crimes Enforcement Network at the U.S. Treasury Department the leading U.S. agency fighting money laundering said his bank is reviewing its client base to assess whether its non-financial and other high-risk customers pose any money-laundering concern.

"We are looking at that to determine whether we need to pay attention to those sectors and clients. The risk is there," he said.

The latest FATF evaluation of the U.S. was positive overall, but it identified  serious gaps" in U.S. oversight of beneficial owners, as well as of non-financial organizations such as non-profits, lawyers or accountants.

Rick McDonell, executive director of ACAMS and the former executive secretary of the FATF, said that despite the shortcomings, the U.S. is viewed as a leader in the anti-money laundering area, and other countries learn from its example.

"When the U.S. does well &that is a plus, and a positive incentive for others," he said.

Sarah Runge, director of the U.S. Treasury Department's Office of Strategic Policy focusing on terrorist financing and financial crimes, said the U.S. government welcomed the report, noting that the U.S. received the highest marks in four categories studied by the FATF.

"This report is a reflection of the success of what we put together" on anti-money laundering and counter-terrorist financing since the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks, she said. SInce that time, she said, the U.S. Treasury made the financial sector "a key partner" in the national-security framework.

"We were not surprised about the findings," she said.

Cash Cow Credit Cards May Get Hit by U.K. Bank Regulators

By Paul J. Davies

British regulators may take a chunk out of the credit card industry's revenues, just as the debt cycle looks ripe for a rise in defaults.

For Barclays, Royal Bank of Scotland and Lloyds Banking Group which has just acquired a big U.K. credit card book from Bank of AmericaMerrill Lynch this will likely prove painful.

The proposed changes came about because the U.K.'s Financial Conduct Authority, whose role includes safeguarding consumers' interests, is concerned about the high level of debt held on credit cards for 18 months or longer.

More than three million people in the U.K. have card debt that costs them more each month in interest and charges than they repay. Each customer pays around 2.50 ($3.14) for 1 of debt repaid, the FCA says.

The FCA wants banks to intervene earlier, encourage people to pay down this debt quickly, or stop charging them interest and come up with a repayment plan. The body says that in the first few years, this could save consumers between 310 million and 1.3 billion.

That would be a hit to the industry's revenues. Card balances in 2016 averaged 62 billion, of which 57% was interest bearing, according to data from the British Bankers' Association. At an annual interest rate of 20%, in line with that of many credit cards, that would put annual industry interest income at about 7 billion a little more than five times the top-end of estimated consumer savings.

Credit card income is significant for U.K. banks. Cards bring in nearly 30% of Barclays's U.K. revenues and nearly 10% of group revenues. At RBS, card income represents about 5% of group revenues. Lloyds doesn't report card income separately.

Consumers have relied more heavily on cards to fund spending following 2016's Brexit vote, which has kept the economy humming. However, fast growth in consumer lending has led the Bank of England to review credit standards and to focus on consumer risks in 2017's banking stress test. Add in the FCA's measures and banks look likely to curtail consumer lending, which could be a drag on the economy.

Credit card profits have been helped in recent years by very low default rates, but rising inflation is now expected to put pressure on real incomes in the U.K., while interest rates are likely to start rising.

Losing these revenues as the credit cycle turns will likely give the card industry a bloody nose.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

Fed's Tarullo: Details Tuesday on  Room for Change' in Dodd-Frank

Departing Federal Reserve governor Daniel Tarullo gave an interview to National Public Radio Monday in one of his final days in office. The central bank's regulatory point person didn't say much that he hasn't said before, emphasizing the improvements in the safety of the financial system since 2008 and outlining how the U.S. economy is strong, but not as strong as officials would hope. He did give a preview of his farewell speech set for Tuesday evening at Princeton University, saying he would talk about where there is "room for change in Dodd Frank" but defend high capital requirements for big banks. (Ryan.Tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

AIG Chairman Says Breakup Would Be  Value-Destructive'

AIG Nonexecutive Chairman Douglas Steenland tells shareholders in annual letter that the board has "great confidence" in the insurer's management team "to continue executing against the two-year Strategic Plan" announced in January 2016. He emphasizes that the board isn't interested in splitting AIG apart--as called for in 2015 by investors Carl Icahn and John Paulson--because analysis shows it "to be value-destructive." Board "is actively engaged in the process of identifying the right individual" to succeed CEO Peter Hancock, who is stepping down upon naming of a new CEO. (leslie.scism@wsj.com)

Wells Fargo Ordered to Reinstate Whistleblower

The Department of Labor's OSHA division has ordered Wells Fargo (WFC) to reinstate a former bank manager who lost his job after reporting "suspected fraudulent behavior to superiors and a bank ethics hotline," according to DOL. The manager had received positive performance reviews but was terminated after reporting separate incidents of suspected fraud. He was initially told he had 90 days to find a new position at WFC and when unsuccessful, he was fired. OSHA concluded that whistleblowing contributed to his ouster and ordered WFC to compensate him about $5.4M including damages and lawyers fees. A WFC spokesman says said the bank disagrees with the findings, adding: "To date there has been no hearing on the merits in this case, and we will be requesting a full hearing of the matter." (emily.glazer@wsj.com; @EmilyGlazerWSJ)

Credit Suisse Stocks Extend Losses

Credit Suisse shares extend losses after the bank's disclosure Friday that it had been contacted by Dutch, French and UK authorities over client tax issues. This prompted fears of more legal headaches for the Swiss lender. Shares fell about 1.2% Friday and were down another 1.7% to 14.65 Swiss francs at 0841 GMT Monday. (brian.blackstone@wsj.com)

Finnish Covered Bonds to Get Regulatory Boost

The Finnish Financial Supervisory Authority's decision to set the minimum risk weight on residential mortgages at 15% is positive for banks' credit and their covered bonds, Moody's says. Finnish lenders will now need to increase the capital they hold against mortgages. This will come in handy if and when losses start kicking in. Finnish household debt has risen to 125% of disposable income, the Bank of Finland said earlier this year. Covered bonds are debt backed by collateral, typically mortgages. (tasos.vossos@wsj.com, @tasosvos)

Postcrisis Bank Closures Hit Urban Areas the Hardest

Bank-branch closures since the financial crisis have disproportionately affected Americans who live in urban areas, as well as low-income households and minorities, "http://online.wsj.com/public/resources/documents/NCRCResearch04-03-2017.pdf"by the National Community Reinvestment Coalition, a consumer-protection group. The NCRC found that 6,008 branches, or 6% of the total, were closed between 2008 and 2016. Of the closures, 82% were in urban areas and the remainder in rural areas. Several metro areas lost 15% to 25% of their branches, with the most acute losses in Baltimore, Chicago, Philadelphia, Las Vegas and Detroit. The NCRC also found service gaps in rural areas, resulting in "banking deserts," defined as areas where there are no banks within 10 miles of populated areas. These deserts disproportionately affected minorities, according to the paper.

Could Bank Mergers Return Soon?

Since the financial crisis, no bank has "dared challenge regulators" that discouraged financial firms from getting bigger through merger deals but that could start to change under the Trump administration, "https://www.nytimes.com/2017/03/31/business/dealbook/why-the-urge-to-merge-could-return-to-wall-street.html?ref=dealbook&_r=0". At the outset of the crisis, the Federal Reserve put the banking industry "on a path toward being an oligopoly," as it encouraged healthy banks to buy failing ones. But "then the Fed shut off the spigot," he writes. A loosening of Fed restrictions on mergers could lead to a wave of commercial bank mergers, though Mr. Cohan believes that won't happen in the immediate future. Still, "one thing we know for sure is that the times are changing in Washington, and fast," he writes.

Britain's Proposed Debt Relief Rules Destined for Failure

Rules proposed by Britain's Financial Conduct Authority that would compel credit card issuers to "bail out" overindebted consumers are destined to be unsuccessful, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-u-k-boom-that-debt-built-1491262223". "The FCA wants card issuers to prod such debtors to repay faster, or ultimately give them a break on their repayments," the board writes. But that would be akin to treating a hangover "before the patient has stopped drinking," they write, and such rules are not likely to solve Britain's personal and credit-card debt issues.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/can-housing-bubbles-be-stopped-australias-tiny-apartments-say-no-1491210939", authorities are learning a hard lesson in their efforts to curb the foreign money flooding their property markets: deterrents quickly lose their punch.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinese-firms-shy-away-from-european-debt-1491238263" issued in European currencies lag far behind their dollar counterparts, a trend expected to continue as the U.K.'s exit from the European Union threatens to shake financial markets on the continent.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/student-debt-may-prevent-some-americans-from-buying-homes-1491230359" on student loans at a "stubbornly high" rate, and a small share of borrowers are unable to buy homes due to high levels of student debt, the head of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York said.

"http://blogs.wsj.com/cio/2017/04/03/bny-mellon-ai-chatbot-to-help-it-automate-data-center-tasks/" is building a voice-controlled artificial intelligence platform that can help the firm's IT staff manage enterprise storage, at a time when analysts predict information technology infrastructure will become more automated.

Rubin's Take: Populists, Globalists Clash on Financial Integration

Dodd-Frank Overhaul Vote Unlikely Before Summer, Lawmaker Says

Fed's Kashkari on Too Big to Fail, Dodd-Frank and More

SEC Chairman Nominee Was Vetted by Executives Close to Trump

Frankfurt Landlords Welcome Brexit Business

Populists, Globalists Clash on Financial Integration

Resurgent nationalism in the U.S. and Europe could have lasting consequences for global financial regulatory standards, and some argue the changes could hurt economic growth and financial stability.

The twin geopolitical earthquakes of Brexit and the election of Donald Trump as U.S. president pose acute challenges to regulatory bodies such as the European Commission or the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision and their ability to impose or recommend (with any significant amount of moral suasion) uniform standards for the global financial system.

With Brexit in particular, the financial integration of the European Union is at stake, given the likely loss of "passporting" privileges for British banks, which have long constituted the hub of financial services in Europe. Passporting allows London-based lenders and insurers to operate anywhere in the EU. Large multinational banks with operations in London are "https://www.wsj.com/articles/j-p-morgan-scours-europe-for-best-post-brexit-home-1490779804"for new homes within EU member countries.

"You've just turned your back on 40 years of financial integration," said Institute of International Finance President Tim Adams in an interview with The Wall Street Journal, referring to Brexit. A subsequent "tit-for-tat effort to wall off capital" would introduce major inefficiencies into global capital markets and encourage financial institutions to seek favors from national regulators, rather than operating under a set of uniform international standards, he said.

Mr. Adams highlighted the impact that devolution in financial integration could have on economic activity, using the example of an international supply chain. In an integrated system, a single bank could handle the financing for all the transactions involved in a particular deal, regardless of where they occurred. But in a less-integrated system, different banks might have to finance individual parts of the deal creating inefficiencies and higher costs for the parties involved as well as consumers.

Brexit, which has been championed by Mr. Trump as an example of nationalist interests winning out over unaccountable globalists, has also emboldened Republican skeptics of international regulatory bodies.

Shortly after Mr. Trump's inauguration, Rep. Patrick McHenry (R., N.C.), the vice chairman of the House Financial Services Committee, sent a letter to Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen calling negotiations with international standards setters like the Basel Committee "unacceptable."

In a committee hearing shortly after that, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/gop-lawmakers-tell-yellen-to-pause-new-fed-rules-1487201336"on suggestions the Fed should stop engaging in conversations with international regulators and noted that rules are only binding if U.S. regulators choose to implement them.

Nonetheless, some international regulators recognize the need to be more transparent and accountable if they are to maintain the trust of national policy makers, particularly those skeptical of their mission.

"https://www.bis.org/speeches/sp170228.pdf"about financial integration in Geneva in February, Bank for International Settlements General Manager Jaime Caruana argued for making the "positive case for financial globalization," which he said has been responsible for boosting living standards.

Financial globalization hasn't yet reached its peak potential, Mr. Caruana said, cautioning that potential "can be realized only if there is far-reaching cooperation in its management."

Both the U.K. and the Trump administration seem to be moving away from the model of international cooperation on regulatory standards and financial integration. It remains to be seen whether the "globalists'" can regain their footing in the debate and restore the influence of international financial organizations.

Dodd-Frank Overhaul Vote Unlikely Before Summer, Lawmaker Says

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/dodd-frank-overhaul-vote-unlikely-before-summer-lawmaker-says-1491245784?tesla=y" summer at the earliest on changes to the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul law, a senior Republican said Monday, demonstrating how the path for regulatory relief remains in flux as lawmakers grapple with health-care policy, a tax overhaul and other issues.

Banks Turn to Virtual World to Modernize Physical Commodities Trading

Blockchain, the technology used to record ownership of the cryptocurrency bitcoin, has been making inroads recently in the financial world. But Frankfurt appears to be in pole position. Around 15 major banks from the U.S., Japan, China and Britain are in talks with German officials about relocating to Frankfurt, according to people familiar with the matter.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/inspector-general-says-fed-could-improve-monitoring-of-big-banks-1491235482"do a better job harmonizing and clarifying the role of bank supervisors tasked with the ongoing monitoring of financial institutions, the Fed board's Office of Inspector General said.

In a report released Monday, the Inspector General's office said it had assessed the effectiveness of the central bank's continuous monitoring for large and complex banks, and suggested a series of improvements to its training practices and record keeping.

Digital Clue Links North Korea to Theft at New York Fed, Security Firm Says

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/north-korean-link-found-to-theft-at-new-york-fed-security-firm-says-1491242401" links the hacking group blamed for a multimillion-dollar cyberattack on Bangladesh's central bank to a computer in North Korea, according to the Russian cybersecurity company Kaspersky Lab ZAO.

Kaspersky announced Monday at its security conference on the Caribbean island of St. Maarten that its researchers had obtained digital records showing a European server used by the group to launch its attacks exchanged data in January with a computer that had an internet address belonging to North Korea's state-owned internet service provider.

Bank of America Using FATF Reports to Gauge Risk

"http://blogs.wsj.com/riskandcompliance/2017/04/03/bank-of-america-using-fatf-reports-to-gauge-risk/" is using a recent assessment of the money laundering risk in the U.S. economy to help determine risk among its customers, according to a bank official.

William Fox, global head of financial crimes compliance at Bank of America, said during a panel discussion at an anti-money-laundering conference that he had briefed senior management about the latest evaluation of the U.S. from the Financial Action Task Force, an international standards-setting body. "If you haven't briefed senior management [about the report], you should think about it," he told the audience at the conference in Hollywood, Fla.

Postcrisis Bank Closures Hit Urban Areas the Hardest

Bank-branch closures since the financial crisis have disproportionately affected Americans who live in urban areas, as well as low-income households and minorities, "http://online.wsj.com/public/resources/documents/NCRCResearch04-03-2017.pdf"by the National Community Reinvestment Coalition, a consumer-protection group. The NCRC found that 6,008 branches, or 6% of the total, were closed between 2008 and 2016. Of the closures, 82% were in urban areas and the remainder in rural areas. Several metro areas lost 15% to 25% of their branches, with the most acute losses in Baltimore, Chicago, Philadelphia, Las Vegas and Detroit. The NCRC also found service gaps in rural areas, resulting in "banking deserts," defined as areas where there are no banks within 10 miles of populated areas. These deserts disproportionately affected minorities, according to the paper.

Could Bank Mergers Return Soon?

Since the financial crisis, no bank has "dared challenge regulators" that discouraged financial firms from getting bigger through merger deals but that could start to change under the Trump administration, "https://www.nytimes.com/2017/03/31/business/dealbook/why-the-urge-to-merge-could-return-to-wall-street.html?ref=dealbook&_r=0". At the outset of the crisis, the Federal Reserve put the banking industry "on a path toward being an oligopoly," as it encouraged healthy banks to buy failing ones. But "then the Fed shut off the spigot," he writes. A loosening of Fed restrictions on mergers could lead to a wave of commercial bank mergers, though Mr. Cohan believes that won't happen in the immediate future. Still, "one thing we know for sure is that the times are changing in Washington, and fast," he writes.

Britain's Proposed Debt Relief Rules Destined for Failure

Rules proposed by Britain's Financial Conduct Authority that would compel credit card issuers to "bail out" overindebted consumers are destined to be unsuccessful, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-u-k-boom-that-debt-built-1491262223". "The FCA wants card issuers to prod such debtors to repay faster, or ultimately give them a break on their repayments," the board writes. But that would be akin to treating a hangover "before the patient has stopped drinking," they write, and such rules are not likely to solve Britain's personal and credit-card debt issues.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/can-housing-bubbles-be-stopped-australias-tiny-apartments-say-no-1491210939", authorities are learning a hard lesson in their efforts to curb the foreign money flooding their property markets: deterrents quickly lose their punch.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinese-firms-shy-away-from-european-debt-1491238263" issued in European currencies lag far behind their dollar counterparts, a trend expected to continue as the U.K.'s exit from the European Union threatens to shake financial markets on the continent.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/student-debt-may-prevent-some-americans-from-buying-homes-1491230359" on student loans at a "stubbornly high" rate, and a small share of borrowers are unable to buy homes due to high levels of student debt, the head of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York said.

"http://blogs.wsj.com/cio/2017/04/03/bny-mellon-ai-chatbot-to-help-it-automate-data-center-tasks/" is building a voice-controlled artificial intelligence platform that can help the firm's IT staff manage enterprise storage, at a time when analysts predict information technology infrastructure will become more automated.

Pro Private Markets
Fear of Automation's Dark Side Leads to Calls for Action; Some investors back policy or private-industry moves to head off a dystopian future

By Tomio Geron 
948 words
3 April 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Matt Ocko is a venture capitalist placing big bets on the latest in artificial intelligence and big data.

But he also expects that leaps forward in related automation technology will put many people out of work, and he's one of several Silicon Valley investors who think it's unfair to expect society to adapt without offering help to people who are at risk from rapid change.

"You can't simply command a welder who just lost his job and has a family of four to become an AI TensorFlow expert," said Mr. Ocko, managing partner at Data Collective. "If you lose your job, you don't have the capital to invest in retraining. You can't send yourself to MIT while your kids are starving."

Mr. Ocko of Data Collective says that people thrown out of work by automation could receive a tax credit to allow them to own robots or other types of devices that displaced them.

Several venture investors support public-policy initiatives or moves by private industry to head off the threat of a dystopian future in which human labor is devalued or made superfluous.

Some say the technology industry itself must take on the issue.

Among the ideas circulating are giving displaced workers tax credits to own robots or other technology that replaces them. Others have proposed new kinds of insurance or expanding the social safety net, along with expanding job-skills training for a world staffed by robots.

Doing nothing could be an ugly scenario, according to some forecasts. A PricewaterhouseCoopers report predicted that automation will place 38% of the U.S. labor force at high risk in the next 15 years. U.S. venture capitalists more than doubled investment dollars poured into artificial intelligence to $1.72 billion last year, according to Dow Jones VentureSource.

But many automation-focused founders and investors haven't acknowledged the societal risks, let alone the need for a policy response to address the issue.

Not surprisingly, some of the most active venture backers of automation and AI tend to be more optimistic about the technology's effects on society.

Vishal Harpalani, founder of a new firm called Automation VC, says his companies make lives better by making services drastically cheaper. He has backed startups automating dermatology, security, property management and medical diagnostics.

The firm's tagline? "Investing in companies that automate human labor."

One of the startups, YoDerm Inc., makes online dermatology more affordable.

"You're talking an almost 10x decrease in price for services like that," Mr. Harpalani said. "That's happening in every single service: legal, financial, security. Prices are going to drop so substantially that people who couldn't see a dermatologist will have one that's software-enabled."

Mr. Harpalani gives voice to an outlook that many VCs share but don't often openly trumpet in Silicon Valley: automation, whatever its costs, makes good business sense.

"Efficiencies in the system get passed on to consumers," said Daniel Gross, who heads Y Combinator's AI team. "At the end of the day I'd rather have a future with free housing or free basics for everyone in the world instead of hanging onto a few (traditional) jobs."

Emergence Capital's Gordon Ritter says automation and AI help workers to do their jobs better. He cites his portfolio company Chorus, which improves the efficiency of salespeople.

But many future-minded investors have called for expanding the social safety net. A popular idea is universal basic income, which would give all citizens a cash stipend. Among other things, UBI is sometimes cast as a last resort or safety net if automation eliminates a vast number of human jobs.

Research into UBI has gained support from venture capitalists such as Roy Bahat of Bloomberg Beta, Hemant Taneja of General Catalyst and Albert Wenger of Union Square Ventures. Y Combinator has also funded a research project on the idea.

Mr. Ocko believes UBI may not give people enough meaning in their lives and could lead to "something that looks like Clockwork Orange."

Others in tech such as Bill Gates have weighed in, saying that companies using automation or robots should be "https://qz.com/911968/bill-gates-the-robot-that-takes-your-job-should-pay-taxes/".

But just retraining people with digital skills and using UBI as a backup isn't enough, says Tim Hwang, an automation expert and a partner at legal technology firm Robot Robot & Hwang LLP. Mr. Hwang suggests automation insurance that would pay those who are automated out of a job. Private industry could contribute, avoiding the need for a large government program like basic income, he says.

His other ideas: community college curricula should adjust every 6 to 12 months based on economic changes; government programs focus on new growth jobs categories; and tax credits to foster growth in industries that are harder to automate.

There have been few proposals by politicians to address automation. Rep. Doris Matsui has proposed funding to retrain displaced automated workers into science and technology jobs and to forgive up to $100,000 in student loans for recent grads to train those displaced workers.

Meanwhile, looking into the distant future isn't the only way to address this issue.

"My big message is: if you care, look at the present," said Mr. Bahat, whose firm, like many others, has invested in artificial intelligence startups. "There are plenty of forms of work society needs: elder care, teachers. You don't need to look to the future. Just solve that now."

Pro Private Markets
KPCB's Mike Abbott: Enterprise IoT is All About Data; Digital technology investor looks for startups that can help enterprises harness data for profit

By Patience Haggin 
534 words
3 April 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

For Mike Abbott, the enterprise Internet of Things is here and data is at the core. His firm Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers has bets on companies bringing connected devices to businesses from Relayr, a startup making sensors to monitor industrial equipment, to drone services provider Airware, to his most recently announced deal, Ripcord, a startup making robots that digitize paper records.

WSJ: Why is Kleiner Perkins getting deeper into the enterprise Internet-of-Things right now?

I look at it in the following way: it ultimately is around data. Airware is looking at imagery data. In the case of Ripcord, we're talking about analog and digital, including how to take unstructured and make it more like semi-structured kind of information. It's because most companies are realizing that they're either a data company and they need the structure and platform to offer insights from the data they're collecting, or they see that in the future they need to augment that part of the business. Look at the prediction that we're hitting over 40 zettabytes (of data) by 2020. If the insights or the ROI of that data is not delivering, then the value of that data to the enterprise is near zero.

That's a particular interest of mine: what are the areas that we should be leveraging within deep learning, within these advances in these neural nets to solve real problems within the enterprise that either can save the company money or provide some advantage for them within their business?

These advances in computer vision have been key to some of the latest applications, and I think will be key to some of the things that are on the horizon. With some of the advances in natural language processing, how can we do a more efficient job with customer support than maybe certain companies today are doing today that are just human-driven?

You've been credited with solving what some have called "Twitter's greatest problem." As a former vice president of engineering at the company, you slew the once-frequent "fail whale." What is Twitter's greatest problem right now, and how would you solve it?

I've been out of the company for quite some time, so it's hard to say exactly what's going on internally. What it feels like from the outside to me is there's not the conviction to make big, bold product decisions. And I think what concerns me to a degree as someone who led the team that designed and built WebOS at Palm, I know that public markets are not always that patient with product changes. I think those two things make an inherent challenge for the business. Having Jack (Dorsey) as the CEO is the right thing in that there's a founder at the helm who has that ability to make really radical product decisions. I'm hopeful that those are still to come. Time will tell.

Subprime lending: elevator pitch

368 words
2 April 2017
16:20
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Peer-to-peer lending is so last year. Old-fashioned banking is the new frontier. At least, that is what subprime lender Elevate is pitching as the Texan companypreparesfor a second attempt at a flotation that makes more sense as an exit than an entrance.

Like outfits such as Lending Club, Elevate relies heavily on technology for credit analytics and to keep costs down. But, in contrast to its sales pitch two years ago, it stresses that it is not a peer-to-peer lender. Instead, Elevate retains loans on its own balance sheet rather than selling them to third parties.

Elevate s lending is largely unsecured. The need for its product is not surprising; a recent "https://www.federalreserve.gov/2015-report-economic-well-being-us-households-201605.pdf" by the Federal Reserve estimated that almost half of all US adults did not have enough savings to cover a $400 emergency expense. The same proportion said they were looking to take on more debt. The climate is supportive for alternative financiers, too. Risk-averse banks have stepped back from lending to less creditworthy borrowers, while financial stocks have risen sharply amid expectations of higher interest rates and abonfire of regulations.

The company increased its revenue by a third to $580m in 2016. The effective annual interest rate it charges on its loan portfolio is 146 per cent. Despite high rates, the company has been losing money   for obvious reasons. In each of the past two years, it had to provision over half of its revenues for loan losses.

It also has expensive debt, and wants to use the cash injection from the initial public offering to retire some of it. Such savings might have moved profits to the black had the Brexit vote not also caused foreign exchange losses on the UK operations.

The company warns it may not be able to  achieve consistent profitability  in the future. Limiting the amount of debt funding is prudent, although   for growth   its clients must not do the same. The IPO provides two venture funds that own half the shares with a route towards an exit. Smart investors will leave others to help them along it.
